The Red Page
MORE SONNETS TO BEAUTY.
Go, spend your penny, Beauty, when you
ivill,
In the grave's darkness let the stamp be
lost.
The water still will bubble from the hill,
And April quick the meadows tvith her ghost;
Over the grass the daffodils will shiver,
The primroses with their pale beauty abound,
The blackbird, be a lover and make quiver
With his glad singing the great soul of the
ground;
So if the body rot, it will not matter;
Up in the earth the great game will go on,
The coming of Spring and the running of the
water,
And the young things glad of the womb’s
darkness gone;
And the joy we felt ivill be a part of the
glory
In the lover’s kiss that makes the old couple’s
story ....
What am I, Life? A thing of watery salt
Held in cohesion by unresting cells,
Which icork they knoiv not why, which never
halt,
Myself unwitting where their Master dwells.
I do not bid them, yet they toil, they spin;
A world which uses me as I use them,
Nor do I knoiv which end or which begin
Nor which to praise, which pamper, which
condemn.
So, like a marvel in a marvel set,
I answer to the vast, as wave by wave
The sea or air goes over, dry or wet,
Or the full moon comes swimming from her
cave,
Or the great sun comes north, this myriad I
Tingles, not knowing how, yet wondering
why.
What is this atom which contains the whole,
This miracle which needs adjuncts so strange,
This, which imagined God and is the soul,
The steady star persisting amid change?
What waste, that smallness of such power
should need
Such clumsy tools so easy to destroy,
Such wasteful servants difficult to feed,
Such indirect dark avenues to joy.
Why, if its business is not mainly earth,
Should it demand such heavy chains to sense f
A heavenly thing demands a sivifter birth,
A quicker hand to act intelligence.
An earthly thing were better like the rose
At peace with clay from which its beauty
grows
Let that which is to come be as it may,
Darkness, extinction, justice, life intense,
The flies are happy in the summer day,
Flies will be happy many summers hence »
Time with his antique breeds that built the
Sphinx
Time tcith her men to come whose wings will
tower,
Poured and ibill pour, not as the wise man
thinks.
But with blind force, to each his little hour.-
And when the hour has struck, comes death
or change,
Which, whether good or ill, we cannot tell,
But the blind planet will wander through her
range
Bearing men like us who will serve as well.
The sun will rise, the winds that ever move
Will blow our dust that once were men in
love.
From “Good Friday and Other Poems,”
by John Masefield (Macmillan, New York;
$1.25).
Mr. Wells Sees It Half-way
Through
Once more an Englishman looks at the
world —a surprising world, utterly unfore-
seen. Wells calls his latest and greatest
novel Mr. Britling Sees It Through (Cassell,
per Sydney Book Club) ; but though Brit-
ling comes out of his sorrows into the clear
light of sympathy, his trials are only begin-
ning. There is a whole world of fresh suffer-
ing and fresh sacrifices opening up before
Mr. Britling and all the other Britlings of
this Empire. Mr. Britling is not likely to
survive those sufferings; but Wells may. And
before peace happens there is plenty of time
for Wells to write several more books upon
the reaction of the English soul to the war ;
but it is doubtful whether lie can write such
a remarkable book as this.
Briefly, it is merely the record of the effect
of the sledgehammer blows of this war upon
one sensitive, intelligent and typical British
mind. As a piece of psychology this is the
most incisive tract that Wells has yet
written; as a record of the war it is a docu-
ment of historical importance, since for pos-
terity it recalls the rapidly-varying moods of
the English people and their successive men-
tal and emotional attitudes to the shifting
phases of the war. How well Wells makes
us recall our own absurd and puerile atti-
tudes, as he lays bare the writhing soul of
Mr. Britling'! But the book is not a mere
essay; it is a full-blooded novel of real
people, a story delightful, poignant, sincere
and ending on a note of exalted altruism.
How brilliantly Wells pictures the world
that was!
People may be too safe. We live at the
end of a series of secure generations in which
none of the great things of life have changed
materially. We’ve grown up with no sense
of _ danger—-that is to say, with no sense of
responsibility. None of us really believe that
life can change very fundamentally any more
for ever. All this looks as though it was
bound to go on steadily for ever. It seems in-
credible that the system could be smashed.
.... Old Asquith thinks that we always have
got along, and that we always shall get along,
by being quietly artful and saying: “Wait
and see.”
How, indeed, could England envisage the
possibility of war ? Especially Mr. Britling,
in his secluded home at Matching’s Easy?
What was war to Matching’s Easy—to all
the Matching’s Easies great and small that
make up England? The last home that was
ever burnt by an enemy within a hundred
miles of Matching’s Easy was burnt by
the Danes rather more than a thousand years
ago. And the last trace of those particular
Danes in England was certain horny scraps
of indurated skin under the heads of the
nails in the door of St. Clement Danes in
London.... The English were the spoilt chil-
dren of peace. They had never been wholly
at war for 300 years, and for over 800 years
they had not fought for life against a foreign
Power.
J*
But, inevitably and relentlessly, the war
invades the sodden peace of Matching’s Easy
and the philosophic optimism of Mr. Brit-
ling’s bright English mind—though it re-
serves its cruellest and, indeed, its vital
stroke till the end of the story. One of the
finest chapters in the novel recalls for us our
racial inability to believe that the Germans
could fight with bitterness and malignity. We
may remember how we forced ourselves to
believe that the main body of the Germans
were peaceable, kindly folk, rather fond of
us. Yet Mr. Britling is fair:—•
These Germans were an unsubtle people, a
people in the worst and best sense of the
words, plain and honest; they were prone to
moral indignation ; and moral indignation is
the mother of most of the cruelty of the
world. They perceived the indolence of the
English and the. Russians, they perceived
their disregard of science and system, they
could not perceive the longer reach of these
greater races, and it seemed to them that the
mission of Germany was to chastise and
correct this laxity.... After all, what right
had we English not to have a gun or an
aeroplane fit to bring down that Zeppelin
ignominiously and conclusively? Had we not
undertaken Empire? Were we not the
leaders of great nations?
And his sanguine temperament even led
Mr. Britling to endeavor to discover in
cruelty and hate something good, “greater
perhaps in its ultimate values than mercy?”
And, the inevitable stroke comes: Mr.
Britling’s son enlists. The war passed tin
father by to strike him through his son.
Here were the fathers, who had done
their work, shot their bolts, returned some
value for the cost of their education, unable
to get training, unable to be of any service,
shamefully safe, while their young inno-
cents, untried, all their gathering possibili-
ties of service unbroached, went down into
the deadly trenches He felt himself a
cowardly brute, fat, wheezy, out of training,
sheltering behind his son.
But before Mr. Britling sees it through he
will have ample opportunity to do his share.
We hope that Wells will tell us about it,
too. And Hugh, his son, stepping, young
and smiling, into the tank of blood that
Europe has become, writes home his bril-
liant letters.
War is an exciting game—that I never
wanted to play. It excites once in a couple
of months. And the rest of it is dirt and
muddle and boredom, and smashed houses
and spoilt roads and muddy scenery and
boredom, and the lumbering along of sup-
plies and the lumbering back of the wounded
and weary—and boredom, and continual
vague guessing of how it will end and bore-
dom and boredom and boredom, and thinking
of the work you were going to do and the
travel you were going to have, and the
waste of life and the waste of days and
boredom, and splintered poplars and stink,
everywhere stink and dirt and boredom....
This going to and fro and to and fro and
to and fro; this monotony that breaks evefi
and again into violence —violence that never
gets anywhere—is exactly the life that a
lunatic leads. Melancholia and mania. It’s
just a collective obsession—by war. The
world is really quite mad: I suppose when
an individual man goes mad and dances
about in the street without his trousers jab-
bing at passers-by with a toasting-fork, he
has just the same sombre sense of unavoid-
able necessity that we have, all of us, when
we go off with our packs into the trenches.
So Mr. Britling broods over the careless-
ness, the negligence of those twin slack
giants, England and Russia, opposed by dis-
ciplined scientific force. “It is true that
righteousness should triumph over the
tyrant and the robber, but have carelessness
and incapacity any right to triumph over
capacity and foresight?” Mr. Britling grows
impatient at the Northcliffe clamor for a
change of Government: it seems a pity that
Wells did not wait before ending his story to
tell us Mr. Britling’s opinion of the Lloyd
George coup d’&t.ut.
What’s the good of all this clamoring for
a change of Government? We haven’t a
change of Government. It’s like telling a
tramp to get a change of linen. Our men,
all our public men, are second-rate men,
with the habits of advocates. There is
nothing masterful in their minds. How can
you expect the system to produce anything
else?
Mr. Britling sneers at a suggested Cabinet
of “Carson and Lloyd George and North-
cliffe and Lady Frensham (who is meant
for the fussy society political wire-puller),
with, I suppose, Austin Harrison and
Horatio* Bottomley thrown in—as a Strong
Silent Government.”
We can’t suddenly go back on the past
and alter our type. We’ve never thoroughly
turned out and cleaned tip our higher
schools. We’ve resisted instruction. We’ve
preferred to maintain our national luxuries
of a bench of bishops and party politics.
And compulsory Greek and the university
sneer.... We’ve got to plough through with
it—with what we have —as what we are.. ..
I saw this war, as so many Frenchmen have
seen it, as something that might legitimately
command a splendid enthusiasm of indigna-
tion.... It was all a dream... .Everywhere
cunning, small feuds and hatreds, distrusts,
dishonesties, timidities, feebleness of pur-
pose, dwarfish imaginations, swarm over the
great and simple issues.... It is a war now
like any other of the mobbing, many,-aimed
cataclysms that have shattered empires and
devastated the world; it is a war without
point, a war that has lost its soul, it has
become mere incoherent fighting and destruc-
tion, a demonstration in vast and tragic
forms of the stupidity and ineffectiveness of
our species.
But from such despairs and such bitter-
nesses Mr. Britling rises to a mood of
supreme and exalted charity. For his son
is slain. And in his grief he meditates upon
God, a finite God.
A finite God who struggles in his great
and' comprehensive way as we struggle in
our weak and silly way—who is with, us—-
that is the essence of all real religion. Why!
if I thought there was an omnipotent God
who looked down on battles and deaths and
all the waste and horror of this war—able
to prevent these things—doing them to
amuse Himself —1 would spit in His empty
face.
And he hears all Europe lamenting for
her dead. .. .and writes a letter to the father
of the German tutor who had lived in his
home at Matching’s Easy. That boy had
been slain, like his son. Mr. Britling, who
had been foolish and weak and lovable,
sloughs his mean humanity and learns the
lesson of his tragedy; and pens his letter
from one bereaved father to another, a
letter of consolation and comfort. He be-
comes a peace advocate—the sort of peace
advocate whose motives one can appreciate.
Massacres of boys! That indeed is the
essence of modern war. The killing off of the
young. It is the destruction of the human
inheritance, it is the spending of all the life
and material of the future upon present-day
hate and greed. Fools and knaves, politi-
cians, tricksters and those who trade on the
suspicions and thoughtless, generous angers
of men, make wars; the indolence and
modesty of the mass of men permit them.
Are you and I to suffer such things until the
whole fabric of our civilisation, that has
been so slowly and so laboriously built up, is
altogether destroyed?... .It is you and I
who must stop these wars, these massacres
of boys.
The last chapter is headed: “Mr. Britling
Writes until Sunrise.” He will have to
write until many sunrises before he sees it
through.

What Billzac Reads.
“Two Blues” : Billzac will take you right
to his heart if you send him plenty of
papers and magazines. With regard to
choice, remember that first and foremost he
reads pictures. My own experience was con-
firmed the other day by my cobber’s letter
from France, acknowledging the receipt of
Stead’s and The Triad, which in a spasm
of extreme foolishness I had sent out to
him. “What sort of a cow are you,” lie
wrote, “for sending out that sort of stuff?
For the good Lord’s sake, send out some-
thing with a few blanky pictures in it!”
This is dinkum, and just about expresses
Billzac’s literary taste. He craves for his
home papers, but for general matter give
him an illustrated paper. He simply de-
vours the English halfpenny illustrated
dailies, the more elaborate weeklies, and
lastly, any good short-story magazine that’s
not full of war.
Australian
C. H. Currey’s little study of British
Colonial Policy, 1783-1915 (Oxford Univer-
sity Press, 2s. 6d.), compactly summarises
our national and imperial development. The
bad old days, when the early governors were
usually distinguished military or naval
officers, “frequently destitute of the slightest
knowledge of the resources of the country
and of the feelings, interests and aspirations
of the people over whom they were to gov-
ern,” when “the oversea Englishmen felt
that England regarded them with mingled
contempt and condescension, and warmly
resented her attitude,” when “anything was
regarded as good enough for the colonies,”
when they were looked upon as merely “very
convenient places for persons of tainted
imputation and broken fortunes to recoup,”
and when “broad acres belonging to the
Crown were either given way or sold for
ridiculously low prices,” are feelingly
described.
Dealing with the question of re-organisa-
tion of the Empire, the author, who is
Lecturer in History at the Teachers’ Col-
lege, Sydney, rightly says that “The Federal
ideal has always found more support in the
homeland than in the oversea Dominions,
but even there it has never commanded the
enthusiasm of responsible statesmen in re-
cent years,” while the suggestion that the
present British Parliament should be placed
on a parity with Dominion legislatures, sub-
ordinated to the suggested Imperial Parlia-
ment, is at present regarded by the British
Government “with uncompromising hostil-
ity.” That double obstacle may no doubt be
surmounted under the pressure of the strin-
gent necessities of the post-bellum period;
but it is evident that L. Curtis’s project for
an Imperial Federation needs all the driving
power of enthusiasm to reach its goal.
On the question of Dominion navies,
Currey aptly points out the parlous situation
of Australasia “if a strong European Power
and an Asiatic Power were to declare war
on the British Empire simultaneously,” or
“even were Japan to declare war against
England.” “How would the United Kingdom
fare during the absence of the British fleet
if a European Power wished to have revenge ;
and, moreover, what would be the condition
of that fleet by the time it reached the
theatre of war?” Rightly or wrongly, the
people of Australia, Maoriland and British
Columbia, fear that their lands may be
peacefully penetrated or roughly seized by
a strong Asiatic Power seeking to expand.”
So he ends with the statement that the only
obvious policy, consonant with our national
aspirations, is one which supports the cre-
ation of Dominion fleets, growing and work-
ing in co-operation with the British fleet.
Frequent Imperial conferences and a wider
latitude of discussion thereat will, in
Currey’s opinion, safeguard the future of
the development of the Empire.
J*
Val Jameson writes Peter's 'Nationality
(W. Brookes, Sydney) with a purpose.
Books written with a purpose are seldom
works of art. But her heart has been
touched by stories of cruelties to which our
State-kids are subjected when they are
boarded-out in the country districts, where
the inspectors’ visits are few and probably
foreseen, and the difficulty of proving
cruelty is naturally great. For her purpose
Val Jameson has made the torturers of her
forlorn and half-starved hero a German
family, thus fatally weakening' her case.
There is no likelihood in the future of the
State boarding-out its kids with German
settlers: what the author ought to have
shown is that the evil may exist among Aus-
tralian farmers. The story is earnestly
written, and frankly makes its appeal for
the reader’s sympathy and indignation. The
types are well drawn, and though the long
arm of coincidence rudely grasps the climax,
the author evidently knows something about
the conditions she hopes to alter.
For Is. 3d. you may acquire Preludes, by
Alan Gross (Australasian Authors’ Agency,
Melb.). As there are only 13 preludes, though
most of them are short, you pay but a frac-
tion over one penny per prelude. Sounds
cheaper.than State fish, but though the author
knows how to rime prettily, and has a choice
ear for verbal melody, he has nothing else to
put into his pretty fancies. But the first of
this baker’s dozen of verses can be recom-
mended “to the Old Boys of Wesley College
who are serving the Empire,” because of its
faith in the old school:—
Because through many a bloodless field and fray
We learned the code which governs war and play;
Because our hearts were tempered in that fire
Whose flames are hot with pride and righteous ire;
Because we know for what exalted faith
Our Lion stands; and hear in battle wraith
The younger breezes blow; because we hold
our schoolboys’ oath as honored as of old—-
To play the game ; to set above the fee
Honor and Truth, Freedom and Courtesy ;
And knowing well what manner of foes assail
Our King, and how our race can never quail;
Because of this, O Motherland, we bring
All that our lives can bear, our lips can sing.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Seated Yonder.
Hastings, whom I knew of yore,
Home from war to-night I greeted ;
Though he’s gone this hour and more,
Yonder, still, I see him seated.
Somewhat sterner, grey and set,
Worn by war, more stained by weather,
So he seemed, than when we met
Two years gone this night together.
How he talked then, while we sat
By the red logs richly glowing—
Now of this and now of that,
All the soul within him showing!
How he talked then, two years gone—
Threading.homespun themes with laughter;
While the fickle firelight shone,
Lighting blackened beam and rafter!
Then, of something sweet and bright,
Less of earth than Heaven, he hinted;
How upon a certain night
All his world grew rainbow-tinted.
Here one did not need to seek,
Prise and pry to find the lover;
More than fire-glow lit his cheek
As he named her three times over.
This was two years since, and then
Smiling Fate bent o’er his shoulder.
Now, he came to-night again
Somewhat greyer, sterner, older.
01der.... yes, and somewhat grave,
As when one looks through the pleasant
World about him, bright and brave,
On a dread scene grimly present.
Yet, what more could man desire
Than that hour of cordial greeting—
Heart warmth, and a warmth of fire
For the chilled blood’s genial heating?
Much had happened, much ’twas plain
Best forgotten ; war and glory,
When the wounds still ache with pain,
Make no pleasant fireside story.
Yet Love stayed ; and having this
Had he not Life’s best and sweetest—
Youth’s gemmed crown, consummate bliss;
Heaven-on-eai;th and joy completest?
Tick by tick the moments sped,
Yet the one great question tarried....
Then he spoke it; and I said,
As one says some trifle: '“Married !”
Pain more fierce than Death, I think,
Scourged him with its knotted lashes
At my one word ; with a clink
Fell a live coal on the ashes.
Then he left me, broad brows knit,
Much as one who seems to ponder
Some great puzzle.... and I sit
Seeing him still seated yonder.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Tree.
My roots grow stronger in the earth,
And I perforce give birth on birth
To leaf and twig that grow apace
In height and strength and forest grace.
But birds when nesting shun my boughs;
Beneath are heard no lovers’ vows;
The daisies born within my shade
Quiver with fear and swiftly fade.
And children pass with quickened pace,
Save he who with youth’s easy grace
Will catch my lowest branch and swing,
Feet dancing on the air, and sing.
(My branches rock with cursed glee,
I know the thing that is to be —
That blackened tongue, those eyes astare.
Swinging and dancing on the air!)
Tossing their laughter to the sky,
Glad of their growth the trees near-by,
Each in its turn, will be hewn down
And fashioned in the neighboring town.
And one whose limbs are green and brave
Shall hear the shout of storm and wave,
Or in the holy quiet be
The cross of Christ’s own agony.
And one a bow shall teach to sing
Long notes on music’s softest string.
Voicing the soul of that sweet wind
It knew when sun and star were kind.
To toll of bell one will be sped,
Clothing with dignity the dead,
When that fierce, passionate lover, Death,
Kissing, has choked the struggling breath.
From seedling each grows to its end
(Under his weight I sway and bend),
Since sun or time or seed might be
I am foredoomed —the gallows tree!
MAORILAND.
JOHN EGAN.
The Gods that Youth Loves.
Oh, I have loved the cheeks of May,
And sought the eyes of Flo,
Have worshipped at the lips of Fay;
Till, with my years, I know
That charms will fade, and beauties fly.
Because the gods that youth loves die!
I’ve lived within a golden dream,
Have felt ambition strong,
And high intentions, like a stream.
Have swept my days along:
I view them not without a sigh—
Alas, the gods that youth loves die!
Like myths from times when earth was
young,
And Jove was still alive,
To grace a song that may be sung.
My memories survive—
Sweet, pleasant things! I know not why,
But all the gods that youth loves die.
N.S.W.

LOW PRICES FOR SPARE PARTS.
Great Advantages of Standardisation.
Substantial Inducement to Purchasers.
One of the great advantages of the standardisation of
the Dodge Brothers Car, which in its main features
remains practically without alteration from year to year,
though refinements are continually being added, is that
a full range of spare parts can always be maintained by
the distributors. Moreover, on account of the very large
production, the prices can be made more than attractive.
All spare parts are sold at low prices, but in order
to fully appreciate the extraordinary values disclosed by
the rates in force it is necessary to see the parts them-
selves, and we therefore extend a cordial invitation to all
motorists to inspect same.
The permanency of the Dodge Brothers standard
overcomes the difficulties inseparable from alteration of
parts in consequence of change in models, and makes for
substantial economy to motorists.
Dalgety & Company Limited,
Sydney and Brisbane,
Agents for New South Wales and Queensland.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex
pressly reserved by the publishers.

ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form, of

and not exceeding Jtwo columns in length, or, say,

Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 21, 1916.
THE PITY OF THE WOLF.
“Germany has been seized with pity at the
unspeakable misery of humanity and is ready
to give peace to the world.” That is the
text of the gentle, soul-stirring note which
has gone forward from the Kaiser to the
Pope; and the world naturally reads it
through misted eyes - and feels its universal
waistcoat swell with emotion at the thought
of the vast mercy of the Teuton taking up
the Christmas refrain where the Herald
Angels dropped it nineteen hundred years
ago. The war is not at a standstill. Far
from it! If at places it pauses for breath,
it does so in the hope that the next blow
of its paw will be more murderous than
any that have gone before. Russia is
gathering strength ; France, clear-eyed, shows
no sign of shrinking from the biggest test;
Italy keeps busy and enthusiastic; Britain
is slowly beginning to be resolute. At the
lower end of Europe, Turkey, Bulgaria,
Servia, Roumania and Greece are tangled up
with their aiders and abettors in a clawing,
mauling, stabbing mess. On the sea the
big British fleet watches for battle, while
the enemy submarine crawls under the waves
to make war on the collier and the trawler
and buoys itself with hope of getting a hos-
pital ship or a passenger liner freighted with
women and children. In the centre of all
the disturbance lie the Teuton empires,
which up to the present we have regarded
as wounded giants snorting with fury and
working destruction in its most horrible
forms, but which prove on their own assur-
ances to be lovers of peace “seized with pity
at the unspeakable misery of humanity” and
ready to fold all .the world in a vast
embrace of love. They have told the Pope
so and hinted the same thing to Woodrow
Wilson.
* * * *
Certainly, with the pungent war-smoke
blowing in our eyes, it is hard to get a dis-
tinct view; but through the cloud the Ger-
man peace proposals and the German peace
sentiments don’t appear to have been turned
out at the same factory. The offer made
by the pitying Kaiser and his fellow-con-
spirators is that the war should cease, that
Belgium and France should be evacuated by
the invader, that Poland and Lithuania
should be torn from Russia and formed into
a separate kingdom, that the affairs of the
Balkans should be unravelled without fur-
ther use of cordite, and that all Germanyls
colonies should be handed over intact as
before the war. The programme sounds
quite a pleasant one and suggests that every-
body having learned the great lesson that
powder burns ‘and knives cut should
go back again to his job of ploughing or sell-
ing shoddy and forget what has happened
in the last, two and a half years. Had the
war game been played on old-fashioned lines,
it is quite possible that the offer might look
almost decent; or had the war been due to
a sudden madness on the part of all the
nations engaged, it might at least have
formed a decent basis for negotiations.
Unfortunately, however, the big controversy
has been bad both in origin and in manners.
England’s reason for joining the fight was
the trampling of Belgium. Murder, lust,
rapine, theft and destruction were let loose
on the Belgians by the Germans who had
sworn to protect them. The “unspeakable
misery of humanity” was deliberately
planned in Berlin, advocated by statesmen,
explained and excused by philosophers, sanc-
tioned and blessed by the clergy and carried
out by German soldiers with every accom-
paniment of abominable crime and every
refinement of hideous cruelty. That Germany
should leave the country that has been raped
and murdered is every way desirable; but
by merely leaving it she makes no atone-
ment for her crimes committed there. Will
Germany, in quitting the country she was
pledged to protect and ruthlessly attacked,
build again the broken cities, sow again the
war-trampled fields, restore to Belgian citi-
zens the wealth, filched from them, hang a
German soldier for every rape and murder
committed, and leave hostages of her princely
families in the hands of the Belgians as a
guarantee that the next treaty will be some-
thing more than “a scrap of paper”? In
the light of her declaration of pity this
would seem no more than a fair thing. To
merely get out of the country after she has
destroyed it does not suggest any particular
enlargement of the heart. Then there are
the other places that have been strafed in
a similar fashion to be considered. Belgium
was the cause of Britain’s entrance into the
war, but it is not the sole cause of Britain’s
staying in it. The German pity, new from
the local hunger-factory, must expand and
amplify itself before it can get us to regard
it as the real article. If the burglar enters
the' house, smashes the furniture, robs the
safe, kills the housemaid, ruptures the cat
and sets fire to the washhouse, his penitence
for his crime is not sufficiently indicated by
his mere willingness to walk away with so
much of the plunder as he can conveniently
carry.
* * * *
The fact is that, whatever started the
war, it is now being waged for the destruc-
tion of the Prussian spirit of organised
aggression. If that is not smashed it will
have won the war whatever nations are
nominally victors. To stop now, even if
the Germans gave twice as much as they
have offered, would be to Prussianise the.
world. Every nation would have to arm
itself to the teeth and stay armed all the
time; and arming to the teeth when there
isn’t any immediate cause for it is the very
soul of Prussianism. If we believe that we
can win the war straight out, the cheapest
thing we can do is to win it. Compromise
must cost more in the end. If we are not
sure about being able to win the war, the
best thing we can do is to try to win it,
because that is the most expensive _ thing
we can force on the enemy, and Prussianism
exhausted and scared is Prussianism beaten.
The great new outburst of German pity,
which may or may not have for its driving
force the lack of sausage in Hamburg and
elsewhere, is nothing to the vast pity of
the Allied nations who foresee the possible
miseries of the future as well as the present
“unspeakable” ones. However sorry the
Teuton may or may not feel about it now,
the fact remains that he is solely responsible
for what humanity has suffered through this
war, and he is the responsible person who
must pay. There is a verdict _of wilful
murder recorded in the English courts
against Wilhelm the Baby Killer, and there
is an unwritten verdict to the same effect
recorded against the German nation in the
Court of Mankind. Whether the Allies can
break the power which imposed these and
other countless crimes upon the world is
another question ; but it would be better to
allow the war to become a fixture than to
answer cant with cant and fool ourselves
out of our convictions merely because the
enemy uses a pleasant catchword.
ARBITRATION AND THE MELTING-
POT.
Joe Carruthers, ex-Premier of N. S.
Wales, pushed himself forward the other day
to remark that the Arbitration Act should
be publicly burned by the common hangman.
George Beeby, having hooked a portfolio in
the Ministry of the same State, took the
opportunity to announce that in his judg-
ment much of the industrial legislation
relating to wages would have to be scrapped.
Multitudinous strikes have been organised
during the last year or so for the purpose of
expressing the contempt of various unions
for the arbitration system. The general
attitude of office-holding politicians has been
that of a crowd conspiring to reduce the
business of arbitration to farce. Under all
these circumstances, it is not improbable
that the opinions expressed by Carruthers
and Beeby represent the ideas entertained by
a very considerable number of people. The
notion prevalent among one mob appears to
be that, after minute calculations involving
such diverse factors as the purchasing value
of a sovereign and the sustenance to be
derived from given allowances of proteids
and carbohydrates, the State should fix a
minimum wage', below which competition
would be illegal, leaving all the re«t to look
after itself. Among another crowd there is
the idea that organised use of the big stick
is a more reliable method _ of hitting up
wages and improving conditions than strug-
gling to get those things through a Court.
Between them all, these people who. for
reasons of various kinds, are hostile to
arbitration, have made hash of the system.
Now. therefore, Australia must either re-
cast the law and administer it or confess
failure to protect the citizens and the nation
from outrage by warring combinations of
bosses and workmen. In the event of a con-
fession of failure Joe Carrutiiers will be
able to whistle up the official neck-breakers,
and then it won’t be long before the Aus-
tralian people will learn by bitter experience
that cutting one another’s throat is about
the poorest method ever devised of reducing
order out of chaos.
It is as certain as the multiplication table
that an arbitration system which cannot
prevent strikes is not worth tuppence. It is
also open to no sort of doubt that if a large
body of men decide not to work they cannot
be compelled to by any means that would
be tolerated in this country, or that would
be expedient anywhere else. Further, there
are multitudinous reasons to suspect that no
system devised to get rid of strikes by (1)
making them illegal and (2) removing the
reasons which might impel men to strike,
can be anything but a joke if it is treated
like a circus and if Parliaments turn every-
thing which it accomplishes into ridicule.
It is these latter bodies which have done the
greatest damage. They have acted as if
Arbitration Court' awards were something
that stood detached from the work of Par-
liament —that Parliament could legislate
and borrow and boom with confidence that
the bloated conditions thus created would be
met by awards of the Courts. And they have
persistently shirked the responsibility of re-
cognising that, even if men cannot be com-
pelled by the State to work, the State has a
right to expect them not to enter into plots
to create disorder. Under the old system
of bash and biff between the boss and the
toiler, a strike was regarded as a matter
between the principals only. The rights of
the community to be considered were only
recognised after long argument—and mainly
after Labor had grown convinced that the
strike was a dangerous weapon. From that
wretched welter of conflicting interests grew
a higher conception of citizenship and of
communal rights and responsibilities. It was
this which gave birth to the arbitration sys-
tem, and in the view of this paper, mon-
strous though the mistakes have been
and grossly though the system has been
abused, that ideal of civic responsibility
was far higher and better than the one it
superseded, and more decent than many of
those which have been operating to strangle
it. And disappointing though the scheme of
arbitration may have been in many ways,
and dishevelled though it looks to-day, The
Bulletin is firmly convinced that it repre-
sents something of more value to the citizens
of Australia than a reversion to conditions
under which Capital and Labor punched each
other round the ring, with the State looking
on as a mere observer.
* * * *
The rock-bottom causes of arbitration
getting into a state of coma are, broadly,
two in number. First, from it has devel-
oped the dead-level wage and ihe application
of the one-man-is-as-good-as-another theory to
industrial effort; and, in the second place,
when Labor obtains another Is. a day, it
presently discovers itself to be often no
better and sometimes worse off. Neither of
these things is incapable of correction.
The first of them is obviously a blot.
That a skilful, industrious tradesman
should receive no more than one who
is something else is, as the world
stands to-day, an atrocity. And it is
equally atrocious that the deserving wage-
earner who gets an award of 10s. instead of
the old 9s. a day, should find that he really
gets 18s. disguised as fl. When this hap-
pens, and generally speaking it has been
happening all along the line, there naturally
enough grows up a suspicion that arbitra-
tion is no use. It is here that public policy
has been at fault in not protecting the wage-
earner. No scheme of wage-fixing can
claim admiration from Labor if, while the
fixer is making his adjustment, other insti-
tutions are at work to destroy it. It is use-
less to tell Labor that it has had an increase
of 25 per cent, in wages if the advance yields
no more than the coins it received previously.
No “expediting of awards,” no framing of
rules and regulations, no frantic bargaining
over a bound and gagged public such as
took place in connection with the colliery
strike, can make the arbitration system look
any better than it is, unless such advantages
as it secures to Labor are real instead of
fictional. Labor has everything to gain and
nothing to lose by directing its political
activities to this end, instead of applauding
the incendiary counsel of Joe Carruthers
or inciting Beeby to acts of destruction.
Any fool can burn things or chop them up.
There are some unions which, taking the
narrow view and regarding the public as-
fair game, can do as well without arbitra-
tion as with it. But there are many that
cannot, and there is but a poor sense of
citizenship in a community that is content
to be the chopping-block of industrial war-
fare. To the great body of working men
and women, arbitration, backed by a Parlia-
ment with reasonable vision, instead of one
reckless of the public welfare, can be a
sound protection against exploitation. The
alternative to it is a' squalid struggle in
which the strongest goes to the wall and
the weakest into the gutter; in which Labor
preys incessantly on Labor, and the march
is always to degradation. The trouble is
not that arbitration is in itself defective for
the purposes in view, but that Australian
Parliaments have gone upon a wild career
of borrowing and booming and allowed the
economic conditions of the people to go to
the Devil. These conditions can no more
be controlled by an Arbitration Court alone
than by a billygoat.
HELL’S OUTCAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am dead now, and in Hell, so I have no
reason to lie. Hell, by the way—at any
rate my particular sector —is quite different
from the place which the Churches picture.
In life I was a heavy drinker and frequenter
of race-meetings, disorderly houses, boxing-
matches and hotels. Here I inhabit a vast,
barn-like building with a tin roof —a Metho-
dist chapel multiplied by x. A cold wind
whistles eternally through the open windows,
and it rains day and night. Clerical devils—
narrow-browed little men, with red noses
and monkey-shaves —preach to us in relays
on the virtues of hating the people in the
opposition chapels. We sit shivering in-
terminably, on wooden benches, our feet on
the cold, uncarpeted floor. Sour milk and
stale buns are handed round three times a
day. We will eat and drink nothing else
for ever and ever. • %
This, as I have said, is my hell.
In the distance we can hear the roars of
Stadium crowds, the crash of glasses banged
upon bar-counters, and the cheers of the
Ring, mingling with the oaths of punters, as
favorites finish “down the course ” In this
environment may be met the lost souls of
such men as the Rev. Elias Stinger (in
life he opposed the regulation of all “vice,”
so that those who fell might suffer the worst
torments for the discouragement of the
others), Lumpleet, M.L.A., who got into
Parliament originally on the Social Purity
ticket, and many thousands of virtuous
people who made the fatal error of thinking
that to be virulently Slanderous is to be
vehemently Good. Scandalous songs and
dances, rendered by female fiends in low-
necked dresses ; bad language; the spectacle
of beer flowing like water —by these and
kindred horrors myriads of Pharisees are
tortured without mercy or respite. Hence
the phrase that each of us makes his own hell.
It applies with peculiar force to my case.
I am one of an accursed band —the men
who did nothing for their country during the
Great War. I was not exactly afraid. In
hell, as in heaven, they have a better sense
of moral values than to blame for not fight-
ing those whose hearts at the thought of a
fight turn to water within them. I have
fought often, more especially when drunk;
and where the other man was smaller or
weaker, I found the experience stimulating
—even pleasant. When the Big Catas-
trophes began to happen to our Empire
neither domestic nor financial considerations
kept me at home. As regards fitness I never
knew a day’s illness in 20 years which was
not occasioned by my own excesses. My
dogged avoidance of the Front was due to
one thing and one only—cunning. I knew
too much. I argued the position out with
my soul and decided that volunteering
was not good enough. In Hell no one is
allowed to possess a soul. It is one
of the most awful features of the place. The
mighty army of the Lost see, as in a looking-
glass, what one is—which is to say, what
one was on earth. Each new arrival from
the bloody battlefields of the world regards
me as I sit alone, on the end of my bench,
listening to the interminable nasal drone of
the preacher’s voice, and turns immediately
away in loathing. Judas is popular by
comparison with me. He sold his Master. I
anf of the despised who sold their country.
Regarded from the standpoint of a man
of the world, my attitude towards the War
was, of course, unassailable. What would
I get by enlisting? The obvious answer
was Nothin g! There was little kudos to
be gained by volunteering when most of one’s
friends had done it already, and when threats
of conscription were sounding louder and
louder. The chances of death or mutila-
tion, if one had the ill-fortune to reach the
firing-line, were considerable ; and' there was
the possibility of disease. Also there were
the questions of discomfort, of the pros-
pects of teetotalism for long periods,
and of the solid physical work involved.
The social penalties for shirking were sur-
prisingly light when considered in a philo-
sophic spirit. They were divisible, as I
reasoned it out, into present and future dis-
abilities. The present ones took the form of
the contempt, sometimes even the openly-
expressed anger, of women whose relations
were fighting or dead, elderly male patriots,
and returned soldiers whose comrades
were beinsr slaughtered for lack of reinforce-
ments. I explained to these nuisances, when
assailed, that I would not, for private
reasons, enlist in the A.1.F., but that I
was leaving in September (if it was then
April) or in April (if it happened to be
September) for England, where I proposed
to do my bit in a way that would horrify
the Huns. At one time certain plainspoken
acquaintances became so offensively incred-
ulous, both to my face and behind my back,
that I thought seriously of going to London
and there representing that I was waiting
only for the following September (or April,
as the case might be) to hurry back to Aus-
tralia and join the A.I.F. Some of my
friends had executed this manoeuvre with
great success. By careful attention to the
bottle and other joys, and bv a convincing
affectation of military ineptitude, a number
of them had contrived to be kept on Home
Defence jobs for as much as a year after
enlisting. I resolved that if, ultimately, I
was kicked on board a London-bound vessel
by the boot of popular derision, it would be
my own fault if the British War Office was
so mad as to put me in the firing-line. Mean-
while the assertion that I 'was going within
a few months, or would go if the situation
didn’t improve, served, at the worst, as a
temporary dam against reproach and scorn.
In regard to my position when the war
ended, I had few fears. Soldiers are never
popular after a great war. People want to
forget the horror; and one of the easiest
ways to forget is to ignore those who have
participated in it. The characters ot the
innumerable good men are besmirched by
the actions of uniformed wasters. In my
own interests I rarely neglected a chance
to get a returned soldier drunk. I calculated
that if only 50 per cent, of civilians who
were situated as I was followed suit, the
bulk of our defenders would be regarded by
law-abiding people as pariahs. I also hoped
that, before the finish, most men of 4o and
under would be either dead _or crippled.
Those who, by warily remaining at home,
had eluded both fates, would then be in a
very strong position. They would have com-
mand of their own market, so to speak. Ao
one would dream of asking these healthy,
care-free, solvent and unhaunted individuals
why they had not plunged into the just-
stilled whirlpool of blood and agony. -Ihe
sentiment of the community in regard to
the fools who had —the immemorial attitude
of non-conscript countries towards those
who have done the fighting for them would


be my shield and buckler. I visioned the
shirker as a post-war autocrat, as one who
could pick and choose at will in the matter
of jobs, women and the like.
My life, while waiting for the war to end,
was by no means unpleasant. It resenmled,
in many ways, what I pictured it would be
when the Great Danger—that of being con-
scripted—should have passed away. While
I was at home I kept my family in a pleas-
ingly nervous and adulatory frame of mind
bv the good old gag about going presently
to England to enlist. I was by no means
shunned. On the contrary. Many of my
drinking associates were situated similarly
to myself. They also intended, at some un-
specified time in the future, to enlist
in England. After seven or eight whiskies
we used actually to feel that we were the
real heroes, inasmuch as we meant to face
known horrors, while those who had already
done so had not realised what they
were in for when they enlisted, and so were
entitled to no great respect. A jarring note
was struck now and again. I remember
my cousin Adolphus telling, in a palpably
hopeful fashion, how two friends of his, who
had left Melbourne in a blaze of glory 18
months previously, were still living in
security and comfort as civilians somewhere
in Scotland. This was altogether the wrong
line; but the wretched youth had imbibed
too much to be capable of appreciating the
fact. I was fairly welcome in Society,
despite my disreputable habits. Drunk or
sober, I could usually get particular
women of my acquaintance to be seen with
me in public. They despised me for a cur
and a soaker, and showed it; but there was
a deplorable shortage of men.
And then came my absurd death. I
tumbled down a flight of hotel steps
one night, and broke my neck. In
Hell self-deception is no longer pos-
sible. I heard my epitaph pronounced
by scores of those whom I had told
myself were admirers, if not friends.
“Tight as usual, I suppose, when it hap-
pened. Well, he was no loss.” “Poor fel-
low ! So sudden! But then, he never could
keep sober.” “A shirker and a sot, sir, and
a damned good riddance whichever way you
look at it.” And so on.
But the bitterest hatred and contempt are
to be read upon the open minds of those
who lived as I did on earth and who are
hence called upon to share my fate, but
who, at least, died like men. They shun
me. They gather together, in the horrible
Sunday-go-to-meeting toppers and frock-
coats which we are forced to wear here, and
revile me. I am one of the loneliest men in
Hell. Torquemada sat on my bench for a
little while, having, with a hideous refine-
ment of cruelty, been shifted to this atmo-
sphere of chilly Dissent about a century ago.
I ventured a remark on the disgusting
quality of the milk. He did not answer—
simply took up'his tall hat and hymn-book
and moved away. As the war goes on I
can see nothing ahead but more misery and
more shame. I commented to Deeming on
the difficulty of keeping one’s feet warm.
“You oughtn’t to find cold feet a change,”
he replied brutally, and joined Torquemada.
And this sort of thing is to last for all
eternity J
T. B. D.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Philosophy of Ice-creams.
«t>
lat O Maori discourses :
The source of the great war cannot be found in
the slaughter of a royal personage in Bosnia or in
the desire of the Teuton to sit in the sun. It goes
deeper than that. The real cause of war is idleness
the furious desire to exercise the muscles of big
emotion which have grown flaccid through the
cramping times of peace. Work —the thing com-
monly called work —is not man’s real business at all.
Mere elephants can be taught to pile logs, and dogs
to pull carts and sledges. So the toil man accom-
plishes with a spade or an axe or a pen is on the
animal plane. Where he differs from the animal is
in emotion; and consequently emotion is his true
sphere of labor. The thing called civilisation
cramps and narrows all emotion, and in doing so
twists manhood round and round in its own length
until it grows giddy, loses its grip and breaks cut
into some form of madness. Exhibited on a ji g
scale, that madness usually takes the form of war.
Then wise men get their spectacles close together
and decide that what the world wants by way of
cure is a double dose of the thing that caused it to
go off the handle. •
Nobody seems to think that a counter-excitement
greater than the war itself, but milder in its effects,
might be the true cure. The whole effort of kindly
neutrals, flying their stuffed doves from an ark of
humanitarian theory, is towards restraint and
repression, while in reality a larger and more intense
field for emotion might work the trick. Battles in
the old days sometimes paused right in the middle
of their blood and fury to look at two champions
putting up a special exhibition of attempted
murder; and many a Viking raid stopped short
against a mass of beer and forgot to go on, being
diverted by the emotion of horn-draining competi-
tions, which called for more valor than the swing-
ing of the chain-hammer or morning-glory. In the
case of the present war, short of the arrival of an
archangel with a flaming trumpet to announce the
Day of Judgment with sulphur trimmings, there
seems to be no way left of providing counter attrac-
tions for the combatant nations. Death—the top
adventure of life—is always so near . the trenches
that, unless some genius can provide a thrill greater
than dying, it seems inevitable that the war must go
on until it lies down from plain exhaustion.
With regard to the future, however, a lot can be
done. The chief human need must be properly
catered for, and the umbrella of sad-hued reform
must be folded away. To get down to detail, it is
things like the prohibition of liquor, the prudery
that narrows the scope of public entertainment, the
insistence on the old Hebrew Ten. Commandments, and
the general atmosphere of moral coercion that make
wars possible. Nobody who can do exactly as he
likes is going to war; and there isn’t any special
reason why everybody shouldn’t do exactly as he
likes within very wide limits. The thing that man
has in common with the animal comes nicely within
the sphere of law and order; it is the thing man
has in common with the Devil that lifts him to the
higher emotional plane. No devil could ever have
planned any hell worse than that which rages in
France and Roumania; but a world of decent
devils, given a fair chance, wouldn’t make a hell
at all. They would make an Arcadia, and turn to
Fauns and Satyrs, exactly as the Pelasgian devils did
when they came into the joyous freedom of a Greek
poet’s dream. Let the barriers of human restraint
go down, kick out Bung the moralist and Stiggins
the libertine, and there will be no more war.
Wilhelm could then cry out for a .place in the
sun, and 40,000,000 Teutons over their beer would
tell him to go and sit in it. The whole trouble is
that the safety-valve has been screwed down too
tight; and on the evidences persons in authority,
mole-blind to the shape and make of things, intend
to screw it tighter. __ Why, the English Government
has now prohibited the sale of ice-creams 1
Dodder.
The average age of British generals taking
a hand in the war is said to be 63, while the
big Russians who have been doing things
whenever there was powder to load the gun
are a trifle older. It may be all right to
thus give the command to veterans, but,
again, it may be all wrong. Wellington
was 46 when he smashed Napoleon at
Waterloo, and Nelson 47 when he died at
Trafalgar; but both these English fighters
had reached their prime some years earlier.
Wellington looked as good as ever he was
in the first hours of his Peninsula command
and Nelson certainly was at the top of his
powers at Aboukir Bay. So the yarn that
it takes a thousand years or so to train a
general and a bit more to complete the
education of a icommander-in-chief is at
least doubtful. Certainly the sort of war
French and Haig have had to fight differs
a lot from that of the Napoleonic age ; but
so did that of the Napoleonic age from the
stoush of classic days. J. Caesar was at
the top of his military fame at 45; Hanni-
bal touched his highest success at 29 ;
Alexander smashed Darius at Issus at
23. _ Unless it can be shown that military
genius is now made instead of born, the in-
ference. is that the young man can best carry
the weight and responsibility of tremendous
deeds in the field. It is said that only once
in British history did an old man carry a
battle through to a complete and satisfactory
finish, and in that case—the controversy
between Duncan and the Dutch at Cam-
perdown—the admiral, though 70 years of
age, was a giant in jsize and strength, and
heavily fortified with prayer. It looks as if
Australia’s great defence job in the years to
come might be to discover a means by which
the young man with war genius could over-
leap the red-tape and assume a high com-
mand before his seeing eye got dim and his
notions of handing out punishment crystal-
lised into mere formal*.

What About the Local War Machine?
An explanation from Defence Minister
Pearce about the departmental cashbox and
administrative machinery might be a good
thing to kick off the New Year with. Con-
scription is out; recruiting on the voluntary
basis is proceeding slowly. In the state-
ment of expenditure estimated for the
current year by the Defence Department,
submitted in September, it was announced
that £70,830,965 would be required to meet
the cost of maintaining a daily average of
50,000 troops in camp in Australia and
keeping the army at an average monthly
strength of 312,1.000 until June, 1917. It is
not giving away anything to the enemy to
say that this estimate of military strength is
not being realised —that instead of keeping
50,000 men in camp and shipping 3000 a week
oversea the numbers handled are much fewer.
As Pearce had all the apparatus and red-
tape for dealing with the larger bodies of
men, a statement of what this departmental
equipment is doing now would not be
out of place. If it is eating its head off
there is no reason why the taxpayers should
not be told. If it is rushing away to the
Front it would do no harm if the public
heard the facts. Anyhow, it seems that what-
ever it is doing the organisation for grap-
p mg with what was coming if compulsory
service had arrived is an unnecessarily ex-
pensive institution to maintain for grap-
pling with what has materialised since. The
circumstances make it not unreasonable to
suppose that there is a quantity of lumber
i? e Premises which might without injury
to the Treasury be cleaned away and that
the time is ripe for someone to overhaul
the business side of this Department.. There
are an amazing number of individuals about
m khaki who, whatever else they may be.
are neither returned soldiers nor reinforce-
ments. There might have been something
for them to do if the yell for “No” had not
interrupted the proceedings. As it is this
paper has two suspicions. One is that some
of these people are killing time instead of
Germans, and that there are others who
might without disrupting the national organ-
make way for a few returned soldiers.

marks about his War Offiee? ’

The Hunger for Something on the Nod.
Senator Gardiner's defence of O’Malley’s
dole of free railway passes is worth framing
Gardiner puts it this way:—
To refuse a retiring Minister a free pass is un-
worthy cheeseparing, and shows that there Is not a
spark of gratitude to the splendid public men who
have neglected their own affairs in the interests of the
people. When real democracy becomes a governing
principle, men who served it well, but had at last
been turned out by an adverse vote, would receive
the same consideration which nearly all reputable
firms extended to their employees.
This is as relevant to the point at issue as
it Gardiner had made an impassioned
address about rabbits; and the same sort
of nonsense might be talked with equal
cogency if O’Malley had handed out pants
or cigars. Why should splendid public men
like Gardiner and O’Malley and Pearce
and Millie Ivelly have to buy their own
pants when they are no longer looking
after “the interests of the people”? The
answer to that, whatever it may. be, is
the same as the reply that must be giveu
to the question why they should have to
pay, like others, for being dragged about
on the Commonwealth Railways. Poor old
democracy sees some queer things done in
its name, and this justification of a steal
on the ground that it is a prelude to a
blessed time when everyone will be able
to get in as a deadhead is a cheerful
illustration of what some people imagine
democracy means. Every time a poli-
tician or anyone else raids the public
cash-box or acquires a “concession” of
any sort or is converted into a barnacle
the taxpayers are being looted. Every dole
handed by a politician to himself or his
friends has to be paid for by the man who
works. Every parasite battening on the
democracy draws sustenance from the sweat
of the mob. And there is nothing more
“splendid,” socially or politically, about
men like Andy Fisher and Kink
O’Malley and Gardiner and the rest of
them than there is about the most obscure
toiler in the land. In so far as the obscure
toiler devotes his time to something else
than hatching schemes by which he can
become a hanger on the public funds he
is worth a thousand of them. The objection
to O’Malley’s vicarious generosity is that
grabbing the taxpayers’ money to create
hangers-on is a scandalous abuse of autho-
rity. If Gardiner feels equal to the task
of stating a case in favor of politicians pass-
ing round surreptitious charity to Parlia-
mentarians and their wives (and camp fol-
lowers), he ought to make a start. But
he cannot do it without becoming involved
in advocacy of all that frowsy, disreputable
process of nepotism and patronage and
plunder which most democrats viewed in
the past with disgust.
The Mess that Jack Made.
Westralia’s deficit is climbing towards the
two-million mark. This is a deplorable
record for about years of Labor adminis-
tration and a few months of shaky control by
the Liberal team. Driven by an irresponsi-
ble crowd, Scaddan essayed to reduce the
cost of everything from mutton to mowing-
machines ; and except for the fluke of heavy
war earnings by two of the State steamers,
which are not carrying cheap beef for the
local consumer, the combined showing of the
State enterprises would be appalling. Happy
(sometimes) Jack was certainly struck by
a disastrous and a partial drought during his
term; and the war blew out the timber in-
dustry, sending the railway earnings down.
But instead of economising, as the circum-
stances demanded, Scaddan threw loan
money about so prodigally that the State’s
interest and sinking fund bill increased by
over three-quarters of a million in five years.
Moreover, he spent the money on “agri-
cultural” railways into the Back of
Beyond and further than that, and
now they don’t pay for axle-grease let
alone interest. For a start, Wilson
brought in a freak commodities-taxatioa
scheme, which was dubiously constitutional
and undoubtedly clumsy and inequitable. He
then bumped up the income tax for the small
man, without any proportionate increase for
the bloated person. Finding, naturally, that
his poor scheme met with snorts from the
Government’s adherents as well as from the
Opposition, he made hastily for harbor with
his taxation proposals stowed away. Infor-
mal negotiations then led to a suggestion
that) a joint committee of the Assembly, repre-
sentative of all parties, should consider the
finances and devise some plan to get the
State clear of the rocksi Opposition-leader
Scaddan was not unfavorable, but the men
beliind him would have none of it. They
elected to play the game of party politics—
“ Out you go, in we come”—to the finish; and
now the Liberal Government is up against it.
Wilson has gone to the Federal head-
quarters to see whether he can raise a breeze,
and Parliament has adjourned over Christ-
mas pending the result of his mendicancy.
Whether he succeeds or not, a very Black
Wednesday seems inevitable in the civil ser-
vice before very long, and the Labor party is
obviously waiting for a regime of retrench-
ment and heavy taxation to produce an anti-
Wilson wave which will float them back on
Wrr c!L J l< ,' a ,' Slll T bfinches - So the position of
jSon, like that of every man who has to
do the cleaning-up after a loan-drunk, is
odious. Civil-service retrenchment will lose
him thousands of votes in the metropolitan
aiea, while the Country party, whose sup-
poit is essential to him, demands retrench-
ment and economy in the service instead of
increased taxation and heavier charges on
the railways. In the end, Wilson may have
to go; but whoever else attempts to clean up
the mess that Jack.' made will find trouble in
heaps.
From an Australian politician on active
service (16/10/T6) -
To-day we commenced taking the Referendum on
the Commonwealth Government’s proposal to con-
script for service overseas, and there is very strong
opposition to it. Logically, one is a little at a loss
to account for this. Asked why they intended to
vote No, the men framed replies obviously to conceal
more than they conveyed. They said, most of them,
that—well, you know, they didn’t think Australia
could spare more men without handicapping home
industries. This among other reasons less tangible.
But yarn with them, and you find that the hos-
tility is temperamental rather than logical; it is a
sort of protest against the British military system
and the restrictions imposed by what passes for dis
cipline. There is a deal of ignorance, .too, amonj
the men concerning the proposal, which practically
no attempt has been made to counteract. For in-
stance, the exemption, provisions are unknown or
imperfectly understood by many. Were they pro-
perly understood there would be less hostility.
Hughes’s manifesto, complete enough in itself, would
have been well chewed over if circulated a week or
10 days ago, and it might have cleared up much
misconception. But the thing was muddled, almost
as a matter of course, and in this big camp it was
only issued this morning, and to many of the men
not at all. Then mark what followed, because it is
so characteristic. Polling commenced at 8 a.m. and
was to have concluded at 8 p.m. ; but during the
afternoon word was received from someone in autho-
rity to discontinue the poll, pending receipt of a
message from General Bjrdwood, which was to be
delivered to the men later. The polling, it is
understood, will be continued at some future date
but whether on the basis of a wholly new poll or to
complete the interrupted one, no man knows. The
latter course will probably be pursued. When the
Fed era! Government, evading its own obvious responsi-
bilities in a period of national crisis, handed over to
Billjim this proposal for conscription, it prepared
for itself trouble it wotted not of. It has within it
all the terrors of the doctored dish or the fatal
goblet, and we who are here with the army, and who
recognise the potential evils of an adverse vote know
it.
“Beeritus”: Travelling in the Far North
of .South Aus. one is struck by the varie-
gated appearance of the aboriginal popula-
tion. Half-castes predominate, but there is
an increasing number of quarter-castes. Since
the advent of Abdul and his camel the
moral quality of the blacks’ camps has
become rotten; and the orgies indulged in
aie simply indescribable. Nearly every gang
of ’Ghans travelling with their teams tr.ke
along two or three gins, and I have seen a
Ghan with a regular harem. ' Syphilis is
rampant, and the progeny from infected gins
is in some cases absolutely revolting. Sore
eyes and ulcerous growths are .common,
while in some cases idiocy is only too
apparent in the unfortunate offspring of
these damnable misunions. In my opinion
the only remedy for this horrible condition
of affairs is a strict quarantine of the whole
of the abos., with proper medical attention,
and the removal of the infected ones to a
separate camp. Then let it be made a crimi-
nal offence for any unauthorised person, be he
black, white or brindle, to be found in any
of these camps. It is the least the Govern-
ment can decently do for the original
owners of our country, and if it has no
other effect it will at least ensure a natural
death for the few remaining members of the
once healthy tribes.
It was put this way the other day by Sydney
Telegraph -
The bank clearings continue to show a decided in-
crease in the turnover of money; the imports are
expanding in value; increases in wages continue to
be of common occurrence; and the various Govern-
ments of Australia are spending great sums of money
both from revenue and loans. Surely, such indica-
tions ought to spell prosperity.
Most surely they do nothing of the kind.
They have nothing to do with prosperity or
well-being. It is one of the common delu-
sions among men that if bank deposits show
an increase, if imports go up, or if wages
take a jump, then the country is prosperous.
As a matter of cold fact, all these things
may happen in a country verging on bank-
ruptcy. The bank transactions in Victoria
had never been so extensive, the flood of
imports never so copious .nor work and
wages so plentiful as in the times immedi-
ately preceding the discovery that there
was only a fink in the £1 on the premises.
So far as the figures relating to N. S. Wales
to-day go they are the reflex of enormous
borrowings and indications that the commer-
cial and industrial life of the State is full of
combustible gas.

“Seven Bells” : More than one brass-
bound shellback will endorse the remarks
of “Nefas” (B. 14/12/T6) on the lack of
provision in the War Pensions Act for
transport officers and men. The points made
should be driven home with a belaying-pin.
A number of Australian mercantile marine
officers, taking their first, second or third
officer’s ticket in one hand and their pro-
spects of long life and a peaceful death in
the other, have offered their services as
officers of transports—and the Government
has been mighty glad to get them. Their
compensations are the adventurous life and
the chance of prize-money when a submarine
is outed. And as every transport has to
pass through seas where one of Fritz’s tin
fish may bob up at any moment, the betting
is about even on Plutus or Pluto. This is
cold, wet fact, as the writer of some of the
best nautical and soldier verse The
Bulletin has lately printed could tell you
from personal experience. Hi's transport're-
cently sent a submarine to D. Jones's
capacious locker, and though the Admiralty
has allowed the score, he is still waiting
for his share of the prize money. Melbourne
Herald is taking up this matter of trans-
port officers’ pensions, but can get no satis-
faction beyond the official reply that each

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No one who has intelligently observed the arbitra-
tion principle in operation can, after the coal strike,
have any faith in its efficacy left.—Argus.
Brethren, let us amend our ways—bid
struggle and striving cease!
Let us return to the older things that ever
have made for peace—
To the mounted trooper and loaded six and
the fray in the crowded street,
Where the right and the wrong and the
don’femare-which in a beautiful discord
meet.
The past is dead. For a future bright
Let’s bank on the mottto that might is
" right.
Let us have national harmony—gird up our
loins and yell
For the peaceful noise of the roaring mob
@nd the clang of the curfew bell.
Cheers!
Settle the matter with dynamite!
The representative citizen should foster the
claims of class
With the clash and the rattle of broken
bricks and the clatter of falling glass,
And the Special cop on a hairy horse and the
dutiful magistrate,
While the rabble is flapping its scarlet flags
and bellowing hymns of hate.
Brethren, yearning up towards the light,
Let us abandon the fruitless fight.
Back to the baton and bunch of fives and
sensible stuff like that —
To the simple way of the man with the hoe
and the methods of fine old Fat !
Whoop !
What’s the matter with dynamite?
Blood in the gutters soon we’ll have, and the
girls from the factory
Shall tear apart in a frightful way the
first fat soul they see.
And the Truly Stout in a frenzy shall rush
around with boiling oil
To anoint, in the name of the sacred law, the
ravaging sons of toil.
Everyone earnestly holding tight
To the things he feels to be just and right,
While the pushes flock to the killing, each
with his trusty forget-me-not.
This is the land of the free, y’ know, the
country of equal— what?
Rats!
Ti e’ll settle it all with dynamite.

The Peace Proposals—and Australia.
The German may possibly have thought that
his offer to “grant peace to the world” would
be received with cheers. He may merely
have been on a fishing expedition. Or,
again, he may have aimed deliberately at
breaking up the Alliance. There is little
evidence one way or another beyond this —
that as the proposals stand they contemplate
the German pulling out a winner, and that
is the last thing in the world the Allies can
afford to let him do. Peace on that basis
would be almost as frightful as war. It
would be oply an agreement to stop fighting
until somebody felt strong enough to start
another horror. The return of the Hun to
the Pacific would make that danger ever-
present to us; and it is a contingency which
Australia should be fighting with all her
heart and soul to prevent. It is no good
fooling ourselves with the idea that the dan-
ger of this return is past. It must always
be present so long as there are unconquered
German armies in the field and German sub-
marines in the ocean. Nor is it any use
imagining that newspaper yells are going to
either win the war or decide the terms of
peace. The bayonet and the shell alone will
count, whether it be for the German or the
Allies; and the best hope Australia can have
is that the resolution of the people who are
still under the harrow of war is greater
than our own, and that their endurance will
carry them to victory. They are fighting for
the freedom of this country as surely as for
their own, and the wallop the soldiers of
France gave the German at Verdun the other
day is an encouragement to continue believ-
ing that they can finish the job if they hang
on to the brute who launched this monstrous
tragedy on the world. Incidentally, it will
do this young democracy no harm to reflect
that if others adopted the Australian atti-
tude, the restoration of the German colonies
at the back door of the Commonwealth would
be fixed up in a very few days.

There was a lot of moaning and groaning
that was mostly sham in Sydney lately about
the calamity that would overwhelm the pro-
prietary racing club if it had to put a tote
on the course. Wliat was not explained was
why the proprietary club lias been allowed
to exist. As Joe Carruthers, ex-Premier,
holds a swag of shares in some of the
most pajhiblq, one explanation might be
found without laborious search. In point
of, fact, these institutions are as superfluous
as an umbrella in drought time. They do
no good to racing or the public or horse-
breeding. All they do is to amass cash for
shareholders, give the books a chance to
do business and divert human' effort and
horse-feed towards a lot of worthless brutes
which ought to be dragging meat carts.
Sydney has at Randwick a very fine race-
course, well equipped and within easy reach.
All the racing the metropolitan area wants
could and should be done there. Instead,
the city and district are spotted with courses
and Randwick remains empty most of the
year. The position is grotesque from every
point of view, except of that happy band
of parasites who hang on to the racing
game and might be blotted out if the thing
was handled as it ought to be and the totali-
sator used for right purposes.
“8.D.” : We have reached government by
deputation now. When the Reps, had put in
most of the day on the amusement tax
Hughes asked them to pass the schedule as
it stood, because a deputation that waited
on Higgs and himself approved of it. Some-
one who had an amendment exempting six-
penny picture-show tickets said the deputa-
tion was mostly J. C. Williamson and didn’t
represent the cheap picture-show people.
“Well, pass the thing as it stands,” said
Hughes,” and I will make inquiries. If I
find the deputation wasn’t representative of
the picture-shows I will then have the
amendment made in the Senate.” As Willy
Kelly pointed out, it’s a great idea to let
the man who would have to pay the tax
say whether it should go on or not. What’s
wrong with applying the idea to the income
tax?
You don’t get cold feet by training for it.
One Melbourne ice-cream firm —Mr. S.
Meyers is the head of it —has sent 16
employees to the Front, and that exhausts
its supply of eligibles.
