—•*.
A weird and wonderful entertainment
which Miss Kate Howarde introduced to
Melbourne at the King’s on Saturday night
emphasises the fact that Australian drama
may run in all shapes. “The White Slave
Traffic” is one of ’em. The optimistic
authoress-manageress has taken the title
of her show literally, so the heroine is a
harmless, frivolous Sydney girl who tangoes
at Manly by the Sea, and there excites the
unquenchable desires of a discolored panto
prince from “the East,” clad in a crumpled
white cotton suit and attended by an Evil
Shadow of mysterious nationality, who is
said to have Ice in his Veins. These two
gentlemen plot the abduction of the artless
tango maiden to India, and then inform her
that she will henceforth be known as The
White Slave. Fortunately she has the sym-
pathy and support of the penitent Magdalen
by whom she was decoyed. The sinner in
question seems to have been formerly
married to the man with the frozen blood-
vessels, and he still holds her in thrall by
promising to restore their deceased infant to
her arms. But when she finds that her
long-lost offspring is positively dead and
buried she turns traitress to her evil em-
ployers and persuades the poor White Slave
to play at being as mad as Ophelia. There-
fore Joy Arden, the White Slave aforesaid,
trips into the Seraglio Bahat with a bundle
of lilies that “life as if asleep along her
bended arm,” and when Abru Pahlan, the
discolored trafficker, applies tests to her
sanity she merely subdues his burning pas-
sion with an Ophelianic stare. Subsequently
the evildoers learn that the lively girl from
Manly is pulling' their legs, and then the
raging Prince chases Joy around the Serag-
lio Bahat, with intentions that are strictly
dishonorable, until her brother and. her best
boy come to the rescue. A most praise-
worthy stage struggle ensues, the seraglio
becomes the scene of an all-round wrestling
tournament and the floor is strewn with
baffled profligates. A fourth and final act re-
stores triumphant virtue to the same old
Manly. as before.
The drama, apart from its giddy bit of
plot, is a tissue of flirtatious trivialities,
dotted o’er with memories of pierrot vaude-
ville. The hero (A. Milroy) is understood
to be a noble squattocrat who has lost the
family estate and become a detective. He
doesn’t deal with any other case save that
of the abducted Joy, but he is suspected of
a natural instinct for picking up clues. For
the rest of the sinless characters there are
the uneventful brother (L. Carey) and a
comic-relief party (Elton Black) who occa-
sionally sings or shares a turn with a neat
little comedienne (Olive Sinclair). The
tinted slave-catcher and his cold, forbidding
Shadow are impersonated by Louis Vernon
and Fred Neilson. Miss Enid Bowman is
the sweet-voiced penitent ■who cherishes illu-
sive hopes concerning her baby, and Miss
Adrienne plays the girl that all the trouble
is about. Her voice, by the way, is not
sweet. There are streaks of pain in her
elocution and some of her hanging ringlets
are even less real than her prevailing air of
ingenuousness. But she puts up a capital
fight for her honor during the two rounds
of applause at the finish of the third act.
“The White Slave Traffic” has come to the
King’s with the intention of staying four
weeks. Next week it will be associated with
a matinee company of children, who are to
do their little best in the way of a revue.
Melbourne Majesty’s is given over for the
moment to rehearsals of the panto, which
comes due on Saturday. The advt. promises
a quite tremendous show containing 18
scenes, six people new to Australia, several
astounding novelties and frequent refer-
ences to “The House that Jack Built.”
Even a panto, must have a name to swear
by and a central idea to lean up against.
Last year it was “Mother Goose.” This
year the curtain rises on the alleged indus-
trial achievement of Jack —doubtless a nice
tall girl whose nearest relative is a red-nosed
apparition in skirts.
“The Land of Promise,” now at Sydney
Criterion, turns out to be well-advertised
Canada. Norah Marsh, a well-bred lady’s
companion who knows how to ring for tea
and arrange flowers, is first discovered in
deep mourning in the English household
where she has eaten humble pie for 10 years
at £3O per annum. Her old female tyrant
is dead at last, and she is waiting to hear
officially from the family solicitor that she
lias been left a promised £250 a year, the
prospect of which has held her to her awtul
job. But the old employer postponed put-
ting the amiable intention in writing; so all
the estate, goes to a selfish mixed double of
relatives —the reluctant mourners at the
little funeral. Thus the pie-eater’s dreams
of freedom and foreign travel dissolve, and
she is left refusing the cheap and sneering
benevolence of the other cat in black. Left
alone at last by the winners, she is raging
tearfully to an older friend in the companion
line and talking doubtfully of her farmer
brother in Canada when the curtain falls on
a scene of stuffy respectability.
* * * * *
Act 11. finds the lady’s companion in her
brother’s Canadian shack making things hard
for herself and awkward for her hosts by
turning up her nose at everything and every-
body, and giving it an extra tilt towards her
brother’s hard-working wife —an ex-waitress
who has a temper of her own and isn’t in-
clined to have sauce passed to her in her own
house. When the final row arrives Norah
practically proposes to her'brother’s farmer-
friend (whom she cordially detests), and he
agrees to marry and take her away to his
new selection, “because women are so scarce
and I always fancied an Englishwoman.”
On that basis they agree to partner up, and
Act 111. begins with the new firm’s entry to
the unfurnished shack. The nearest neigh-
bor drives them up, wishes them luck and
leaves them alone to make tea and trouble.
Norah slaps her prairie-bridegroom’s face
when he tries to make love to her. But
when the time is ripe for another interval
the healthy, casual brute holds the lamp aloft
and the bedroom door opens for a badly-
cowed bride to pass in. In real life they
would probably fight for a year or two and
then race each other to the divorce court.
But not in modern drama.
* * * #
Act IV. is filled with conventional last-
act sunshine. The fierce bride has made the
Brute’s shack clean and bright, and a fine
field of standing wheat is visible from the
door. Tlie Brute moons about apparently full
of gentlemanly remorse, and Norah’s brother
comes to call and report that a baby is
expected presently at his place. With him
is a young Londoner who is retreating to
the Strand at the pater’s expense to comfort
his Canadian-ravaged soul with a “slap-up”
dinner at the Ritz. Little Sister, happy as
a bird, welcomes both and sits down to hear
the news beside the table she has innocently
decorated with a deadly weed that is one of
the worst pests the Canadian farmer knows.
Big Brother has a letter for her from a Lon-
don lady who has a vacant dog-washing job
at £35 a year in a refined household, and both
he and the husband are amazed in turn when
she sneei-s at the offer. When it dawns
on her that the weed-infested crop will have
to be burnt and her husband go out odd-
jobbing for a living until next sowing, she
finally decides that she would rather go with
him than to the English dogs, and they are as
genuine a married couple as any employ-
ment agency could wish for when the
dramatic end comes.
Florence Rockwell, as Norah Marsh, the
salaried slave of female tyrants, gives some
very natural displays of bad temper ; in the
sugary last act she is less happy, artistically.
Consistent Eily Malyon puts one more fine
study to her credit as the shrewish waitress-
wife tormented in her own home by the
suspicion of condescension. Maggie Knight,
the worried old farmer’s wife, is welcome as
the only good-tempered female in the cast.
Beatrice Holloway makes a brief first-act
appearance as the detestable she-mourner
who exults over the disappointed companion
at the will-reading. Arthur Greenaway
seems natural as the she-mourner’s tamed
city husband ; so does Arthur Styan, as Big
Brother. Frank Harvey plays the clean-
shaved Canadian woman-tamer in gentle-
manly style and drops an occasional “h”
with cultured care, and Hugh Huntley
squeaks amusingly as the well-brushed young
Londoner. An interesting comedy very
fairly done.
It being the J.C.W. tradition that Mel-
bourne must get the first slice of panto.,
“The Cinema Star” will start to heliograph.
a Christmas message to the front stalls of
Sydney Majesty’s on Saturday. With, luck

ing in the air that tlie dachshund has got his
tail down at last and that the German eagle
is learning arithmetic to find out how many

With her bundles and her dog Sara All-
good intends to do the goodwill-on-earth act

ney), and Saturday will see her commence

The Fuller pantomime at Sydney Opera
House this Christmas promises to be Aus-
tralian from heel to hairpad. There will not
be a single musical number that isn't the
work of an composer, and the
Australian authoress of “The Bunyip” will
direct the orchestra. Every scene is to be
Australian, ranging from the Jenolan Caves
to a gigantic corroboree with boomerangs
that hurtle over the Australian audience’s
heads and return to the scenes painted by
Australian hands. The producer is Aus-
tralian, and large quantities of Australian
girls will wear Australian-made dresses
which have been hand-painted by a Mel-
bourne-born artist. The Australian special-
ties will include something warm in the way
of big bushfires, and there will be a proces-
sion of the people who hav“ made Australia,
not forgetting the Sentimental Bloke and
his Doreen. .
“The Passing Show” (1916-17 edition-) i§
keeping Sydney Tivoli from looking like
Goldsmith’s Deserted Village. Sydney James
and Billy of the hinged mouth are the chief
performers, but dancing Madeline Rossiter
and cheery Gwen Lewis help with the good
work. One of the best turns on the list is
that of Degnon and Clifton, the quiet, casual
hand-balancers; but the light partner
shouldn’t cluck like a sick hen.

The Dandies’ programme at the Garden
Theatre (Sydney) has been freshened by
Flockton Foster and Ada Bellfield’s Scottish
and Jewish turns. Nobody before has had
the temerity to bracket the original brother-
races. Yet Auld Lang Syne is merely the
three brass balls.

providing some splendid glimpses of the Somme

On Saturday the eo. reverts to Shakspeare.
Wilkie’s much-talked-of "Richard III.” will

“Damaged Goods,” at Melbourne Royal,
continues to afford cynics the entertain-
ment of watching Henriette’s pa when
he discovers what his son-in-law has
brought into the family. The old man
makes a great show of righteous anger
and threatens to massacre the lad at
the earliest moment, though he casually con-
fesses under pressure that he has been no
better than his intended victim, only a bit
luckier. The ancient’s attitude is horribly
middle-classy, and demonstrates once more
that the greatest champions of virtue are
disused rakes, if it is reasonably safe to
attempt the hypocrisy. This incident in
“Damaged Goods” contains a line moral for
heavy fathers, and the author makes it so
plain that only tin' deaf can miss it.
The Follies, reappearing at Melbourne
Tivoli, are repeating their old-established
mannerisms with the help of fresh scenery
and truckloads of new clothes. Cannot
heads the disturbance as of old, and is the
same old asylum for lone and friendless
jokes. Weems’ ridiculous boots and Dark-
town countenance continue to be separated
by live feet of shapeless attire. Thelma
Kaye and Vera Pearce are still working
their dresses to exhaustion, while Welch’s
insobriety remains one of the distressing
ceremonials which the Follies apparently
cannot do without. The only new face is
that of “Florie,” a Noo Yark lady, who in
attempting to warble to young bloods in the
stalls shows that she cannot sing for Nuts.
Of the three-dozen items on the programme,
excluding four songs by Miss Sen-Mei, the
dances of the Yateses and little Jean Keith
are entirely satisfactory. The other
“specialties” amount to second-class vaude-
ville stunts requiring first-class personalities
to make them go.
The Dandies continue to get their share
of attention at St. Kilda (Vic.). Recent
moist weather somewhat affected attend-
ances, but a welcome jump in the tempera-
ture has caused a fresh influx of customers,
with the result that the cashbox is beginning
to look its old self again.
The combative energy of William Farnum
is the most-discussed subject among Hoyt’s
Melbourne followers this week. Bill is a
stousliing parson aching for punishment
in five reels of excitement called “Fighting-
Blood.” This film is exclusively screened at
Hoyt’s Bourke-street theatre, where “The
Wheel of the Law” is also in motion. At
Hoyt’s ©lyjnpia the last-mentioned item is
the cheese, an adaptation of Locke’s
“Jaffery” being the meat on the menu.
Showman E. J. Carroll has caught the pre.
vailing frenzy for social reform and is now
screening “Where Are My Children?” to
prove it. The picture, which is on view
at Melbourne Auditorium, professes to re-
veal how the hand that robs the cradle rules
the fashionable world. A swell abortionist
is the villain of the piece, and the hero is a
stern lawyer and amateur eugenist who sools
the Lor on to the malpractitioner, only to
discover that his own wife is the Doctor’s
favorite client. The film finishes with the
attorney and his missus facing one another
in a childless desert; but it also throws in
some scraps of Eternity with dim vistas of
the Unborn, apparently to further emphasise
the moral that a busy bassinet is the basis
of true happiness. The Rev. Carroll’s ser-
mon is to be delivered four times daily, and
the usual collection will be taken up at the
box office.
Adelaide shows are much as before, but
the King’s, long closed,, since the Fuller cir-
cuit moved into the bigger Majestic, is turn-
ing over in its sleep, and talking of what it
will do for home-made Australian drama
when it wakes at Christmas. Herbert
Myers, of the Royal, was Chief Marshal of
a pageant on Repatriation Day ; and school-
children so delighted a packed Exhibition
with a nursery-rhymes display that the
Premier says it must be repeated.
A cutting from a shrub at the grave of
Adam Lindsay Gordon, planted at Mount
Gambier (S.A.), where he lived some years,
failed to. grow, so another, a wattle, has been
got from the same spot and is doing well.
George Riddoch, a local squatter, has put up
a tablet alongside, and in unveiling it recalled
his close friendship with the poet. “A man
not easily approached,” said Riddoch. “A
sort of cold manner. But when yon got to
know him you found him a man of wonder-
ful capacity and with great consideration
for others.”
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Stargazer” : Houghton’s comedy, “Ilindle
Wakes,” produced at Melbourne King’s the
other night, is in many respects an ideal
Repertory play, but its Melbourne produc-
tion was ignored by the repertory crowd,
and the play was greeted by a typical King’s
Theatre audience, accustomed to melodra-
matic sentiment .and comic horseplay, with
the result that the entertainment was woefully
marred by unreasonable sniggering and
uncalled-for vociferation. As a battler for
the repertory idea of 20 years’ standing, I
begin to see only now how far-reaching the.
reform must be before we can attain happy
Satisfaction. The peculiar building is essen-
tial, the peculiar company is necessary, of
course, and then comes the task of securing
the peculiar audience. Here I am stuck.
How can we so draft the people as to be sure
of _ guaranteeeing the presence of only those
spiritually and intellectually adapted to enter
into the atmosphere of the excellent play?
“Vic”: “Ilindle Wakes * rings true. I
saw the big scene done in Melbourne Police
Court years ago. A girl sued her child’s
father for maintenance. He said he would
marry her. Bench: “Oh. that’s all right.
Let him marry the girl.” Girl (passion-
ately) : “It’s not all right! I will never
marry the man who deceived me. I only
want him to pay for his child !” The Bench
sat aghast at her fiery independence and
commonsense. But the seducer didn’t get
her.
Comedian Alfred Frith, the comic life-
saver of. “So Long, Letty,” who was re-
ported to have enlisted, now explains that
he is “the only surviving male relative” of
his family and—“l never actually enlisted.
It was’only a conditional enlistment. All
I did was to pass the doctor and that sort
ot thing.” In short, attended a kind of dress
rehearsal, and then, found, apparently, that
it wasn’t a play at all.
"Amos Kito”: Bitter comments have been
made about the gagging by Fred Patev, the
Moses of Allan Wilkie’s production of "The
School for Scandal.” But practically all
actors cast for Moses count on the license
to gag, and stage-managers encourage them.
By the way, the pomedie Franqaise (I’m
glad I’m writing it, not pronouncing it),
which is a sort of repository of the nation’s
classics, permits gagging. If I purchase
one of the comedies —say Moliere’s—with
stage directions, from the Comedie Frau-
Caise, I get the latest and best gags of the
comedians added to the original lines with-
out any extra charge.
“Mujik”: These educational films are,
. after all, the most delightful phase of the
picture business. A film, now showing in
Sydney gives _ the democracy an hour’s in-
struction in lingerie. The film depends for
its pull on the nightie and the undie, and
the other night it was pulling like a dentist.
Concealed in a plush-cover seat with a
packet of chewing-gum, the maid who pines
for the silk camisole and the lace-trimmed
necessary-commodity may see the wonders
produced by white-workers—can see them
hung up, and on, and round-about. Also
she can dream of how “lovely” it would
be to be inside them until it was necessary
to climb into something more gorgeous.
These exhibitions of needlework and going
to bed and getting up seem incomplete with-
out a few bathroom scenes; but that is of
course merely a critical commentary upon
the imagination of the producer.

“Adelaide”: If ever there was a case of
the office seeking a man, it is the appoint-
ment of “Sammy” Lunn as spruiker at the
new Grand picture-theatre here. Mr. Lunn
would at any time rather talk than eat. He has
collected literally hundreds of pounds by pass-
:”,r round the hat for patriotic funds at pub-
lic gatherings. He talks in verse (or worse)
<*•s icMuiy as in prose. And now ho is paid to
stand at the door of the Grand and invite
inside you and your wife, the joy of your
life, your son and your charming daughter,
and the man who is going to court her; and
a delighted crown hangs round the whole
time. It is an ideal appointment.
“Kaniva”: “J.F.H.” (B.’ 7/12/TO) asks
what women Hamlets have been seen in
Melbourne. Miss Cleveland played the part
finely at the Theatre Royal in 1867 ; Mrs.
G. B. W. Lewis played it at the Bijou
Theatre; Rose Evans did it at the Royal,
and hers was excellent; Louise Pomeroy
was very successful at the Theatre Royal in
1881; and Louise Crawford was the last.
“J.F.H.”: The bad example of Australian
bishops who constantly cadge in London for
charitable contributions is infectious. Fritz
Hart has now issued an appeal for the
library in connection with the Melbourne
Music Club. Indifferent to the fact that
British composers get their bread by the sale
of their works, Hart audaciously makes a
shameless appeal to them to send him copies
of their “songs, instrumental pieces,' cham-
ber music, orchestral and choral works—in
short, music of every description.” By way
of stimulating British musical composers
into presenting free copies of their works,
he assures them that Melba is “taking a
warm interest in the scheme.” Which merely
makes it more shocking for Australia to
sponge on London at a time when London is
paying over Bs. a bushel for Australia’s
wheat.

“Ancient Prompter” : Theatrical managers
in Fogland who tour the provinces, startling
the natives of country towns by billing
their plays “like a circus,” are bidden to
economise in that direction, too. “All resi-
dent and touring proprietors shall take steps
with a view to limitation of the excessive
printing used,” it is ordered. In short,
less paper on the walls and more in the
house.

Some interesting revelations were very
nearly made the other afternoon in the Vic.
Legislative Assembly, but the cat was pushed
back into the bag and drowned, as it always
is in such cases. Amendments to the
Venereal Diseases Bill were being consid-
ered, when Billson jumped up to say that
he regarded it as the Ministry’s sacred duty
to stamp out disorderly houses. There were
wealthy men who were receiving rentals
from Flossie-flais 100 per cent, higher than
would be collected from respectable tenants.
Whereupon Bayles interjected: “That’s not
fair, and not a fact either.” Billson turned
dramatically ( on the interjector. “If Mi*.
Bayles dared' him,” said he, “he would read
the names.” But the names were not read,
and although another member asked why
they should .be suppressed he was not
answered.
The Martins of Gawler (S.A.) had a
great name for nearlv half a century. Their
engineering works made locomotives and
mine-machinery for all parts of the conti-
nent, and a statue of, James, the founder of
the business, stands in the main street of the
town. His nephew and right-hand man,
John Martin, carried on the business for a
while after his uncle’s death. But mining
business fell off, and the Government found
it could make its own locomotives at Isling
ton workshops. So the co. passed into liquida-
tion ; and two Martin sons, though
thoroughly trained for carrying the works
on, went farming in Westralia. There John
Martin joined them, and has just died, a
disappointed man, at 72.
“Boswell”: The acquittal of W. W. Sie-
benhaar, Deputy Registrar-General of West-
ralia, on a charge of disloyalty moves me to
pray that no other writer may be tried 17
years hence for what he may be scribbling
to-day. That is what happened to Sieben-
liaar. lie was suspended by F. D. North,
Bigjolin Forrest’s brother-in-law, mainly
because he collected a few bob for the de-
fence of poor old Monty Miller (aged 85).
But this “charge” being too shadowy alto-
gether, the zealots cannoned off on to
“Dorothea,” Siebenhaar’s book of verses, and
made his rhymed sentiments on the Boer
War the basic motif. A detective was put
in the box to interpret “disloyalty” in 1916
by a book written in 1899, and it was re-
freshing to hear this “expert,” under Sieben-
haar’s thrusts, admit that history was a
sealed tomb to him. The accused was able
to 'show that both Lloyd George and G. F.
Pearce helds views identical with “Doro-
thea” at the time the verses were written,
and that North himself had supplied an
appreciation of “Dorothea” in 1909. The
prosecution gave it up then.
We don’t, as a rule, admit our age, but
Mrs. Jane Cocking, of Camberwell, having
been presented to Queen Vic. when her Gra-
cious was a young princess, and having voted
“Yes” at the Referendum, rises superior to
such foibles. When she was 99 she was
thrown out of a buggy and broke an arm and
a leg, but is now not much the worse for it.
Her father, Captain Gransden, commanded
the frigate Belle Poule in Nelson’s day; and
that’s how she came to curtsey to Victoria,
then, a girl of 17, when the princess visited
Woolwich attended by the* Iron Duke, Lord
Bloomfield and the Duke of Northumber-
land. Later she watched the coronation
procession, holding on to an officer’s sword
belt.
Brisbane City Council nearly choked with
righteous indignation the other day when it
discovered that a parson to whom it had
issued a permit to lecture in a movie hall
on the Sabbath had trampled on Bumble
law and sinfully screened a film. Bumble
threatens to go at the next offender with
the steam-roller. And that is more than
Bumble does when you ask him to smooth
the lump iu the middle of the highway.
ART IS LONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Upon the stage to find a place
Was Gubbin’s fond ambition,
He quite believed his style and grace
Would there gain recognition,
For once he’d, won a pencil-case
At Cow Flat Competition.
lie nightly bellowed “Denver’s Dream,”
With frenzied declamation,
And murdered “Lasca” in a stream
Of turgid recitation,
Until he heard tiie neighbors scream
Their wild appreciation.
At Cow Flat “evenings" he displayed
His talents, to perfection,
When he charged with the Light Brigade,
The pearl of his collection,
The jolliest people were dismayed,
And sobbed in deep dejection.
From Cow Flat down to Lrg-rope Creek,
11 is fame was wide extended,
And even out at Poddy Peak
His efforts were commended—
’Twas there he did “The Maniac’s Shriek” ;
The cockies thought, it splendid.
And when De Verc’s Dramatic Co. —
“Fast Lynne” the chaste attraction—-
Presented its unrivalled show
To Cow Flat’s stupefaction,
The morbid Dubbin felt a glow
Of gloomy satisfaction.
He sought the eminent De Vere,
And breathed his secret yearning;
De Vere said at the sixteenth beer
(A keen man, and discerning),
“For thirty quid I’ll star you here
Next month when I’m returning.
“We’ll give ’em ‘Hamlet’ in Cow Flat,
Unparalleled Sensation!
Dubbin the Moody Dane! Why, that
Will crown your reputation!”
Ecstatic Giibbin beamed and sat
In speechless exultation.
He wrote a cheque with eager pen;
De Vere was much affected,
And swore that Gubbin of all men
Was one he most respected.
Next morning ht the bank at ten
The cold cash he collected.
And Gubbin duly got his “part,”
With most minute instruction
In how to get it off by heart
In time for the production,
Which to the soulful realms of art
Would be his proud induction.
And so De Vere passed on his way,
Whilst Gubbin stormed and spouted,
And tortured “Hamlet” night and day;
And as he raved and shouted,
His sympathetic friends would say,
“This actin’s got ’im outed!”
The sanguine Gubbin still avows
That cruel fate prevented
De Vere from keeping solemn vows
With beer and art cemented;
He babbles Shakspeare to the cows,
And seems to be contented.
QUEENSLAND.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.

SOCIETY
“S. Pallida”: Anyhow, it is a fact that
since Brieux’s “Damaged Goods” was staged
in Melbourne Collins-street doctors have
had to deal with an increased number of
people who want to know whether they are
free of what most of the outwardly-nice
papers still call “the Red Plague.” Inci-
dentally, one grim local drama might have
furnished a plot for De Maupassant. An
engaged girl, with commonsense unusual in
one of her age and stage, asked her fiance
to undergo a medical examination and fur-
nish a clean bill of health, agreeing to do
likewise herself. The young man was no
prude, and he filled his part of the con-
tract with success. To the girl’s horror,
however, the blood test in her own case
gave a positive reaction. There is no reason
to suppose that the taint was other than
hereditary ; but the orange blossoms turned
to Dead Sea fruit, none the less.
* * * *
From a Light Horse man:—
They can say what they like about Australian dis-
cipline, but it will do me —and I’m fussy. We
charged right into sheets of machine and rifle fire
one day, and were so close up to glory that the
Turks were grabbing ai the bridles of the troop. It
was wheel and go then —all in terribly heavy sand.
I simply had to sing out: “Now, boys,"don’t kill your
horses!” At once they slowed up just as if they
were on the parade-ground! I tell you I was proud
to be leading them. They do .the most extraordinary
things. One of them who was hit, and hit badly,
was trying to catch a riderless horse in the middle
of hell when his own lot came along. His mates
carried him for miles across sand on a blanket
wrapped round a couple of rifles. Do it! They’d
do anything for one another. I have to grin when I
remember the pause before setting out on a tough
job, and my little effort at encouraging oratory:
“Now, then, C Troop, make a name for yourselves!”
And the plain, direct Australian reply from a large,
unshaven corporal: “You give us a blanky lead, and .
we’ll blanky well foller yer!”
* * * *
“Hen”: My military friend “Hr” (B.
7/12/T6) is out of step when he say that
“crucifixion” as a punishment is unknown
to Australian Army Regulations. In the
army it is usually referred to as “spread-
eagle,” and is part of the treatment awarded
to a man given No. 1 Field Punishment.
This latter sentence is rather rare in the
Australian army, perhaps because the
officers do not approve of it. But I Know of
one case -where it was ordered and carried
out. The treatment must not last more than
a specified time at a stretch, and the full
dose is of course limited.
* * * *
“The Old Soldier” is emphatic:—
“Crucifixion” is not a British army punishment
(B. 7/12/’IC). A search through the “King’s
Regulations” will show that no form of torture can
be lawfully inflicted on a soldier since'the “cat” was
abolished. Company and regimental officers can sen-
tence to extra drills, certain extra duties, fines and
forfeiture of pay for absence; but nothing in the
nature of physical torture can be ordered. Any
soldier in the British army would instantly insist on
his right to be taken “in front of the General” if
he were “crucified” or treated in any other unusual
or unauthorised manner, no matter of what offence
he was accused.
What are the facts? Half a dozen appar-
ently competent people write assurances that
the punishment of “crucifixion” or “spread-
eagling” is known to the Australian army,
as well as to the British. And the “Old
Soldier,” who has spent 70 years filling him-
self with army lore, is just as sure .that it
isn’t.
• * * *
“S.A.” : I was lying in hospital in Valetta
(Malta), fervently wishing for something to
fracture the monotony, when there entered
our ward the daughter of Governor Methuen
and a small boy with a vast heap of parcels
and printed matter. She reached my bed.
“And would you like to read these, my
good man?” she asked, handing me a Sexton
Blake detective story and Comic Cuts. I
said .1 wasn’t anxious, exactly. She pon-
dered, and then from amongst the parcels
she produced a substantial-looking one,
triumphantly dumped it on my bed and
sailed majestically on. The ward sur-
rounded me as with eager expectation I
undid the wrappings. It was a set of picture
blocks! She might have known that marbles
and tops were my favorites.

Should a jury ask questions? The point .
was discussed in the S. Aus. Criminal Court
when the usual girl under 16 had made the
usual shocking charges against a constable of
hitherto good reputation. When the evidence
on both sides had closed, a juryman sug-
gested that they (or he) might want more ;
if they could have it, might they retire and
consider what evidence they would like? It
took an informal conference of Judges to
tell him that, if he meant fresh witnesses, he
(or they) couldn’t; a jury had to decide on
the evidence put before it, and to go beyond
would be making it the prosecutor. But that
seems arguable, to put it mildly. It might
be making the jury the defender. Even the
Court didn’t seem to have much of an opinion
of the informal conference’s dictum, and
went very near to allowing the disputed
point when, at the request of the same bat-
tler, it let the jurors retire to consider what
further questions they would like to put to
witnesses who had already been called.
After taking an hour to decide, they had the
accused and his wife and the girl and a
doctor all in the box again. And then the
case fizzled out, the Judge advising that they
would not be safe in believing what the girl
had said.
“Beckom” : Our chaps raided a trench a
couple of nights ago, but all except one of
the Germans had either flown or been killed.
The solitary survivor met the party with
cries of “Nein, nein, kamerad!” “Nine be
” said the front Billjim. “It’s 10, and
you’re out.” And he was.

The sins of the- father are visited on the
old woman —by the Defence Department. At
any rate, Mathews, M.H.R., alleges that the
Department deducts from the wives’ allow-
ances the fines inflicted on the absent hus-
bands. The prayer in many a warrior’s
household now contains an additional clause :
“And please keep father from being crimed.
Amen.”

That was a dangerous experiment the
Brisbane passengers by the inter-State
steamer Canberra made when they under-
took to serve their own meals and make
their own bpnks, the stewards having
struck. It was recently stated in the Arbi-
tration Court that these servants are still
supposed to exist partly on tips, the various
rich steamship cos. preferring to throw some
of the burden on their customers rather
than pay their employees a decent wage.
After the successful Canberra experiment,
coastal travellers may expect to find the
practice carried a step further, passengers
being required to wait on themselves when
the first fierce blast of war-economy strikes
the fleets. The willing horse always gets it.
Again the resource of Billjim lias brought
him into conflict with the Army Act, the
Articles of War and Routine Orders. Bill
soon ascertained that his steel helmet was
useful in the preparation of tucker. Fol-
lowed this portentous notification :
Troops are reminded that steel helmets are issued
as a protection against shrapnel, and are to be used
only for that purpose, and not as a medium for
making porridge or stew.
There was an Australian artillery driver
once who used his “tin hat” to carry away
a setting of eggs. But that is. anothe:
Furphy, as Kipling would have said had In
thought of it.
* * * *
Too much rain in N. S. Wales has pro
duced the worse season for rust in wheat for
30 years, and in addition hail and storms
have knocked crops down wholesale. What
is worse, unsuitable weather continues, and
in many parts cocky can only perch on a
kerosene-box in the stable and curse fluently
while he watches the ceaseless rain, so often
longed for, but just now an unmitigated
curse. It is impossible to take the strippers
or harvesters into the paddocks and work
them. There are many out in the rain hope-
lessly bogged. Consequently men in the trade
are figuring that the yield, one time ex-
pected to be 60,000,000 bushels, will not be
more than 20 or 25 millions. Anything
like a two- or three-bag crop, six to nine
bushels, means that the sowing of the
next crop will be a tough problem. The
one bright spot in the gloomy outlook is
the sale not only of the current year’s
harvest, but also the large part of last
year’s that is left, to the British Govern-
ment at 4s. 9d. per bushel, f.o.b. Sydney,
delivery not later than June next. In the
circumstances it isn’t any too much for the
poor crop that will be harvested this year;
but it will mean a shilling a bushel or there-
abouts more than cocky had lately expected
for the balance of his 1915 bumper harvest.
A CHRISTMAS CAROL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Christians, awake ! Salute the happy morn !
(The night was quiet save for shot and shell.)
To-day on earth the Prince of Peace is born !
(Now, coves, it’s up to us to give ’em hell!)
Rise to adore the mystery of love !
(The cows shoot straight. Bill got it in the head.)
The Star of Bethlehem is bright above!
(We counted sixteen hundred German dead.)
Our herald angels with their pilots pass;
A rush of shrapnel screams along the sky....
Praise God, from whom all blessings flow —for gas!
It’s Christmas.... and we’ve only once to die.
Christians, awake! Salute the happy morn
With gifts of murder for a whole brigade!
If peace on earth is ever to be born,
It follows up the bursting hand-grenade !
N.s.w.
MARY McC.
A VACANCY FOR A GENTLEMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ACCORDING TO THE LANDLADY:
The cream of all things was his one request;
Living at leisure, sumptuously arrayed,
He would have nothing but the very best;
Price was no object, for he never paid.
ACCORDING TO THE LODGER:
Cash for a bed that shuddered sleep away!
Money for meals that flouted appetite!
Seeing how much she took me in by day,
I took myself (and luggage) out by night.

“Haka” : Add this chapter to the Book of
Coincidences. Two Maoiilanders, Bob Frost
and Joe Lockwood, were running a contract-
ing business at Northern Wairoa. Frost
was on the road to Whangarei when his
horse fell, and lie pulled-in at the hospital
with a broken leg. Later, Lockwood was
doing the trip to Whangarei on the same
nag, when it fell at the same place, and lie
arrived at the hospital with a duplicate of
Frost’s accident. They were both attended
by the same nurse and the same doc. One
enlisted with the 3rd Reinforcements, the
other with the sth. Both were hit, and met
on the boat coming back to Maoriland. I
shouldn’t have bothered mentioning these
details but for the fact that in a recent land
ballot Lockwood and Frost drew adjoining-
sections.

The leading episodes of a year in the
life of a fishmonger :
Three trawlers imported by the Government of
N. S. Wales.
P.N.’s exported to pay for them.
Holman orates about cheap fish for democrats.
George Black appointed Minister for Fish.
George has his photo, taken opening an oyster.
Trawlers commence to catch fish.
Ministry gives loud cheers, appoints numerous
officials, opens shops, and sends for the reporters.
Strike on trawlers.
, G. Black outlines vast scheme of fish distribution
by fleet of ice-waggons.
Strike fixed up.
Auditor-General yells that accounts are in an
awful muddle.
Strike on trawlers.
Discovery that taxpayers are losing £2OO a week
cn the hsu business.
Strike settled.
Loud noise about the advantages of a fish diet for
the multitude.
Captains, mates, engineers, firemen and crews of
trawlers cease work.
Trawlers tied up for another rest.
Auditor-General sends for the documents.

vessels as the Railway chiefs do, he would
find himself in holts with the police. The

“Hassan”: “Adelaide” (B. 7/12/Y6)
speaks about capital punishment for rape.
In the course of his argument, with which,
in the main, one must agree, he classes rape
as a lesser crime than murder. With this
I cannot agree. De Quincey says that mur-
der, in some instances, is a fine art. I
don’t go so far as that; yet murder may
be a most meritorious action in certain
circumstances, and rape never is. The
murder of a politician is a mere peccadillo
when one considers the
vast amount of good it
may do. It would be a
venial offence to murder
some actors. The Aus-
tralian who says “Sure,
kid!” and “Some girl,
believe me!” is better
dead. On the other
hand, no woman is
bettei* for having been
outraged. It is a more
cowardly and less ex-
cusable crime th a n
murder, apart from its
consequences. One of
our greatest objections
to the German is the
frequency with which he
committed the crime in
the countries he devas-
tated. Cold-blooded mur-
derers should be hanged.
And in bad cases of rape
(for there are degrees;
sometimes the “rape”
charge arises from the
girl’s conscience) the
rope is as good as any
other method. An ex-
cellent system of meet-
ing the natural reluct-
ance to hang a man on
the word of a hysterical
flapper would be that
adopted in America,
where a juty can bring
in a verdict of murder
in the first or second
degree. “Rape in the
first degree” would fit
the hempen collar round the outrager of
little children and young girls, while “rape-
in the 17th degree” would lead to a paternal
admonition from the Judge that “you two
foolish young people should get married.”
The trouble is that the law lacks elasticity.
A delightful bit of evidence was given in
a Melbourne suburban court case the other
day. Sikes was accused of battering his
wife with a frying-pan, and Mrs. Smith, from
next door, was in the witness-box, giving her
version of the recent career of the happy
couple. “Once he ’it ’er over the ’ead with
his shovel,” she said. “Another time I seen
him kick ’er insenseless. Last time he took
his. ’ands an’ feet to her proper; but when
he saw ’er next mornin’ with her fice all cut
up an’ her nose broke, he forgive her every-
think.”

The hand of Mars has been laid heavily
upon the black coats in Maori land. The
first ballot 'under the Military Service Act
resulted in 14 parsons, two Starvation Army
officers, a home missionary and a travelling
evangelist being called up. There are no
statutory exemptions, but Defence Minister
Allen has stated that bishops and heads
of denominations will be permitted to apply,
as though they were employers, for exemp
tion for ministers under their control.

lipoli have been the. keenest in protesting

Harding Davis. Each refers to Gallipoli
in passing—Davis was just across the way,
at Salonika-—and it does not occur to
either of them to mention the Australians
at all.

THE RAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A terrific storm flooded the trenches yesterday.
Some of the raindrops seemed as big as half a
crown.— Soldier’s letter.
The rain it falls, from west to east,
Where grim War makes its vulture feast;
In drops as large as half a crown,
Incessantly the rain comes down.
But greater things than rain will fall
Where battle holds its carnival;
As shrieks from brothers slain arise,
Rebellion shines in many eyes,
And fnutters, in a vengeful breath,
’Gainst thrones that build their power on death,
Intent to see, when chaos frowns,
The fall of things as large as crowns!
The mists of rain are blown and torn;
A spirit old, but newly born,
Breathes through war’s shrouding coverings
And threats the sway of Warring kings."
Man, driven forth by State and throne
To fight in battles not his own,
Sees, surely rising, after all,
His triumph come. The reigns they fall.
N.S.W.

Grey Hair —Home

An old-fashioned, home-made recipe restores

There are plenty of reasons why grey hau-
ls not desirable and plenty of reasons why
hair dyes should not be used. But, on the
other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want it.
To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has oniy
to get from -the chemist two ounces of con-
centrate of tammalite and mix it with three
ounces of bav rum. Apply to the haii with
a small sponge for a few nights and the
greyness will gradually disappear. xtus
liquid is not sticky or greasy and does not
injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the for-
mula.— Advt.

The first ballot for men under the Maori-
land Military Service Act sprang some sur-
prises. In Christchurch the president of the
Conscription Repeal League was drawn first
pop. A telegraph-operator on the Dunedin
line, who was, taking the list at a furious
rate, had the privilege of hearing one string
of dot-dashes spell out his own moniker. And
a cocky who recently told the Red Cross
ambulance collectors that lie was unable to
contribute as he had just expended £21,000
on a property for his son, found that young
man bagged.
Tommy Walker, formerly M.L.A. for
Northumberland in the N. S. Whiles Parlia-
ment, lately received the King’s permission
to be Honorable for the remainder of his life.
This is a reward for services as Attorney-
General of Westralia. Sixteen years ago it
would have sounded like a cruel joke.
Tommy, down and out, was just then being-
helped' to the West by his old political
friends— -more to get rid of him than any-
thing else. ■ Ilis recovery, followed by. his
study for and admission to the Bar when he
was already middle-aged, is one of the most
creditable performances .any man could put
up.
Frederick Goldsmith, who has worn the
Anglican gaiters at Bunbury (W.A.) since
1904, has given notice that he will take
them off next February. Increasing deaf-
ness and sciatic twinges make him realise
that he is not as young as he used to be, and
he reckons it a Mir thing to make way for
a shepherd who can round up the stragglers.
Goldsmith came from the Cold Country in
ISBB as Dean of Perth (W.A.), and started
bishing 16 years later, when Bunbury diocese
was formed. He is one of the four Anglican
bishops in the Commonwealth who are game
to wear their mitres in public, the others
being Trower, of North-West Australia (his
probable successor), Feetham. of N.Q., and
Halford, of Rockhampton. The rest use
their mitres only on their notepaper and
their spoons. Goldsmith is a better Aus-
tralian than many native-born, and for over
a quarter of a century has plugged away
trying to persuade Australian Anglicans that
their church has grown up now and should
have its own name and manage its own
affairs, instead of calling itself a foreign
institution (“the Church of England in _Aus-
tralia”) and hanging on to the Archbishop
of Canterbury’s apron strings. The demo-
cratic and “High Church” States —W.A., Q.
and S.A. —support him, and Vic. and Tas.
look kindly at his scheme; but N.S.W. is
the nark State, as usual, and has so far
blocked the change on the ground that Aus-
tralians are too ignorant and shiftless to
manage their own religious affairs, and that
to stand alone would be “disloyal ”to Mother
England.
John Farrell, of White Cliffs, is the
biographer:—
Last Monday was marked by the decease at Yancannia
station (N.SAV.) of the oldest aboriginal—los was
her reputed age, but as there was no bonus in her
time her mother was careless about registering the
proof. She was known as Granny Mullins ; her abo.
name sounded something like Murra-gurra-wurra.
She remembered the first white man to die in this
district —Poole, with Sturt’s expedition, 1844-5 —and
was present when he was buried. She then had a
piccaninny as big as herself. Assuming that she was
30 then, it would put her past the century. She
looked it. She had a thick grey beard covering the
greater part of her. face, and when allowed to grow
it was of the Dundreary pattern. She never
learned English, but was fluent in abo. Blind for
the past three years, she never lost her spirits, and
enjoyed a joke till the last sundown. Her appetite
never forsook her either, and Yancannia station saw
that she never wanted her one meal a day, which
lasted from morning till night.
Laurie Abrahams, tobacco-manufacturer
and many other things, who passed out in
Melbourne the other day after much
unmerited suffering, was the kindliest friend
the Melbourne artist has had. He was not
only a lavish buyer; he was always ready to
give liberal help to any art object that
chanced along. Often the object manifested
small gratitude after the first burst of
thanksgiving. He went so far as to indulge
several Melbourne artists in European trips
for the benefit of their education —in a few
instances with very satisfactory results to
the artists. One of these Abrahams met at a
picture function on the occasion of a return
trip. The painter was very, very cold. The
patron bore this pleasantly, but eventually
trapped the artist in a corner, and whis-
pered : “Do not be under a misapprehension,
.Chrome, old fellow. Even if you are polite
to me, I shall not expect you to pay me
back.”

bourne (Collins-street) Independent'Church.

Richards, who succeeded £. £. Bevan. The

Bucks Infantry.. Kite was a Tasmanian,

WOW IT’S DONE.
THE MAIDEN: “Just to think, twice Lord
Mayor! Honwever did you manage it?”
THE MEAGHER: “Well, when I was young I
read about ‘Turn Again’ Whittington,
thrice Lord Mayor of London, and so
I just keep on turning."
A Svdney man who figured in the first
verse of the Gallipoli epic got back last week
on a hospital ship with two stripes on his
arm. He is E. A. Roberts, stretcher-bearer
of “the 2nd. Two days after the Landing
someone noticed that a few of the huddled
figures on the beach were moving, and succes-
sive pairs of stretcher-bearers tried to rescue
them with the help of the guns of the fleet.
But Turkish snipers kept dropping the
rescuers, until Roberts and Stan Carpenter,
of Newcastle (N.S.W.), got to work and
brought in five wounded men to the dressing-
station. The Anzacs reckoned it was worth
a D.C.M. apiece; but the decorations neyer
arrived. Carpenter later won and got his at
Pozieres.
A draper, a Methodist and an ex-member
of Parliament, but a good sport and a useful
citizen, was W. P. Brownell, one of the firm
of Brownell Bros.,'about the biggest soft
goods dealers in the Speck, who passed out
the other day, aged . r >4. In his day he was a
fine cricketer and footballer and a first-
class tennis-player. He leaves three sons,
all at the Front.
Captain Stanley Hunter, wild obtained
leave from the Vic. Mines Department to go
to the Front, visited the cathedral city of
Canterbury while he was briefly absent from
the eternal dread fulness. This is how the
boss archbishop's home town strikes him : “I
did Canterbury, and the simple-minded souls
in that quaint old town did me. They have
learned the golden rule that all Australians
are s.quatters; that all squatters are mil-
lionaires ; and that all* millionaires are
fools.”
Lino Gattorna, who died last week in
Adelaide, was an unusual kind of hotel-
keeper. As a youth in Italy lie took up
chemistry and mineralogy at Verona Uni-
versity, and continued his study in Ger-
many. Then lie somehow found himself
handling pick and shovel at Broken Hill in
the early ’9o’s, after which he “found him-
self” in the other sense, and pushing from
one pub to another became almost the best-
known Bung in Adelaide. He was only 46
at the end.
Two girls claim to be the first to ride.right
round the coast of Kangaroo Island (S.A.),
Thqy are the daughters of the lighthouse-
keepers at Cape Borda and Cape Willoughby,
and they took' a fortnight over it. The roads
are awful, and round the "back” (or south
side) of the island there are none at all.
Will Dyson’s exhibition, which began in
London, has since been to all the large pro-
vincial English towns, and is now on its
way to Paris. The set of lithographs which
were considered the finest pictures of the
show, have been purchased by various public
institutions —British Museum, South Ken-
sington Museum and Basle Museum (Swit-
zerland) among the rest. A selection of the
cartoons will go to the Australian Common-
wealth, along with Dyson’s other records of
the war.
CLEANING UP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The N. S. Wales Government will ask a confer-
ence of Premiers to agree to tlie abolition of State
Governorships.”
Out on its purple and astonished ear
The wreckage goes!
The cocked hat which right-thinking folk held dear
No longer shows
Its ancient shape where patriots congregate.
The long jest ends. Reform has wiped the slate
Of one more figure-head that lingered late
To mouth and pose.
The useless thing] the thing with brand untrue,
Is killed and done.
Pale shows and shams no more their youth renew.
The web is spun.
We see. We know. Our hands are strong to smite.
The cocked-hat goes mirth-hurtled through the night;
And more shall follow in its path all right
Now we’ve begun !
What’s next? Proscribe for death the biggest fraud?
No task we burke.
Bring forth the sham to meet the cleansing rod
Where’er it lurk.
Who comes? No king with sceptre in his hand,
No gilded mummy on its mouldered stand,
But this large thing that wears the Labor brand,
And will not work!
N.S.W.
C. O. M.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
An important banking amalgamation is
projected and, having been unanimously
approved by the two Boards, will be sub-
mitted to the respective shareholders, pro-
bably next month. The concerns interested
are the North Queensland and the Royal of
Queensland. Circumstances are altogether
in favor of the amalgamation; The
Bulletin sees no single point against it.
Bo far as country branches are concerned
there is at present hardly any overlapp.ng.
Later on, however, as the banks grow, they
are bound to get further and further into
one another’s territory, which will mean
duplication and unnecessary expense. In
Brisbane, where both head offices are, there
is duplication and unnecessary expense
already. Outside Queensland the amalga-
mation will bring strength to both. For
various reasons the Royal has had
no branch in Sydney or Melbourne, the
financial centres of Australia ; and that has
at times been felt to be such a drawback
that one of the Sydney trustee cos. was
appointed an agency for the receipt of
deposits—an unsatisfactory arrangement, of
course. The North Queensland has a very
vigorous branch in Sydney, and one of more
recent date in Melbourne. But it is a small
bank; and small banks are heavily handi-
capped when they go out to compete for
business.
The amalgamation will give the Royal a
chance to do something which it ought to
have done long ago, and which it could not
do now, on its own account, without some
risk. It got into the usual trouble over 20
years back, and it did not write off nearly
enough capital to wipe out the losses. One
result has been that, like the City Bank in
Sydney, it has had to struggle to make the
unprovided-for. losses good out of current
profit. And because 20 years ago, at any
rate, it did not have assets to represent
much more than half its capital, it had also
to struggle to pay a dividend on this non-
existent money. In other words, when it
could have paid 5 p.c. on its actual intact
capital, it was paying 21 p.c. on twice as
much, half of it non-existent. Naturally the
fact that the bank was paying a very poor
dividend only made the struggle harder.
Hence, for the past half-year, after over 20
years of battling, it was only able to
pay at the rate of 44 p.c. p.a. And that, of
course, meant that the shares are at a heavy
discount in the market. Perhaps it shouldn’t
matter what the shares are worth. But it
does, because a bank lives on credit, to a
great extent,' and when the dividend is very
small or the share values very low the public
is always inclined to suspect that something
is wrong and be nervous about it. And no
more is necessary to hold a bank back, no
matter how solid its foundations or how care-
fully it is managed.
The nominal capital of the new bank will
be £1,000,000 in 100.000 shares of £lO each ;
what the issued capital will be will depend
on the final valuation of the assets, which is
now almost complete. It is to be hoped that
the pruning-knife will be used wherever it is
necessary, and that the capital will be sub-
stantially under rather than over the value
of the assets. That will make it possible for
the new bank to pay an attractive dividend
-—say, 7 p.c.—for a start. A small dividend
is no good as a business-getter for any bank.
The new concern will have the benefit of the
combined knowledge and experience of the
two Boards (which consist of only three
men each). It is proposed that general-
manager Denny Day, of the Royal, who is in
very bad health, shall retire on a well-earned
pension; and general-manager Lees, of the
North Queensland, will take the same posi-
tion in the new bank.
■* * * *
The Australian Provincial Assurance
Association, Ltd. (Sydney), which began
apparently with £BO6O of preliminary exes.,
or anyway showed that much on the issue
of the first accounts: —
Income. Claims. Expenses. Deficit.
Oct., £ 8,060
1913 .. £10,219 £289 £18,213 16,343
1914 .. 17,288 866 28,473 28,394
1915 .. 28,663 1169 29,689 30,589
1916 . . 56,978 4790 44,220 22,621
The deficit is the amount of capital not
represented by visible assets. Put it this
way. The book value of the assets is
£56.018. Deduct liabilities (exclusive of
liabilities under contracts). £3294. That
leaves £52,724. The share capital is
£75,345; and the difference between that
and the net assets is £22,621. But observe
that the liabilities allowed for here are
exclusive of under contracts. The
co. has been in business four years and has,
so it says, “written over £1,600,000 of Ordi-
nary business.” Against those policies a
considerable sum ought by this time to have
been accumulated. How much this actuarial
liability amounts to it is impossible to tell
from the accounts; but an investigation is
promised at the end of the current year.
Until then all that can be said is this. The
office has so far spent £31,608 for which it
has no tangible assets; and it has £BOB7
accumulated towards meeting liabilities
under policies, whereas the actuary will
probably say that by this time the accumula-
tion should be £25,000 or more. That would
mean that about £48,000 of the shareholders’
capital has thus far been spent in securing
the business, and is not represented by any-
thing else.
The progress of some of the figures—of a
good many of the figures, in fact- —is djuite
satisfactory. The business in the Ordinary
department lias moved this way —
Policies Issued. Sum Assured.
1913 .. 1029 £291,300
1914 .. 1650 325,925
1915 .. 2739 432,060
1916 . . 3290 609,030
And the expense rate is moving the right
way, too. But there is still too much of the
cheap-jack about the co.’s methods. For
instance: —•
Considering the large amount of Ordinary business
written since the inception of the association, viz.,
over £1,600,000, and that we have now completed
our fourth financial year, the mortality rate continues
highly satisfactory and demonstrates the care exer-
cised in the risks accepted.
It; isn’t the amount of business written that
matters ; it is the amount that stays on the
books, and apparently there isn’t anything
like £1,600,000 in force. The first-year pre-
miums of the business written in 1913
totalled £10.086. The renewal premiums
actually collected in 1914 totalled only
£4316. The first-year premiums of the
business written in the three years 1913-4-5
totalled £37,632. The amount actually re-
ceived in renewal premiums in the year
ended October 3.1, 1916, was £20,177. And
as the renewal premiums no doubt include a'
considerable amount of war loading (an
extra 10 p.c. is ' charged on policies issued
since the outbreak of war if the holders pro-
ceed on service), it seems that, even makings
allowance for the policies that have become
claims, barely one-half of the policies that
are got are kept. That experience isn’t
cheerful, especially in view of the abundance
of money during the last year. The best
efforts of tlie management should be directed
to the improvement of these figures.
The co. has done the right thing in drop-
ping fire business: it now merely “acts as
agents for a fire co. at a liberal rate of
commission, whereby a profit is made on all
this class of business secured.” The revenue
account for the year showed £730 of net
premiums standing against £360 of exes, and
£273 of claims. Presumably, under” the new
rule, there will be no claims in the future.
But it would be better to drop the fire busi-
ness altogether. It doesn’t pay for the pre-
judice it creates. Whether even accident
business should be carried on by a co.
engaged in life insurance is very doubtful.
Canada is positive that it shouldn’t be, and
declines to allow its cos. to mix the two.
At the same time the co. is fairly entitled
to look round for ways and means of earn-
ing interest on its substantial capijtal of
£75,345. If the more or less speculative
accident business doesn’t provide it, we may
be sure that the life business will be ex-
pected to. And that would be most un-
desirable. The proper course later on may
be to separate the two sections, and allocate
portion of the capital definitely to the acci-
dent business.

They did it. but there wasn’t enough in .the
bottle, and they had to fill up out of the cask.
There were two troubles during the year—
two direct troubles, at least. One was a
shrinkage of the receipts from the brewery
combine by one-third. And that was serious,
because of this co.’s big interest in the com-,
bine. The co. nowadays isn't in the brewing-
business itself: it was one of those which
sold out for shares in the Carlton and United
Breweries. But the C. and U. B. didn’t take
over all the assets: it took over no pubs at
all and only leased what brewery premises it
wanted. So this Castlemaine (Melb.) Co.
has two big lots of property: the combine
shares, which last year brought in only £6260
as against £9395 iii 1915 and £8857 in 1914.
and pub and other premises, which gave
about the usual £7591 in rents, etc.
While the income was thus cut down
exes, ran up through the expense of
replacing some debentures that fell due.
“Interest on mortgages, etc.,” was £948
in the previous year; last year the
"etc.”—apparently the expenses of this new
issue—consumed another thousand. And
even now it doesn't, look as if the debt has
been arranged on a permanent basis. The
old debenture liability had been gradually
reduced to £69,800. The present accounts
show £38,900 owing that way, and the bal-
ance has been raised on overdraft.
These and other movements in the figures
are shown in this table: —

So, in addition to the combine shares —the
value of which consists mainly of goodwill—
the co. has assets valued at £123,856; and
it owes £84,681. apart from a few pounds
of debenture interest. That means just
under £40.000 net of assets exclusive of the
shares. But, as mentioned above, £118,406
is represented by freeholds, mostly pubs; and
pubs are a doubtful proposition these times.
Yet it speaks well for the value of the
assets of this concern that “realisations of
securities over book values, transfer fees,
etc,,” have in the past three years added to
the revenue, in this way: 1914, £1033; 1915,
£402; 1916. £2153. No C. and U. B. shares
have, of course, been sold: they alee not
to go on the market. What they are worth
to hold depends largely on the success of
the Cold Tea campaign. Its success of late
has been simply amazing; but whether the
devil who has been a saint amid the distresses
of war will be a saint amid the rejoicings
over peace has. yet to be seen. Still, on
account of the • uncertainties, this isn't an
investment to recommend to Aunt Tabitha.
Usher’s Metropolitan Hotel. Ltd, (Syd-
ney)—the deficit of £18,067 with which the
co. started was made up of goodwill £16,573
and preliminary expenses £1494 :—■
To Reduce
Aug., Loss. Dividends. Deficit. Deficit,
£18,067
1914.. £921 - 18,988
Additional goodwill items . . 19,547
1915.. 1070 20,617
Profit.
1916.. 105 £lO5 20,512
Sydney Daily Telegraph Co. some years
ago bought the premises in which A. .J. Usher
carried on the Metropolitan Hotel; and when
the lease of the King-street premises occupied
by the D.T. was on its last legs Usher had
to shift. A hundred yards along Castlereagh-
street was the Trocadero Cafe; and as the
premises were old and the Australia had
proved it a good neighborhood for a pub of
the first-class, this co. was formed to buy
Usher’s p-oodwill and build a new house. The
freehold cost £30,000; the lease of the Troca-
dero ran into £2247; and the goodwill (in-
cluding license) of .Usher’s ate another
£17,132. On top of that was the cost of the
building and furniture —apparently £80,633
for the one and £15.231 for the other. Add
£1494 for preliminary exes., and it will be
seen that before the first gin-and-bitters was
served the co. had spent £146,737, without
paying the wholesale gin-and-bitters mer-
chant. The original share capital was
£85,000, with a debenture debt of £50,000.
That being insufficient, and money being by
that time in demand for killing people by
very much more rapid means, there was a
further issue of £lO,OOO 2nd mortgage deben-
tures and £7OOO 8 p.c. fixed cumulative pref.
shares. But dear money wasn’t the only
skeleton the co. found gibbering inside. Six
o’clock closing, threatened for some time,
arrived ; and “in addition to this encroach-
ment on our profits, since the hotel was
opened two successive awards of Wages
Boards have had the effect of largely increas-
ing the employees’ wages.”
During the first couple of years the bar
business was carried on in a mere shell of a
place, and almost sufficient profit was made
to pay the heavy overhead charges. In the
past year—practically a full year of trading
in the new premises—the early months were
naturally unDrofitable, but the later months
were almost good enough to swing the
balance to the right* side. • There is, how-
ever, a long way to go yet before shareholders
can have more than a taste. Pref. dividend
is accumulating; in the years to come in-
creasing amounts will have to be spent on
renewals ; the debenture debt will have to be
provided for, and the preliminary exes, etc.,
written off. The whole position of these high-
class residential hotels is most unsatisfac-
tory. It would pay a co. 10 times better to
put its money into a dozen low-class drinking
shops; and that isn’t a desirable state of
affairs, and the law which works out that
way is more of an ass than usual.

Many a time this co. has shown a larger
profit. But there never was a time when
The Bulletin was better pleased with the
look of the accounts. Sales, naturally, are
not wliat they were. Look at the totals for
the Mareh-September halves: —
1911. . £146,107 I 1914. . £182,316
1912.. 172,461 I 1915.. 205,469
1913.. 170,759 | 1916.. 152,065
That the co. should have shown such big
figures for Mareh-September, 1!)15, was due
not only to the sinful borrowing boom which
this country maintained in the face of a
war for its life, but to the evident determi-
nation of the directors to reduce stocks and
consequently debt. This is the result so far
as the debt is concerned :
Mar., 1911. . £32,468 Mar., 1914. , £83,444
Sept., 1911., 38,768 Sept., 1914., 96,918
Mar., 1912. 46,796 Mar., 1915.. 103,787
Sept.., 1912.. 72,276. Sept., 1915. . 65,188
Mar., 1913.. 66,836 Mar., 1916.. 53,482
Sept.-, 1913. . 70,968 Sept., 1916.. 29,953
In view of the growing dearness of money
and the uncertainty of being able to get rid
•of stocks later on at anything like present
costs, those last , four lines constitute one of
the co.’s best achievements. And it is all
the more creditable because It was partly
accomplished by abstention from dividends:
five half-years were allowed to pass without
distributing a bean, which meant an addition
of just-on £40,000 to the reserves. It is
quite likely that these reserves are not the
most substantial ones in the world: £51.289,
or nearly one-half of the total, is a deprecia-
tion reserve. But even if they were used
to write down the assets to bedrock and
lower, there would surely be a large amount
over, because the total book value of the
assets is only £277,651. That, of course,
will help to pay dividends in future, for it
is easier, generally speaking, for a co. with
£200,000 worth of assets to pay a dividend
on £130,000 of capital than for a co. to do
it on a bare £130,000 worth of belongings.'
All the same, times are not going to be bril-
liant for many people in the construction
trades. There is not only going to be a lot
less demand than there used to be, but two
years hence people will be looking a lot
harder at sixpence than they do to-day, and
prices asked for timber are going to be very
different in consequence.

Adamax Roofing is made from superior-qualitj'
fibred woollen felt, which is thoroughly saturated
with pure mineral asphaltum. The saturated felt is
then heated at a high temperature. The process
results in a rough surface being deposited on the
roofing, which protects it from all the elements—
heat, cold, wind, rain, acids, gases, etc. Adamax
Roofing contains no tar, fish oil or resin, which are
sensitive to climatic conditions. It will not rot,
perish or crumble. It costs less than galvanised iron
—less for freight; easier to handle and means cool
homes during the summer. For full particulars call
or send to William Adams & Co., Ltd., Sydney, Mel-
bourne, Brisbane and Perth. — Advt.

The extraordinary success and popularity of the
Babcock and Wilcox Water-tube Boiler, so univer-
sally used where the economy in steam-raising is con-
sidered, is due, no doubt, to the splitting up of the
water .into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-
tion and excellent facilities for cleaning, the heavy
lin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with. — -Advt.

The old remuneration seems to have been
about 5 p.c. as an average; the present is
2 } p.c. 'A. H. Ilasell, the managing agent,
whose business this more or less co-opera-
tive co. took over, has treated the concern
very generously, and for the past year,
instead of taking the 4 p.c. div. on the 51)00
shares which he • received for the business,
he makes the £2OO available to reduce the
book value of paper assets. The past year’s
conditions were adverse in almost all
respects but one. Costs were high and raw
materials often impossible to get; but,
owing to the extra-good season, it was
rather less difficult than usual to do business
with the cocky without giving him credit.
This co. didn’t feel sure enough of the
future to give much credit, anyhow. One
result is that the co. has only £5842 on its
books, while it has £10,495 in cash or on
call; and its total liabilities are £7671, in-
cluding mortgage on the works freehold.
The share capital is £22,718, including
Hasell’s £5OOO. That loading is a small
insect in the pot. But it would be stupid
t 0 s ? e . the insect and forget the large
possibilities of co-operative beneficence about
the concern.
Broken Hill Junction, the lame dog of the
big lode, prints it in heavy black letters, and
must have been tempted to-make a splash and
do it in red : after writing off all expendi-
ture on development, and also £2283 from
°, hi plant scrapped, there remains a profit of
£5644 for the half-year. It is too many
years to flog the mind back over since the
co. paid a dividend. At Sept. 30 it still
owed £lO,OOO on debentures ; but as it had
about £lO,OOO net liquid and semi-liquid
assets in hand, including estimated sur-
pluses on ore delivered and ore on hand, the
directors say: “Provided recent conditions
continue this period [to the end of a new
12 months’ contract lately made with the
Sulphide Corporation] should not only pay
off the balance of the mortgage, but also
provide a dividend for shareholders.” War
prices have of course helped enormously.
The output was 19,142 tons; so the profit
(including estimated surpluses) was only
6s. per ton. With normal prices for metal's
the co. would certainly get 6s. less for ore
assaying (crude) lO.loz. silver, 12.87 p.c.
lead and 7.37 p.c zinc ; and that would wipe
out the profit shown. On the other hand the
co. spent £4893 on development and £5363
on preparatory work; so at a pinch there
would be a wider margin than this 6s. to
work on. But the figures are not good
enough to lead one to expect dividends under
normal conditions. Still, it is always worth
while plugging away at the Junction even if
only working expenses are met; for the mine
is so situated that a rich bulge may be found
almost anywhere.

Adelong Gold Estates (N.S.W.) showed
a miserable profit of £8666 for the six
months ended October 31. This profit added
to the amount brought forward and £4OOO
lrom the equalisation fund provided £5200.
There are 75,000 shares, so the shilling divi-
dend cost £3750. The balance of £1450
goes for depreciation. These dredges do eat
up money for repairs and renewals. This
co. charges its working account with £4lOO
for this kind of work. The surplus of liquid
assets is reduced to £3002. which compares
with £9689 at the end of the previous term.
The debenture debt has been reduced to
£3OOO, so the co. is in fairly easy .circum-
stances. But unless there is an improve-
ment in values it will take an enormous
quantity of wash to return the paid-up
capital. Last half-year 640,500 cub. yds.
returned 2773|0z.
Lobb’s Hole Petroleum and Copper Mining
Co. issues a prospectus, and if the thing
floats it will probably be the first attempt
ever made to mine petroleum and copper on
the same spot. J. B. Jaquet’s report upon
the earth-slide near Lobb’s Hole, which is in
the wildest part of the Monaro (N.S.W.),
refers to the presence in the old copper mine
of a natural gas found to contain methane.
T\yo analyses of this, gas by different
analysts disclosed a high percentage of this
elemenf, and in all oilfields methane is
accepted as proof that there is petroleum
handy. But it does not necessarily mean
that oil is alongside the gas, and it. does not
follow that oil will be found in the copper-
mine. But £3OOO won’t help to strike oil
with an ordinary drill used for boring for
gold and coal. In petroleum mining only a
standard oil drill and rig should be used.
All other drilling plants are worse than
valueless ; they do harm because they cannot
find oil, and their, negative results mislead
the searchers.
* * * *
Chillagoe, which has evidently been wait-
ing for something to turn up, has delayed
issuing its report for 1915, and has only just
given, it to the long-since tired-out share-
holders. In an undated report (which
must, however, have been written within the
last week or two, because it laments the
death of H. F. C. Keats, one of the direc-
tors) the Board “regrets that it is not yet
in a position to make any definite statement
regarding; the resumption of operations.
Notice of foreclosure has been given by the
debenture-holders, but nothing further has
been done.” The loss for 1915 (including
only £470 for depreciation) carried the 1.
and L. debit from £60,269 to £90,069, and at
that date the co. owed: General debenture
account, £573,300; Etheridge Railway deben-
tures, £225,000; Einasleigh debenture
account, £15,000; deficiency coupons,
£29,862 ; Bank of Australasia, £10,857 ; sun-
dry’ creditors (mostly accrued debenture in-
terest), £57,927. Total, £911,946. Liquid
assets (exclusive of stores) were about
£25,000. It has been a ghastly business.
The co.’s railway, which the State will take
over sooner or later, is 255 miles long, and
stands in the books, without rolling stock,
at £798,784. ' A bill authorising the State to
purchase the entire outfit from the deben-
ture-holders for £450,000 is now before the
Queensland Parliament.
The Etheridge gold mines (Q.), which tlie
Chillagoe people took over and passed to a
separate co., hoping to incidentally feed
their own railway and works, have had a
poor time. In the 12 months to the end of
June, figures for which are only just out,
there was a nominal profit of £877 on work-
ing account; and Melbourne exes, reduced
that to £741. But nothing was written off
against depreciation. Actually, as machin-
ery, plant and buildings cost about £30,000,
and depreciation is at least 12| p.c., the loss
was, say, £3OOO. The stoppage of work at
Chillagoe drove the co. to look south for a
market for its concentrates ; and that meant
heavy freight charges and poor net returns.
It took 4798 tons ore, converted into 505
tons concentrates, to make this working
profit of £877. *' Costs ran to an appalling
535. per ton of ore. Hold on to something
and look at it:- —Mining (including develop-
ment), 19s. lOd. (which isn’t so terrible) ;
milling, 7s. lOd.; power, ss. 9d. (phew!);
ore transport, 4s. lOd.; concentrates trans-
port, Bs. 3d.; and the rest management and
gei oral exes. There are four mines—the
Queenslander, Havelock, Big Reef and Nil
Desperandum—with ore developed sufficient
to keep the plant going at its recent half-
gait for two years. But in present circum-
stances it really doesn’t seem to matter much
whether the co. has two or 20 years’ ore in
reserve. The finances are not too comfort-
able. The co. on June 30 owed £6037,
and liquid and more or less liquid assets
(such as stores) were worth £5127.
* * * *
Corella, Cloncurry (Q-), requires money
to purchase leases within easy distance of
its smelting plant. Knowing that the Rose-
hud has but limited reserves of 5 p.c. ore
above the 300 ft. level, the manager has
strongly advised the Board to purchase
other shows, following the example of the
Hampden, Elliott and Cutlibert. The policy
is expensive, and Is. calls don’t provide
much capital for so ambitious an. enterprise.
The total output of Charters Towers in
November was £9654, so it isn't to be won-
dered at that the brokers are cultivating
business in the Rimu flat (M.L.)' properties.
South Aus. Oil Wells Co. reports SsBft.
drilled in six months—not much of a record
when in California they drill and bring in
oil-wells of 2000 ft. in six weeks. The drill
is down to 3888 ft. in a hard shale with thin
bands of sandstone and layers of .shale and
sand. For the last thousand feet there has
been a strong pressure of natural gas. The
well manager guesses the gas contains
methane, which is an infallible indication of
the existence of petroleum. Why have the
directors not had the gas tested' long ago?
If there is no methane in it, they should
abandon the hole, because a gas well in the
desert near Robe is worth about as much as
King O’Malley’s free-steamer tickets for
M’s.P. The total cost of boring the hole at
Robe, including office expenses, has been
£16,791, of which £5022 has been spent in
the office and not at the mine.
To grip the Edna May ore position it is
necessary to keep an eye on what is happen-
ing in Edna Deeps. Taking the last three,
half-yearly statements, reserves in the big-
mine have worked out this way :
Oct., 1915. April, 1916. Oct., 1916.
Average Average Average
Tons. Value. Tons. Value. Tons. Value.

Put it another way. A year ago ore proved
was worth £202.327; six months ago,
£204,219 now, £143.840, This drop is due
to the eating up of reserves owing to no new
level having been opened up ; but the stone
availably is such that even with the mill
crushing over 3000 tons monthly the co.
isn’t likely to be hard up for quartz, even if
a gusher is got before the present crosscut
finds the reef. No. 1 level still stands up to
the collar; but ore developed at Nos. 2 and
3 has not kept pace with extractions, and
the grade of the blocks left shows that a
lot of the plums have ulready gone into the
pot. Taking into account stone already
crushed, the 300 ft. level should yield about
38,000 tons out of tire main shoot, which
has shortened in length, no quartz of value
showing south of the crosscut, while the
south-eastern bulge at the elbow has moved
up towards that on the boundary to the
north-east. Official figures give the stopes
at 300 ft. a length of 180 ft. In the Deeps
it looks as if the shoot is not 120 ft. long,
for the shaft appears to have gone only into
a corner of the lode. If 60ft. is cut out, a
deduction will have to be made in the re-
serves available for Edna May below 300 ft.
Edna May’s costs of treatment last half-
year ran out at nearly 355. per ton, power
representing over 7s. 6d. Including milling
and sand cyaniding, and excluding slimes
costs, it took over 30s. to get 775. In a poor
mine this would be death. With ounce stuff
to handle, 3s. per month comes along to the
Edna crowd in divs. If costs be set down
at 305., the profit on ore reserves runs out
at about 455. per share, apart from what
may be collected from the dump of accu-
mulated slimes. Should these live up to
sample, the co. will get £30,000, or another
15s. per share, making 60s. in sight. The
problem, then, is: How rich or how ragged
is the shoot from 300 ft. down? The solu-
tion lies in the Deeps. Once the 480 ft. level
in Deeps is measured up properly—a job
that is understood to be in hand —the worth
of Ednas can be counted up to a nicety.
