Commodore (W.A.) claims to have secured
something good in its Macquarie option at
Meekatharra. Fair have been ex-
tracted from the show hitherto, and it is
now declared to have the makings of a big-
low-grade thing. Commodore’s tips, how-
ever, have not been of much value in the
past, so the public will require some con-
vincing evidence before it becomes enthusi-
astic.
* * . * *
With the exception of the Yuanmi, all
Lake View and Oroya’s smaller Australian
mining ventures have given out, and the
Yuanmi was admitted at the recent annual
meeting to be a struggling weakling which
might pass hence at any time. Even the back-
bone of the co., Lake View and Star, is,
owing to poor developments, decreased
values and increased costs, finding it diffi-
cult to keep its head above water. The
struggle is more severe, says the Board,
because of the “selfish, obstinate and idiotic
refusal” of the miners to allow the employ-
ment of alien labor. The Zinc Corporation,
in which the co. is largely interested, was
described as in fine shape. Dividends had
been resumed on the ordinary shares, and
the co. would soon be making quarterly dis-
tributions as before the war. Burma Cor-
poration was quoted as one of the successes
which would atone for permanent and tem-
porary failures among the co.’s ventures.
The original investment of £BO,OOO in this
spec, had now a market valuation exceeding
£400,000, equal to about 10s. per Lake View
and Oroya share. The market value of four
of the co.’s investments was, on the day of
the meeting, equal to 155., whereas the quote
for Lake View and Oroyas was only 11s.
Efforts are again being made to resurrect
the' defunct Hidden Secret (W.A.). Years
ago a small telluride shoot containing a lot
of silver and several ounces of gold per ton
was exposed near the surface by a party of
Italians, and from it about £70.000 was
obtained. Impoverishment, however, set in
at a shallow depth, and the show was ulti-
mately left for dead. Latterly a local syn-
dicate has had a try, and it now reports
good values but no telluride. Without the
latter the future is likely to be drab.
T. H. Martyn, the Sydney broker who
very early specialised in gold dredging, and
subsequently picked up one or two apparently
good tin things for the Malaya Tin Corpora-
tion, is just back from Siberia, where, accord-
ing to a circular to the shareholders of the
Corporation, he has “secured outright some
important properties which promise to prove
immediately remunerative.” An early re-
port quoted some astonishing gold values.
The Speck’s Government has managed to
screw an extra ss. a ton for fuel oil out
of the Navy Department, and now has a
provisional contract for 8000 tons a year for
10 years at the price it asked. As the co.
that owns the Latrobe shale property has
come down from £125,000 to £65,000 it looks
as if there will be something doing, as the
State Parliament isn’t likely to turn down
the purchase now.
Amalgamated Zinc has made a start at
construction on the site of its electrolytic
zinc works at Bisdon (Tas.). Its American
expert is expected shortly.
A temporary shortage of sulphuric acid
lately caused a scare among producers of
zinc concentrates. Australia shipped 146,630
tons of these concentrates in the nine
months to September 30, 1916. The de-
clared export value is £681,583, but that is
only a trifle compared with the market value
of the contents, including some 50,000 tons
of recoverable spelter and 30,000 tons of
sulphuric acid. These Australian zinc con-
centrates contain over 20 p.c. sulphur, the
stuff we require so much at present. When
the concentrates arrive in America they are.
carefully roasted and enormous quantities of
acid are made from the fumes. After this
process, the concentrate is ready for the zinc
retort. At present we are importing sul-
phur from Japan, and the Japs, are eager
to get our valuable concentrates in exchange.
South Bischoff is eight miles from the big
mountain, and the Waratah syndicate has
placed a d-head mill on it. The first return
irom 12 tons raised in prospecting and
development returned eight bags of 70 pc.
concentrates and six bags of 45 p.c. con-
centrates. This seems to indicate an aver-
age of about 3 p.c. tin, worth (say) 90s.
fiecent reports are very optimistic because
a face of porphyry sft. wide at the end of
the tunnel is showing good prospects. But
so far only a short#run of ore has been
proved.

SCOTS WHISKY
A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, December 18, 1016.
My Dear Arini,—
Though it’s' early in the toy industry
in this land of wheat and mutton, there is
quite a good show of Christmas presents
for the nippers in Challis House window.
The smiles on the face of the elephant and
the Wellingtonian beaks of Australian par-
rots have been achieved by returned soldiers.
There’s a depot in Ash-street where the
“voluntaries” are taught the tricks, so that
they may pass them on at Randwick Hos-
pital. It isn’t so easy as it looks to make
a baby emu or an old-man kangaroo. One
damsel, being disgusted with her emu, threw
it recklessly from a depot window. It blew
into the Red Cross Kitchen, and irate autho-
rity stamped upstairs for an explanation.
A. few weeks ago a fashionable Blue
Mountain resort was aquiver with excite-
ment. After a long, cold winter, in which
there hadn’t been even a new curate to
discuss, the wires flashed the message to a
smart local hostel: “Madame Melba and
companion will arrive to-morrow.” Some-
how the news soon ran round ; and the
caravanserai’s chef, hearing the great tid-
ings, awaited instructions to spring some-
thing special .on the distinguished visitors.
The day came and the Western train was
scanned. No Melba hopped out. Then a
goods train jolted into the little station, and
the hostel’s farm-hand took delivery of two
pedigree cows, Madame Melba and Ada
Crossley. They came from the West, where
they had been thus duly christened with a
bran mash or something. Then the pro-
prietress solemnly invited the hamlet to see
Melba in the bail.
The 12th Light Horse dames held their
revel under the trees in the Garden Theafre
grounds. The theatre was heavily disguised
'behind bunting, and it took another shilling
to get through a chink in the drapery.. A
slight vaudeville co., all amateurs, made
heroic efforts to be heard. But the difficult
acoustics of an open-air show call for a pro-
fessional training. Dora Alexander, under
the shade of leafy black millinery, warbled
more audibly than the rest of the co. Mrs.
Nathan—Thelma Maddrell of a while ago —-
gave an exhibition of serpentine writhings to
suitable music; Miss Kirkcaldie attacked
Hill’s “Maori Maiden” in the Poi song.
When a small boy with his- face blacked
took possession of the stage, long-suffering
patriots in the audience melted away to the
tea tables. Mrs. Herbert Allen and Mrs.
Lang Campbell were at the cake stall; and
the tall Miss Von Arnheim, her face swathed
in one of the new tulle bags (all over the
hat and tied in cascade-like bows under one
ear), and lion. sec. Ethel Clibborn were
amongst the lady pirates.
What affection the Lord must have for
Australia —that is, if it be true that He
loves a cheerful giver!. The other day the
Red Cross asked for razors—second-hand or
otherwise. In two days 500 responded to
the appeal.
General Finn has been wearing a •sock
of plaster-of Paris the last week or two.
The cheery, strenuous old soldier, who “takes
it out” of a bit of garden when he feels
that way, had the bad luck to break a small
bone in his foot in his enthusiastic efforts to
till a steep hillside. But he is able to take
the limb to the ’phone and answer the sym-
pathetic friends at the other end of the wire.
The Journalists’ Institute offered oysters
on ice and other oblations to F. W. Ward
at Farmer’s on Friday. After being about
two years out of harness, the popular old
whiskery ex-chief of the Sydney D.T. is itch-
ing to be up and at it again. So he goes to
the Brisbane Telegraph. Spare Tom
Heney, of the Herald, took the chair and
named the speakers. D. D. Braham, who
followed Ward in King-street, poured out
the first flagon of praise. Justice Ferguson
put a foam on the measure, and Brunsdon
Fletcher, Ernest Blackwell and Charlton, of
the Mail, completed the bumper. Harry
Gullett, in khaki, lean and tanned from
camp sunbaths, was in the party.
Over 700 visitors to see the three soldiers’
homes opened at French’s Forest the other
day included workers, spectators and the
press-gang which lias been putting a spade
in the sod. Architect De Putron has been
putting his shoulder to the carpenter’s bench
every week-end since the year was young.
Result, five cottages, for which he is the
responsible party, as they say in the classics.
So Mrs. De Putron had the joy of turning
the key to open one of the cots. A beautiful
nest, built by the Leichhardt branch of the
Voluntary Workers, was given to tall re-
eruiting-sergeant Gallia, an Italian badly
wounded at the Front with the A.I.F. and
the husband of a charming little Irish wife.
A Mosman boys’ school band piped cheer-
fully over the proceedings.
Speech-day at Sydney Grammar School
brought his Ex. from Moss Vale to hand
over the prizes. One small boy took away
enough literature to keep him in reading
matter for the next 12 months —if school-
boys ever read the prizes headmasters con-
sider good for them. Headmaster Sloman
is in khaki on the other side, so veteran A.
H. Lucas reigns temporarily in his stead.
Justice Street, in a speech that kept the
youngsters interested from start to finish,
gripped the school’s yearly record with its
proud boast of having trounced its once
invincible rival, the old King's School, on
the athletic side. His Honor’s voice broke
a little when he spoke softly of the gallant
dead in other lands who once gathered
their prizes at the “Grammar” break-up.
His own son's name is on the school's Roll
ol Honor.
This week there’s a States’ School Con-
ference ; but I’m not sure that it comes
under the heading “amusements.” And
here is one of the hard-worked school marms,
Miss Lilian Ellis, who hopes to wipe away
all tears from the study of at least one
of the three It’s. Her short cut to reading
has now been adopted by the State Educa-
tion Department, and an enterprising pub-
lisher has acquired the rights for the rest
of the Commonwealth. So far Miss Ellis
has worked in chalk on a blackboard, but
the Government Printer has unleashed his
foundry men, and books set up in the Ellis
type are almost ready. The old Roman let-
ters are used. But the long vowels are
printed- in heavy block type and the short
nuisances in thin light type.
What’s it all about? The short cut to
reading, of course, with the knickerbocker
class all smiles. Babies, under the Ellis sys-
tem, rush such literary hurdles as “phthisis,”
for example. The “p” and the two “h’s”
wear little crooks which drag them out of
existence. Then the infants go ahead and
note that the first “i” is in heavy type, and
a few other details that make phthisis almost
as easy as b-a-t.
Captain Hubert Orr, of the Army Medi-
cal, tucked the hand of his best girl,
Sylvia Hargreaves, under his arm in old
St. Philip’s Church last week. The bride’s
white tailored suit had the usual dash of
pink, with a cloud of white tulle veil tossed
up round the neck in the foam of the
moment. Bridesmaidens Fanny Orr and
Svbil Neal, in white muslin, stepped de-
murely behind. The ’groom’s company
formed the guard of honor. Party at the
Carlton. Sylvia’s father is Town Clerk of
Ashfield.
Good-looking Eileen Bain, of the Neutral
Bay hillside, meant to have lent a hand
with the flower-selling at the Hospice
matinee. But man proposes, etc. So she
changed her plans and her name, and was
married to surgeon Roberts, of H.M.A.S.
Brisbane. The bride rejoins her husband
early in the New Year —wouldn’t you like
to know the address?
Let’s hope the Belgian babes get everything
we send their way. Anyhow, the Archbishop
of Canterbury takes the responsibility of the
cheque from Gwen Nelson’s cabaret party at
the Burlington Cafe. This is the affair that
was first dated for the Pacific, at Manly.
Tickets first mentioned at ss. also had to
move —to 7s. 6d. a head. Iced asparagus and
other creature comforts would have eaten up
too much of the crown. Pianist Fay tossed
off some dance music in an alcove midair, and
Nelson pere and mere helped to keep things
moving. The puffed upper drapery worn by
the damsels present had one specially delect-
able example—azure silk with a blush rose
at the waist, and a pretty girl, Miss
Edwards, inside it.
There’s a sea of sympathy this Christmas
for Tom Hughes and his wife. Of their two
sons—other chicks have they none the
elder, Roger, has just died of wounds in
France. He was one of Sydney's promising
young doctors, and had been with the A.M.( .
at the Front only a week or two when the
fatal bullet found him. The younger boy,
Geoff., is with the Aviation Corps at the
same address. Roger’s young widow was
Eileen Maher a year ago. Before leaving for
the other side he did good service for a while
at the Artillery camp at the Warren.
Ina Cornish and Millie Nathan left Syd-
ney last Saturday, destination unknown.
This seeming carelessness about arranging
for a port of disembarkation is accounted for
by the fact that both ladies had originally
booked for England by a certain boat which
suddenly ceased to be a peaceful liner and
became something else. The powers that be,
however, are said to have intimated to the
two would-be war-workers that they might
still travel in the mystery sloop if they got
aboard quietly. Hence the flitting. It is
said that they are the only ladies aboard, and
as there are one or two other warlike persons
afloat, they ought to come back engaged
several times over, if they feel that way.
Helen Christian lately landed back at
Elizabeth Bay after two years in Pharaoh’s
country. She went there with her sister,
Mrs. Syd. Christian —she married, a cousin
—and has been doing nursing jobs ever
since. Another sister, Mrs. Antill, super-
visor of our War Chest operations, is lately
back from a country effort after Chest
expansion. Miss Ralston accompanied the
popular Antill lady to form branches, and
a good haul is reported.
Intelligence-Officer Mills, a brother of
Mrs. Forrest, the Queensland verse-writer,
has lately been in hospital somewhere near
the Nyassa Falls. He had been in khaki
with the Rhodesian Rifles since the begin-
ning of the war. Now, in spite of his
malaria, he lifts a cheer over the quality of
the healing haven that the British have
made out of an enemy mission-station. Young
Mills hadn’t previously slept in a “real
bed” for months and months, and finds it
restful after stalking lions, tigers and Ger-
mans since 1914. He has been offered a
commission in the Coldstream Guards for
services rendered, and will take it —when he
can be spared from his job in the nearly-all-
black continent.
Veteran Sam McCaughey, godfather of
irrigation in the liiverina, went to the
TJ.S.A. and Canada the other day. The
pink-cheeked old man —he must be 80-odd —
is a bachelor: there’s never an Eve, except
a stern housekeeper, to help Adam gather
his rosebuds at Yanko.
Mrs. A. J. Hare, the forceful chieftainess
of the Lane Cove V.A.D.’s, is going to holi-
day for a couple of months. During that
time the Red Cross has decided to close
Reinga—the riverside home for soldiers, lent
by the O’Briens. Last week, by way of
saying “Thank you. kindly,” soldiers who
have been nursed back to health in this
green nest gave an evening party there.
Guests of honor were the lady commandant
(Mrs. Hare), the matron and about 50
V.A.D.’s from the River unit.
Wentworth Falls is waiting patiently till
after the war. Then its new golf links,
opened in March this year, are going to
show the rival Leura course what’s what.
John McLaughlin’s country home is just
over the fence; R. M. Pitt, chairman of the
show, grows acres of fruit and daffodils on
an adjacent hillside; T)r. Davidson and Dr.
Thomas (Manly) have bought properties a
biscuit’s throw from the links’ water dam.
Soms day it’s going to grow into a lake,
but not yet. Soutar laid out the Falls’
course, which encourages golfers in pettis.
One of the best is the Manly lady, Mrs.
Wright.
The other morning Dr. Leslie Fetherston
was married in the sacristy of St. Mary’s to
Emily E. Beatty, ex-matron of the Hospital
for Women, Paddington. The bride wore
a costume of -cream gaberdine 'and a white
moire hat, with touches of emerald green,
and carried a sheaf of arum lilies. Jean
Beatty, the bridesmaid, wore a pink chiffon
taffeta coatee over white organdie; white
leghorn hat and pink roses. J. J. Quinn was
next-best man. As the bride and bridegroom
left the Cathedral they walked between a
double file of nurses from the hospital where
the bride had reigned so successfully. Mrs.
Fetherston, mother of the bridegroom, held
a reception at Petty’s, and later' Dr. and
Mrs. Fetherston left for Medlow.
The snow-haired mother of Lieut.-Colonel
Owen Howell Price, so recently killed in
France, is bearing it bravely. So also is the
youthful fiancee of the young officer, Molly
Gray, of Kiama. Miss Gray casts her
energy into Red Cross work in these sad
days. Mrs. Howell Price was one of the
winsome Winchcombes, sister of Frederick,
of the Upper House.
Nesta Griffiths, Molly Langer Owen and
Barbara Knox, all good war-workers, were
guests at a tea given lately by Mrs. Clibborn
at the Sydney Golf Club. The trio are oft
for a trip to India.
Howard .1. Hunter, genial managing-
director of the people who shoe you with
Goodyear Tyres, has been having a business
gallop through the Noted States and Canada.
He threw his baggage aboard the Matai at
'Frisco the other day to return to the only
entirely belligerent continent in the world.
(Did it ever strike you that that’s what we
are?)
.The Little Theatre was full for the Red
Cross pickle-depot concert, with Yvonne
Gharvin at the piano, and other music-makers
to add to the glad noises. Mrs. Arthur
Joscelyne, who mothers the embalmed onions
and the V.A.D.’s in the Ash-street factory,
was hon. sec. of the movement and Mrs.
Leslie Norton counted the profits.
Mrs. J. C. Williamson, who always turns
up trumps when there’s something doing for
the Hospice -for the Dying, reports “all’s
well” with the recent matinee cheque. Nearly
£7OO will fall into the lap of the Mother
Rectress later on. Mrs. John Barlow,
another fine worker for the Darlinghurst
haven, will now take a rest over the holi-
days. It’s a long way to Tipperary and the
making-up of the £5OOO so cruelly filched
from the Hospice; but “it’s dogged as does
it,” so Trollope said —and he pounded out
three-volume novels.
Young Ormiston, of the Hunter’s Hill clan
(he’s a nephew of Dr. Isabel, now in Rou-
mania), recently won his commission in
France. He has been wounded, besides, and
all on the sunny side of two or three and
twenty.
There is a new man in Gilbert Neill's
chair at the N.S.W. Tourist Office. He
is E. H. Palmer, assistant superintendent.
Neill is in khaki and goes to see the person-
ally-conducted tours at the battle front.
Amidst the tons of Christmas literature
is a Wattle book, by “Tally” Wollaston, the
Adelaide opal expert, who plays with Aus-
tralian flora in the S. Australian Wattle
League.
A former well-known Manly Scotchby-
terian parson named Moleyneux died ■ in
England the other day. It was in __ the
long ago, when lie expounded the Word.
I fancy, that the village kirk’s elders had
an idea. Easy chairs instead of straight-
backed pews! Whether the congregation
slept more. peacefully I can’t say. But no
other local kirk has catered so luxuriously
for its clients.
A young sister of pretty Enid Bennett has
sailed off for the land of Presidential notes,
where she has already been conscripted for
the movies. According to latest accounts
another Sydney girl, Dorothy Gordon, has
“struck oil” on the same patch. She found
herself right in the Jesse Lasky trenches and
before its* manager-in-chief. “Oh. you are
a Miss Gordon, are you? Come from Aus-
tralia? Happen to know anyone named
Fosbery? One of them was my aunt.” Miss
Dorothy gasped out that “she was mine once,
too,” and they got to business.
Two high-placed Samaritans, Miss Eadith
Walker and Miss Edith Hill, will hold car-
nival and a Christmas Tree at Randwick
Military Hospital. All this week they and
a group of assistants are working out there
to that great end. Usually Miss Walker has
a Great Revel and a Big Tree at Yaralla on
Christmas Day. This year the festive
energies are transferred to Randwick.
Graham G. Parry, a writing man known
in Sydney, whose book upon Portuguese
Prisons brought about drastic reforms, is
under the Southern Cross again. He was in
Singapore last month—quite well again,
thanks, after a period in the trenches and all
the experience he wants of being buried alive
by a “crump.”
The Eastwood branch of the Red Cross
reaffirms its faith in R. M. Pontey’s fruit
and vegetable depot, which aims at filling
the bags of soldiers’ wives with something
to help the attenuated chops go round. Also,
glad tidings come of a new bequest. The
Peter Almonds, of Strathfield, have just
handed over the whole of their fruit-crop at
Pennant Hills. This runs to about 70 trees
loaded with apples, peaches, plums, etc.
Peter was severely damaged at Gallipoli.
Hence his desire to help the little things the
Absent-minded Beggar leaves behind him.
Lesta Griffiths, the friend of lonely soldiers,
leaves next week for India —a chance for
someone with the same inclinations to take
on a job with gallons of gratitude flowing
from it!
As Christmas rolls nearer hungry institu-
tions send in their cries. The sisters in
charge of St. Brigid’s Orphanage, amongst
the green hills of Ryde, want help. Send
that pair of fowds —or five shillings will do.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, December 18, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
A Christmas tree is being prepared in Mel-
bourne for the children of Anzacs. Lady
Helen shot the idea into the air and Mrs.
Pearce, Mrs. Hughes and Mrs. Sellheim
caught it in their hats.
Margery Moss’s wonderful husband is now
Colonel Dare.. As most people know, he
left here - a mere lieutenant, but quickly
dashed up to a crown, and now, at the age of
27, is Colonel. Invalided out of the firing-
line in France, he has been given command
of a camp in England. The little girl-bride,
who went to London to flat alone, is over-
joyed.
Young Gilchrist, son of the leather mer-
chant who is President of the Leather Asso-
ciation in Melbourne, has been appropriately
transferred • from the infantry to the
“Leatherheads,” i.e., Motor Transport
Corps. The soldier boy has a rare tenor
voice, and’ was studying quietly and dili-
gently before the war in hopes of a brilliant
career.
Poor Young Stan Benjamin, lately re-
ported “killed in action,” was the gay
young bachelor brother of Leslie and a son
of the old Jewish knight. Melbourne people
knew him well as a yachtsman of great
skill.
Chester Manifold, of Talindert station,
Camperdown, is a lonely squatter these days
and begins to wear his years heavily. His
young son left school to go fighting in France
and his only daughter married naval man
Adams, who gave up the sea for sheep—-
until the war began. Then hubby rushed off
to find a ship, and is now in the North Sea
while his wife waits in England. Mrs. Mani-
fold also went to England to stand by, and
poor Chester himself had to come back from
a trip to sit up with the sheep and order the
cows’ breakfast.
Brother Willie Manifold, of Burrymbeet, is
lonely, too. His three boys are fighting, and
daughter Muriel dutifully followed her air-
man husband, Foster Rutledge, to the other
side of the world.
Camperdown runs a swaggah race meeting
in January; but there has been talk of can-
celling the 1917 outing as a wickedness in
time of war. However, racehorse-owner
McArthur; of Meningoort, wrote heatedly to
Mother Argus on the subject. And as
Mother runs side by side with the sporting
Australasian she had to lend an indulgent
ear to the angry sportsman; and I guess
Camperdown will have its wickedness.
One of the Huns’ victims died in Mel-
bourne the other day. He was Lieut. J. H.
Mackay, a young veteran of the 11th High-
landers, who was gassed in Belgium during
the early fighting. The poison stayed in
his lungs and he came to Australia’s warmer
climate in the hope of beating it. Sar-
goods, of Flinders-lane, offered him a soft-
ware job, and he held it until it was time
to go into hospital and die.
The worst of war-luck has befallen the
Toorak Campbells, who have had three
bereavements in as many months. Lieuts.
Donald and Walter were killed in action
within 10 days, Walter after having won the
Military Cross. The death of their father,
John Campbell, last week was partly due to
the shock of the double bereavement.
Judge Johnston is simply wreathed in
smiles since the glad news came along that
his doctor son had earned a Military Cross
by dashing out under a heavy fire and drag-
ging a wounded mate to safety. Dr. Billy is
a popular and a modest youth. His late
mother was a sister to Donald Carbine Wal-
lace.
Dalgety’s lias sent many of its big staff to
the war, so the return of young Len Butler
was made an occasion, especially by the girls
of the office. As the returned soldier passed
through, his skirted colleagues "pelted him
with roses until he sat smiling waist-high in
flowers and deafened with cheers. Lie lost a
leg in the Gallipoli campaign, and has just
been released from an English hospital.
Mrs. Mclnerney, the Major’s wife, has a
brother, Captain Mackenzie Taylor, who has
been on active service since the beginning of
things. Lately he was detailed for duty in
Egypt, where he found time to invent a peri-
scopic camera. That vague thing called the
War Office is alleged to think a great deal
of the invention. I’ve heard of another Aus-
tralian invention of which the same War
Office was alleged to think very highly. Cer-
tainly the appliance was used, and used with
fine effect, in the biggest desert battle to
date. One co., in fact, says artillery couldn’t
have been employed without it. But so far
the inventor hasn’t received even a formal
thank-you from the War Office. Nor the
smallest acknowledgment of any kind.
Tennis-champion Norman Brookes was
lately an hon. lieut.-colonel in England. The
other day London Daily Sketch mentioned
that “Alfred Deakin, the famous Australian
statesman, is his father-in-law.” Mrs. Nor-
man, of course, is a daughter of the South
Yarra Emmertons. It is Mrs. Herbert
Brookes who is Alfred’s daughter. You
couldn’t easily get two sisters-in-law to
exhibit larger differences of type.
The steps of the Athenaeum Club have a
particular attraction for the eyes of fair
damsels just now. The fact that they are often
adorned by a handsome young Russian offi-
cer —who is said to be seeking health in this
land —is responsible. He looks like the hero
of a Ouida novel, and seems to be compiling
a thesis on Australian womanhood to be pub-
lished later in Russia. In his spare time
he gives medical advice, and Billy Hughes
and the Russian Consul are said to be
radiating health in consequence.
Adelaide’s tablets: —
Richmond had its Gala Day on Saturday, the
result being a good cheque for the Repatriation Fund
and an altercation that nearly went from words
to deeds between certain damsels who were unsatis-
fied with the judging of one of the events. On
the same d;iv the Galways went to Brighton for the
annual meeting of the Blind and Deaf and Dumb
Assoc. Their Exes, received the glad tidings that
the institution had wiped £5OOO off its overdraft.
The Wattle Day League has handed over two
more motor ambulances to the Military authorities.
That makes 17. Salute ! I must tell you, too, that
the Wonderland fete brought in about £2500, very
little of which will go in expenses.
Nurse Atkinson, who was invalided home as the
result of overstrain, died at the end of the week.
She had never been well since her breakdown, and
was for some time a patient in Netley Hospital be-
fore she was well enough to stand the journey home.
A great worker and a holder of the Royal Red
Cross.
A women’s branch of the Farmers and Settlers’
Association has been formed here, with Miss Tom-
kinson as honorary secretary. W. G. Mills, Presi-
dent of the F. and S. A., bucked up the inaugural
meeting by declaring that in many branches of the
primary producing industries women had proved as
competent as men.
Lieut. Sandford, who left about a year ago for
England and got a commission in the R.F.A., has
won the Military Cross. He is the younger son of
the late J. W. Sandford. Professor Chapman has
been informed that his son, Lieut. C. G., of the
Royal Engineers, who was killed in Mesopotamia in
May, was mentioned in despatches by General Lake
for “gallantry and distinguished conduct in the
field.” Another item of military news is that Dr.
Ca-venagh-Mainwaring has won a double —his majority
and the Order of the White Eagle from the King
of Servia. He left' as captain of the Field Ambu-
lance of the 3rd Light Horse in December, 1914,
and hardly a man who returned from Gallipoli did
not speak of him without concluding his remarks
with a round of applause.
Red Cross Hospital No. 1, generally knpwn as
Mareeba, is to be closed, as it seems that the Aus-
tralian sick and wounded will continue to be treated
in English hospitals. The huts are to be passed on
to the Bedford Park hospital. The Red Cross Hos-
pital No. 2, at Henley Beach, is to be kept at work
in conjunction with the Y.M.C.A.’s Lady Galway
Club House.
Judge Murray, who succeeded Way both as Chan-
cellor of Adelaide Uni. and as Chief Justice, made
a long oration at the recent University Commern. —•
his first —on the virtues of his predecessor. What
a time the old Chancellor would have had on the
subject of Miss Kitson, who took a Law degree!
S.A. was the first Australian State to give either
.votes or University degrees to women; but, while
it has been getting scores of girl graduates in medi-
cine, science and arts, this is the first local LL.B.
lady. She is only 22, and, as you’ve no doubt read
before, she’s a daughter of detective Kitson, and
will be qualified to appear in court in another year.
Chancellor Murray, referring to the bequest of
books from the late Chief Justice Way, stated that
over 16,000 volumes had been selected. Incidentally,
Uni. students have done the old school proud by
winning one Y.C., one D. 5.0., two Military Crosses
and four other field .medals. Also three ex-scholars
have been twice mentioned in despatches.
On Repatriation Day, run on much the lines of
Australia Day, the takings were over £2500. The
Wallaroo miners have decided to help the fund by
giving a penny per man from their wages on each
working day, a scheme which will bring in about
£lB5 a month.
Freda James, daughter of the Hazelliurst
family, of Toorak, was married at St.
James’s, Sydney, to her soldier just before
he left for the war. The bridegroom is
Lieut. Irwin, of Adelaide, son of the late
E. LI. Irwin. Mrs. Irwin, who lives at
Brighton, journeyed to Sydney with Freda
and her mother and saw the newlyweds off
on their honeymoon to the Blue Mountains.
Sumner Locke, the bright Melbourne girl
whose “Samaritan Mary” novel has greatly
pleased Uncle Sam, and whose play, “Mum
Dawson, Boss,” is shortly to be staged in
Sydney, will on Saturday become Mrs.
Henry Elliott. He is a Ballarat lad who
now wears khaki. Early in the New Year
Sumner intends flitting to N’Yark.
Ivy Clarke Knox and husband Bob are
delighting in the squeals of a boy babe whom
the Stork left last week. If it were a girl
that infant could guess by one look of its
real lace fixings it had struck a rich house-
hold.
. Mrs. Gladys Benjamin, who advertised the
loss of a £lOOO necklace and got it back,
belongs to the wealthy Hart clan: The
family flourishes at Oharnwood, St. Kilda,
which in the old days belonged to the timber-
merchant Moore. Patriarch * Alfred Hart
pulled the house to pieces and added loggias,
grey-brocaded boudoirs and marble bath-
rooms. As a tobacco magnate, these feats
come easy to him. He lately built the big
building, Lizzie House, in Elizabeth-street.
Nancy Grice Howatson, with a small
daughter in tow, has trekked down, from
Bananaland. She has hired the Parburys’
house for town use, and will go to Frankston
to take in weekly supplies of sea breezes
with the parent Grices. The old people still
shelter in something much too palatial to
find a new owner in war-time.
Bowlers everywhere will regret to hear
that spruce little solicitor Louis Waxman is
lying seriously 7 ill in Mount St. Evius Hospi-
tal. He is the proud father of our champion
girl tennis-player, Meryl Waxman. Meryl is
the ideal tennis girl, and always looks like a
delicious mixture of peaches and cream. Her
blouse refuses to crumple and her hair to go
out of curl, so she is decorative to the end
of the hardest game. She is to be married
presently to young Dr. Roy,Lister, who has
seen active service with the A.M.C.
The medical man who has been chosen to
look after Shackleton’s men down amongst
the ice and penguins is pink-cheeked Freddie
Middleton, of Ormond College. He is a son
of W. 11. Middleton, of Ballarat.
Prime Minister Hughes has been basking
in the small-social limelight of late weeks—
fetes, bazaar-openings, prize-giving at girls’
schools, and so on. It is the sort of thing
which a certain kind of politician regards
as a pleasant and necessary part of the
great game. But the old Hughes didn’t. The
old Hughes was the bitter little cynic who
said of ’himself that he needed mercy, not
justice; the new Hughes who has been to
England isn’t easily worried by being told
that he is the most distinguished man in the
Empiah.
Mrs. Nick Fitzgerald is back from Eng-
land, whither she went to see sons Percy
and Frank. Percy—he is Majah Percy—
was recently married to the Dowager-
Duchess of Sutherland, who a few years
back was considered one of the beauties of
the place.
Alfred Levey, who went Londonwards in
the torpedoed Arabia, reached his destination
with the Clive Miller boy in tow and what
they both stood up and lay down in. The
Leveys won’t think of returning to Austra-
lia unless an avalanche of English taxes
drives them out. The family possessions
have all melted under the hammer, the South
Yarra house has been hired out as a hash
mansion and the dog has been given away.
All is over for the time being.
The Best People had to dive into the
nearest tea den when the adored Tommy
Cochran appeared on the Block wearing
a collar of striped red and white linen and
a red tie! But the exquisite young man
repented of his rash experiment and did
away with the dreadful thing. He ex-
plained soothingly to agitated inquirers that
he was inspired by the scarlet and white
awnings that have protected all swaggah
garden fetes since the floods began.
Four flapper hostesses gave an invitation
dance at Royston, Dandenong-road, St. Kilda,
last week. Aileen Roth well, Lorna Embling,
lima Joske and Mary Roxburg were the
charming parties responsible, and the supper
money went to the Mayoress’s Fund. The
girls wore lovely complexions and the dain-
tiest filmy frocks. The were in full
war paint. True, one busy young dame spent
so long arranging the flowers and turning the
ice-cream freezer that she was surprised by
the first guests in her linen overalls. But
she whirred away in a taxi through the inevit-
able rain and came back clad like two rain-
bows and a red-gold sunset. The decorations
were worth everybody’s trouble ; wreaths and
streamers of small, red-pink climbing roses
were everywhere and the big garden would
have been a paradise to the young dancers
only for the rain. However, the weather
saved the chaperons the usual fussing. There
were more than 100 girl guests and a fine
assortment of youths in khaki. The Bowes
Kelly girls and the-Lyells were there in_ all
their warpaint. Noel Creswell, a McMick-
ing, one of the Austins, a Currie and B.
Woinarski were other belles. The young-
soldiers were recruited from approved
families like the Grimwades, Baillieus,
Officers, Armstrongs and Abbots. Mammas
are extremely careful with their guest-lists
these days.
The fine weather having arrived at last,
the Open Sea Bathers’ League is peeling off
and getting into its costumes. There are
special instructors in swimming and life-
saving, and if you feel you can’t have a
dip without belonging to something, ring up
the League at St. Kilda Beach and demand
the key of the ocean.
This is from a man in the trade: —
A year hasn’t taught 'Australia much. Last
Christmas 1 wrote on the scarcity of toys. But
there are plenty this year. There are a few more
Australians —the good ones rather too dear for the
man whose pocket is light, the cheap ones too flimsy
for any healthy child. (You can guess where the
cheap ones come from!) Exploring a warehouse
the other day, I came across boxes of puzzle-blocks
selling retail at 3d. The cost to the distributor
would be about 21s. a gross! Another exhibit was
a set of doll-size kitchen utensils, all stitched to
litho’d card and packed in pictured carton—retail
Sid., cost ss. 9d. doz. And there are printed-tin
locomotives, engine and three carriages, packed in
carton, 3d. ; celluloid babies (Ilun pattern) at half
the old German price; motor-cars, 3d.; boxes of
tin soldiers, 3d. ; half an army corps, with A.S.C.,
machine-gun section, ambulance and bridging train
for about a shilling ! What earthly hope have our
poor old returned-soldier toymakers ,of competing
with that? We must protect them, or they are
■doomed.
The Motor Transport out at Royal Park
camp gave an open-air concert last Thursday,
and the G.-G. pair sat in the front row with
Governor Stanley and his little lady. Provi-
dence sent a faint moon and a soft breeze
without rain. If it had rained there were
enough Generals present •to have court-
martialled anything. The military concerteers
raked in £lOO or so, with which they intend
to buy musical instruments. They have
plenty of talent available, but so far they
have had to depend on their voices and the
camp piano, with Pan-pipes, penny whistles
or flutelets to help. During a recent march
through town, a dozen or so of the “Leather-
heads” were tootling merrily on these toys.
Lawyer Wilbur Anderson, a nephew of Gen-
eral Stanley, is one of the musical crowd.
Melba sent a merry little coo ee back from
the crest of a blue Pacific roller on the way to
Honolulu. She has caught the happy holi-
day spirit and glories in the success her little
Stella Power has made on a Melbourne con-
cert platform.
“Damaged Goods” aroused acute feeling
among the gods at Melbourne Royal the
other night. When Dupont mere uttered
her foolish reference to the fertility of the
poor, implying that the comparative rarity
of the well-to-do kid made it more valuable
than the low-born brat, something like a sup-
pressed snort was heard up above. Later
on, however, when’the nurse was refusing to
risk catching the disease from the dirty aris-
tocrats, the bob rush was able to ease off its
indignation materially. As a matter of fact,
it raised such a tumult of appreciation for
the nurse that the last words of the second
act were lost, and a gripping scene ended in
ineffectual pantomime. Whatever their other
merits may be, the “Damaged Goods” co.
would never make their fortunes in the
movies.
Melbourne amateur theatrical circles,
especially the feminine segments thereof, are
fluttered by the adventurous spirit of four
pilgrim sisters who are leaving next year to
beg fortunes of Unc. Sam. They are comely
Kate Handley and her pretty little sister
Isabel; Allie Robson, whose grace and
charm are assets in her present job of teach-
ing elocution and the dramatic art; and the
stout pharmacist Ethel Leon, whose solid
characteristics are inherited -from her father,
Sam Leon, K.C. They have all been associ-
ated with Repertory Theatre productions,
Isabel Handley having secured marked press
attention on more than one occasion. ■ They
are not in any sense stage-struck rainbow-
chasers, for each has saved sufficient of her
screw as a business girl to keep her well
afloat. If Yank theatrical managers are
blind to their merits, they will still be a
trip to the good ; and if their luck holds, at
least two of them may. vet be starred on tin-
covers of American magazines. If the worst
comes to the worst, Ethel can roll pills at
Toronto, where her qualifications as a dis-
penser will be officially recognised.

Hal Gye has been discovered wearing a
pale-grey, bridegroomy suit, a bashful, fur-
tive air and a brilliant dark girl on his arm.
His friends have just got wind that the artist
is married, but until Hal is caught and cross-
examined you’ll have to wait for details.
A Melbourne girl writes me that Hilda
Rix (you may remember that she left, here
some years 'ago to study painting, and has
since exhibited in London and Paris)
became Mrs. G. M. Nicholas in October and
a widow a month later. The poor girl’s
husband was Major Nicholas, of the Cole-
raine family. The marriage took place while
he was on a few days’ leave from the Front
to receive the D.S.O. from the hands of the
royal George.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.


DEWAR’S
THE THREE GREAT TESTS
OF GOOD WHISKY ARE
QUALITY—TASTE—AGE
Quality is the Essence of Goodness and Fing
Blending. It conveys that indefinable distinc-
tion, which makes a man a prince among his
fellows, and Dewar’s a king among Whiskies.
Excellence in Distillation is a Science; Pre-
eminence in Blending an Art. Dewar’s is a
Mingling of Choice Spirits. Quality stands for
Character, and Dewar’s for the ideal com-
bination of both.
Taste. The Taste of Dewar’s is the Golden
Key which unlocks all the pleasant avenues
of the memory. In Dewar’s Whisky there is
something creamy and yet not cloying;
something velvety and yet not heating;
something rich and yet not obtrusive; some-
thing crisp and yet not crude Strong and yet
gentle, Dewar’s is the Paladin of Whiskies.
Age
is the Crowning Stage in a series of fer-
mentations. Fermentation means Life. Age
dissipates its roughness, rounds off its edges,
and refines its character, leaving nothing
behind but the Rarest Elixir of Life. Dewar’s
is the Veteran of Stimulants.. It is ripe for
Promotion to your Palate.

LEAVE WELL ALONE.
CAPTAIN: “Have you changed the guard yet?”
THE NEWCHUM JUNIOR: “No, sir; the old guard teas doing the job so well, sir, I thought
I'd let 'em stay on, sir.”
Rabbit Expert”: I think this is the
right explanation of why hares forsake rabbit-
infested lands. When rabbits take a place by storm
they are followed up by such hangers-on as the fox,
hawk and crow. These vermin depend largely
on our cotton-tailed pest for food, and as the hare,
having no dugout, is more open to attack than the
rabbit, he is faced with the alternative of getting
out or going under. Choosing wisely, he prefers to
get out and make his home in a less strenuous
locality. Moreover, the ' human occupant of the
property does his share towards frightening away the
hare. When the rabbit becomes troublesome the
farmer wages incessant warfare on the tribe, with
the result that the hare is again mostly affected, for
the rabbit can evade the enemy by taking shelter in
his dugout and remaining there until the bombard-
ment has ceased. On the other hand, the hare has to
trust to his fleetness and cunning. Again, when a
place is netted, the rabbit, being somewhat of the
Darcy type, clears out, and once again the hare finds
his territory invaded by the “hoppers.” Not being
of an adventurous spirit, our long-legged friend pre-
fers to return to the place whence he came rather
than move on to unexplored ground. This is not
written by a city rabbit-o, but by one who was born
in that part of the country where the trapper places
himself far above So-and-So, M.L.A., as regards
weekly screw and intelligence.
* * * *
'‘The Reefer”; The natives in “Te
Pana’s” (B. 16/11/’l6) locality are more careful
than niggers elsewhere if none of them have heen
killed or damaged by falling eocoanuts. In my
part I have happened on to several such deaths and
many casualties. The palms grow up to 70 feet tall, and
even a light-husked, mature nut falling on one’s
head from that distance will result in something
very close to good-night, while a fully-grown green
nut with its husk loaded with sap needn’t tumble
a third of 70ft. to result in a funeral. Everyone of
experience amongst eocoanuts instinctively keeps
half an eye aloft whenever he is beneath fruiting
palms, and walks wide of the boles altogether when
the wind is strong. Hurrying along a native track
one night during a storm, a bomb suddenly exploded
at my feet, wetting me to the knees. It was a
green nut which had shattered to bits.
* * * *
“Pick”: A working bullock on Gidleigh
station, near Bungendore (N.S.W.), put in a rotten
time during the late rainy season, and demonstrated
again the tenacity of animal life. A boundary-
rider noticed the old fellow standing near a wire-
netted fence, but did not approach him. Twelve
cold wet days afterwards, the patrol found the much-
shrunken beef-stack still there. He had hooked an
inwardly-curved horn over the top barbed wire, and
judging by the trampled puddle underfoot and the
much-frayed horn, had occupied the term of his
captivity sawing to and fro between the posts.
When liberated he took a long pull at the creek
and then casually set about making up for lost
time on the clover flats.

THE BAD: "Well, buy a blanked dash motor-car, an' you'll damn soon learn."
1 .N.T.” ; Latterly I have had my ' faith
in the foolishness of the “divining-rod” business
severely shaken. It is possible to get “diviners”
nowadays to contract for bore-sinking on a rock-
bound, no-cure-no-pay guarantee. It is said, of
couise, that the diviner who works on a guarantee
only sinks in country where any shallow bore
would strike water. But the thing has been written
about and discussed so often that the poor fool on
the farm with a bit of spare cash for bore-sinking
might reasonably expect some brainy, capable
geologist to have come forward long ago and ex-
plained, in words of one syllable, just how the
diviner would get the same results without his con-
founded wand the inanimate piece of stick or wire
which is such an obvious insult to one’s intelligence.
Lately I visited a farm on the Yarra Flats, near the
liver, and heard a lot of tales from locals who had
spent money on diviners. The bore on this particu-
lai farm was put down by a diviner using a wand
of copper wire. He specified that the underground
stream was 4ft. wide in a certain place and duly
struck it at 80ft. —good drinking water, slightly
tinged with magnesia. Elsewhere in the same
neighborhood the diviner has put down bores with
equal success. “Yairs, y’ get water anywhere near
the river, o’ course,” is the usual district scoff.
But one Flats-dweller has a deep hole nearer the'
liver than any of the bores, and all he struck was
a lot of hollow noises and a damp smell. One
visiting diviner told a sceptical farmer that he
would get him water in a specified place at 90ft.,
and made the usual promise, on paper, to charge
nothing for the work if the water didn’t arrive. The
doubtful one wagged his head glumly , and said he’d
take it on if the contraetor’d guarantee him fresh
water. The borer took a chance (all the other
bores were good water, of course) and struck a
fine flow at 86ft. Personally lam sure that the
piece of willow or wire has nothing whatever to do
with the discovery of the water; but it would
gratify me exceedingly to be able to tell the guile-
less agriculturist who has faith in the foolishness,
even to the extent of spending money on his belief,
exactly why I think he is a .goat.
* * * *
“Byrimine” : Here’s one for the big-team
competition. Got stuck in Black Sam’s crossing,
Flinders River (N.T.), about two years ago with
12 tons of wool on the Flinders Queen. Owner, John
Conway ; driver, Ted Brierly. Crowded on 50 horses,
five abreast, and gave them every encouragement.
No go. Borrowed 40 bullocks, also enough wire
from a neighboring fence to make a strong cable,
which we fastened to the back axle. Hooked the
bullocks four abreast to the end of this. Seven or
eight volunteer drivers strafed both horses and
bullocks, with energy and anything else that was
handy. The waggon creaked, moved, staggered for-
ward and capsized. Shifted 20 bullocks to the off
side and pulled the old Queen on to her feet again.
Straightened things up, made another concentrated
attack on the unlucky quadrupeds, and finally
struggled across. Time occupied, one day; length
of crossing, 120 yards. Richmond (N.Q.) carriers
can verify.
“Daybreak”: Apropos the proximity of
our little duck-billed friend, the platypus. Gimme
the book. A- couple of years ago I was stunned to
see one waddling across an open paddock to the
homestead on our selection. The creek wherefrom
he had advanced was about 15 chains distant. Time,
midday. The men were at work in the yard shifting
a heap of stable manure, and plat, dived straight
for the diggers and banqueted hungrily on the
worms that were being uprooted. After a good
blowout he spread himself in the sun, taking no
notice of the bipeds or the inquisitive dogs. He
even allowed the boss to stroke him, while the

mongs. sniffed and licked him. Occasionally he
wobbled round the house and devoured any table
scraps that happened in the way. Whether the grub-
box in his own dug-out was empty or he’d had a
row with the missus I dunno; but he repeated the

DERELICT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Well, it’s twenty good miles to the station,
And the heavens are gleaming like brass.
Ha! it seems that with that observation
I have tickled yon laughing-jackass.
But I’ll stop his confounded guffawing:
And yet—no, I don’t grudge him his glee;
Though I almost could think his haw-hawing
Was levelled at me.
And he’s rig'ht if it is—for, by jingo !
I’m a fool to be riding to-day
\\ ith a beard like the brush of a dingo
And a skin that’s the color of clay ;
With a dirty red shirt on the shoulders
And a battered straw hat on the head
Of the man that admiring beholders
Used to speak of as “Elegant Ned.’’
Ah, well! their friendship was rotten,
As I found when it came to the test;
And my name they’ve by this time forgotten.
But —what of the rest?
What of those who once loved—devil take it!
That drop on my hand was a tear,
And the first for long years; but I shake it
Impatient away, for I fear
■Thai I know what they’ve said, and are saying,
Could I hear them to-day: *
“Oh, he’s only. the penalty paying
Of his chance thrown away—-
Life opened out brightly before him.”
Ay, I Know it! But where was the use ?
I was bound to go, more majorum,
To the dogs and the deuce.
IVas I bound to go? No; I may addle'
My wits with the water that kills,
But I’m not such a fool as to saddle
Blind Fate with my ills.
’Tis the rankest of folly—denying
The part that I’ve played.
I but reap what I’ve sowed, and I’m lying
On the bed that I’ve made.
So I’m sorry, Old Jack, that I blamed thee
For thy laughing at me —since, alas!
Thou art wiser than people have named thee—
And I’m the jackass!
Well, this South Land is strewn with the breakage
Of promise as fair and as fine
As once was my own, and the wreckage
Of ships that were prouder than mine.
What matters for my little shallop?
What matters my puny regret?
So I push the old horse to a gallop—-
And strive to forget!
N.S.W.
ALEX. E. MONTGOMERY.

Beauty Doctor Gives Simple Recipe to
Darken Grey Hair and Promote its
Growth.
Miss Alice Whitney, a well-known beauty
doctor, recently gave out the following state-
ment : Anyone can prepare a simple mix-
ture at home, at very little cost, that will
darken grey hair, promote its growth and
make it soft and glossy. To a half-pint of
water add loz. of bay rum and a small box
of Orlex Compound. These ingredients can
be bought at any chemist’s at very little cost.
Apply to the hair twice a week until the
desired shade is obtained. This will make a
grey-haired person look 20 years younger. It
is also fine to promote the growth of the hair,
and relieves itching and dandruff. — Advt. [2

IP STOMACH HURTS, DRINK HOT
WATER.
A SPECIALIST’S ADVICE.
“If dyspeptics, sufferers from flatulence,
indigestion, acidity, gastric catarrh, etc.,
would only take a quarter-teaspoonful of
bisurated magnesia in half a glass of hot
water immediately after eating, they would
soon forget that they were ever afflicted
with stomach trouble, and doctors would
have to look elsewhere for patients.” In
explanation of these words the specialist
stated that most forms of stomach trouble
were due to acidity and fermentation of the
food contents combined with insufficient
blood supply to the stomach. Hot water
increases the blood supply and bisurated
magnesia instantly neutralises the acid and
stops food fermentation; the combination
of the two, therefore, being extremely
efficient and infinitely preferable to the use
of artificial digestives, stimulants and medi-
cines. It should be mentioned though that
the form of magnesia referred to is quite
distinct from the sulphates, citrates, oxides
or the often met mixtures of bismuth and
magnesia, and to prevent confusion you
should ask the chemist very distinctly for
bisurated magnesia.— Advt:
“Ses”; I have noticed flocks of swans
night after night passing over South Sydney, follow-
ing always the same aerial track and going due east,
a course that would take them right out to sea.
They evidently all get back home before the milk-
man comes round. Does anyone know the particular
banqueting place? The black swan, like the musk
duck and the gnalgan (Nankeen night heron), is
never seen travelling by day unless hunted by shoot-
ers, but it takes long night flights, dropping into
waterholes here and there. As it flies heavily over-
head, with a soft whistling sound and uttering at
intervals its pleasant call-note, the white primaries
and under-surface of the wings are in striking con-
trast to the general glossy black that is presented as
it floast majestically on the water. It breeds in
December in reedy swamps, lakes and inlets, laying
its eight or nine pale-green eggs in a bulky struc-
ture composed principally of rushes. When it sees
the game-hunter approaching it slips stealthily from
the shallow cavity and swims away with its head
and neck stretched out on a level with the water.
When it has placed a considerable distance between
itself and the nest, it will turn and face the in-
truder with its head proudly erect. The common
herons or cranes travel by day, and so do the ibis
and pelican; but nearly all other waders and swim-
mers wander about at night. Now and again in
quiet country places there is a thud and a clatter on
a galvanised roof as the deluded birds, mistaking the
silvery sheen for water, swoop down upon it.
* * * *
“Ped” : Rabbit-trapping as carried on by
the cockies and -their families in Gippsland increases
rather than reduces the number of cottontails. Most
of the traps are set on rabbits’ dunghills, with the
result that, in nearly every case, it is the buck that
is taken. It is a well-known fact that the fewer
bucks there are the better are ma rabbit’s chances of
• increasing her family. About four years ago Gipps-
landers used to poison, and were on the slow but sure
road to exterminating the pest; but as the prices
for “underground mutton” rose the cockies took to
trapping, and now the rapidly increasing curse is
decreasing their lands in value. Rabbit-infested
country (especially country like Gippsland, where
burrowing is such an easy matter) is not much good
either for grazing or for dairying. It’s about up to
the authorities to make trapping—in Gippsland, at
any rate —a misdemeanor, or in a few years the place
will produce bunny and nothing else.

“Leo Lear”: “Bad Australian” asks (B.
23/11/’l6) if eucalypts are ever frosted in Australia.
Yes ; but very rarely. I’ve known them to be so on
two occasions. r lhe first time was in'June, 1908, in
the Burnett district of Bananaland. Exceptional
frosts that year cut down sugar-cane to the fringe of
the sea, and ironbark saplings 70 miles inland were*
blackened and. lost their foliage. Again, in July
this year, young stringvbark shoots were withered by
frosts in S.W. Victoria. Both instances occurred
near the coast, where a hard freeze is very rare, so
perhaps the stricken trees would not be toughened
from birth like their inland relations.
* * *
“Joe Bagley”: The heart of the Fijian
native is gladdened now that summer has arrived,
because it brings with it the bolollo season. This
peculiar fish makes its appearance twice in each
year, first in November, then in December. I wish
I could explain this striking phenomenon. The
bolollo will only rise on an exceptionally calm day,
but’ having risen it remains up until sundown, when
it disappears as suddenly as it appeared. The Lili
species in some parts arrives exactly one month
prior to the Levu species, which shows up only in
December. The flesh is very palatable.

“W.M.8.”: Recently in the Terry-Hie-hie
Valley, about 45 miles south from Moree (N.S.W.),
a cypress pine log was brought to J. A. Philp’s mill
which measured 54ft. long, and was Bft. 4in. in girth
at the big end. It produced over one thousand
super feet sawn timber. Beat that?

“Bilbie”: The McKinlay rush, on the
headwaters of the Cloncurry, was isolated by the
floods of 1870. Damper-dust, sugar and tea ran out;
but Bobby Towns’s store contained a small supply of
rice, cornflour, jam, etc., and a hogshead of gin.
The diggers called a roll-up, at which the solitary
John Hop proposed apd the storekeeper seconded that
the goods in the store be kept for the use of the
women and kids and that the liquor be jettisoned.
Carried by acclamation. The blokes who assisted
at that funeral were genuine mourners, and watched
with sad and longing eye as the sand absoi'bed the
final chop. The siege lasted six weeks, and bullock
was the only tucker the men had.

“Barcoo”: Which is Australia’s best
natural fodder? I’m nominating Mitchell grass as
the soundest,, most drought-
resisting, quickest-fattening
and best weight-producer not
only in .this continent, hut
in the wide world. Time
and again Yankees have
spent thousands of pounds
in collecting the seed and
endeavoring to acclimatise
it in America, without
avail. The same failure
attended the Fairbairns in
the Western District of Vic'.
The grass extends from the
western part of Queensland
right through the N. Terri-
tory and the Kimberley
(W.A.) to within about 100
miles of the west coast.
Here are some records by
beasts fattened solely on
this magnificent pasture. 'ln
1895, three fat wethers,
Afton Downs bred, won first
prize at Hughenden (Q.)
show, and when killed
averaged 9911 b. The same
year 27,000 Lerida wethers,
boiled down at Torrens
Creek (Q.), gave up 291 b.
2oz. of tallow per head.
Two hundred fat Afton
Downs wethers which were
frozen by Harry Johnson,
Townsville, and forwarded
to England, averaged 9111 b.
Three thousand Victoria
River (W.A.) cattle, brought
in by the Skuthorps, walked
2000 miles to Burrindilla,
Gharleville (Q.), spelled four
weeks, and then the first
hundred walked a further
300 miles to catch the train
to Muswellbrook. By rail
they travelled 500 miles and
went straight into the yards
to compete with lucerne-
fattened steers. The Sku-
thorps topped the market
with this and further drafts;
Australia’s fattest and heavi-
est beast was a Monkira
(Diamantina R.) bullock,
which footed it into the
Adelaide Show, took first)
prize and beat the record
weight by over 2001 b. for
any bullock slaughtered in Australia.

“Gee”: A teamster offered me a job to
bring in some horses. The pay was to be a fiver, but
he changed his mind and got an abo. to do it for £2.
I told black brother what I was' to have got. Said
he: “It’ll be all right, mate; you will get £lO
when I’ve finished.” So off he went, and when
about half-way back he scattered the mob. When I
had finished collecting them the teamster had to
pay me exactly £2O. The station abo. will do me
any time when it comes to getting even with the
man who is not on the level.
“Ojo”: I can tell “Bad Australian” (B.
23/11/T6) something about eucalyptus and cold. In
Central Otago (M.L.) some years ago a bad winter
killed thousands of the trees. It is not only the frost
that settles them; they seem even more susceptible
to cold winds, although on the highlands of Bolivia
a few are to be seen that have withstood the rigorous
BELATED BET COURTEOUS.
MORNING JAGGER (subsequent to misspent
night): "Oh, y' there, are yer? Well, lis'en. Jus ’ tell m’ wife I'm mosht awfly sorry
but I shall -not — be — home — t' dinner — lasht — night."
climate. In Potosi, some 13,0'OOft. above the sea,
eucalyptus can be grown from seed, but the seedlings
have to be kept in pots in sheltered patios for the
first few years. Then they are generally planted out
in the patios, sheltered from all winds and most of
the frosts. When the- foliage rises above the tops of
the surrounding houses the vegetables seem able to
withstand all the wind and frost Potosf can produce
—and the city is no mean performer in this line.
In some parts of South Chile I have known eucalypts
to die of the cold, and yet the climate there is not so
severe as it is in the highlands of southern N. S.
Wales. Incidentally, the Central Valley of Chile —
practically all irrigated—is adorned with rows of tall
poplars along the edges of nearly all the paddocks.
These trees are grown so close together that they
serve as impassable fences. The value of the poplars
is considered to increase at the rate of one peso (say
9d.) per tree per annum, and 25 years after being
planted they can be cut down and sold for 25 pesos.
The wood is used for various purposes where light
timber serves. A Chilean avenue lined with poplars
is a glorious sight in autumn when the lovely red
blossom of its enemy, the quintral plant, is seen
amongst the autumn tints of the big fellows, and
the roadway is carpeted inches thick with golden
leaves. Nowadays - eucalyptus are often seen in
place of the poplars. They take only about 15 years
to reach the stage the others arrive at in 25 years,
and they are immune from the quintral. But it is a
crime against aesthetics to plant such trees in the
Chilean' Central Valley, which, but for them, is a
beautiful country with wonderful fertile soil, green
fields, vineyards (from which come really excellent
wines, not to mention chicha de uva), marvellous
avenues miles long, and many varieties of English
trees, incidentally, can anyone explain why it is that
Australia should be covered with such a drab earth
fungus as eucalyptus when all other countries have
trees that are a joy to the eye ? This ugly monotony
of so-called forest is quite sufficient explanation of
the conscription vote. I would vote No to anything,
after contemplating eucalyptus for a time. Fortu-
nately, the gum, when grown in other countries,
doesn’t look quite as bad as it does here. I have
seen some blue-gums in M.L. that looked fairly
respectable, but only when seen isolated from the
native bush, so as not to suffer by comparison.
“Boro-Belle”: During recent stormy
weather at Wooroon station, Wondai (Q.), 49 he<*d
of cattle were killed by lightning, which first struck
a gumtree, beneath which the animals were huddled.
When found in the morning the beasts were heaped
one on top of the other. I suspect this to be the
biggest lot of cattle ever electrocuted at one flash.
“S.A.”: There is a little plant in the
Central Western parts of N. S. Wales which I
will back against all other fly-catchers. It is a
small vegetable with yellow flowers, inside of
which are dozens of little red points. In summer
flies, ants and insects of all descriptions are observed
in various stages of demolition and decay in the
tight clutch of these little red tentacles.
“Barcoo” : Herbage springs most luxuri-
antly in West Queensland after the breaking of
the periodic droughts, which, incidentally, have
their virtue in sweetening the pastures after a
succession of good seasons. Of the herbage that
arrives after a long dry spell pig-weed is the
most rapid grower. 'lt almost chokes the Mit-
chell grass for a time, and sheep will have nothing
else while it remains on the menu. It -has the
additional advantage of being 75 per cent, moisture,
so that the beasts have the whole time to put on con-
dition instead of walking it off going to drink perhaps
five miles away. Its effect, though, on starving
MISTAKEN IDENTITY.
“Are you drawing my cat, Billy?”
“No. I’m drawing you in a temper wif me.”
sheep sent from drought areas and let loose sud-
denly on it is deadly on account of the undigested
mass in the paunch setting up fermentation and
causing enormous distension of the intestines, with
consequent inflammation and choking up of the
lungs. The animal dies in great agony. Starving
sheep handled by the man who knows are given an
hour on the fodder, then yarded for four or five
hours. In a couple of days the digestive machinery
is in good going order, and then all’s well.

“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Ancient mum-
mer Cowham is all out in his reminiscences
(B. 7/12/T6). G. V. Brooke and Barry
Sullivan never met in Australia. The former
left in 1861, and the latter only came when
he found Brooke had left these shores. I
cannot place Cowham, by the way. Cer-
tainlv he never played in early Melbourne
on the same stage as Brooke and Sulli-
van. If he did play with Brooke he is pro-
bably confounding H. N. Warner with
Sullivan.
“T. 0.1.”: Apropos the “School for Scan-
dal” (B. 7/12/T6). The greatest Mrs.
Candour that Melbourne ever saw was Mrs.
Vickery, nee Richardson, of Drury Lane,
imported by George Coppin to support G.
V. Brooke* Brooke, by the way, many
times played Joseph Surface. Mrs. Vickery
. was also excellent as Lady Macbeth,
Emelia and Volumnia. ■ In stately, matronly
characters she had no equal. Incidentally
she fell down some steps at the rear of the
old Theatre Royal, Melbourne, broke a leg
and was laid up for a long time. On her
recovery, or partial recovery, a benefit was
given her. Leaning on the arm of Brooke
she thanked the audience. But she did not
live long after, and was buried in Melbourne
General Cemetery.
Still another darling of the gods has
answered “Here!” Wallace Brownlow has
enlisted with the A.M.C. in (Queensland for
special service.
“Dr. Bloque”: Dr. Nil’s case (B.
30/11/T6) is an interesting testimony to his
professional skill, but inconclusive so far as
the teachings of “Damaged Goods” are con-
cerned. I have seen a good deal of the
results of syphilis in a new generation, and
would prefer to watch that “fine kid” for a
few years before cheering about anything.
Three instances in this line more or less
irrelevant to “Damaged Goods” recur. A
gay youth became engaged to a wealthy girl
during the roaring Melbourne boom days. A
medical friend who knew his condition sug-
gested deferment of marriage and vigorous
treatment. The suggestion was rejected)
the .marriage took place; and after six child-
less years of chronic invalidism the wife
underwent an unavoidable operation which
negatived all chances of future offspring.
That was gonorrhoea. A medical man
attended the wife of a wealthy business man
during her confinement. The child was born
dead. A month afterwards the doctor noticed
a sore on his own finger, and felt ill and
out of sorts. Before the condition was recog-
nised he had infected his young wife. That
was syphilis. The last of these gruesome
reminiscences is one related by Bronardel,
the French pathologist. A young shop-
keeper in Marseilles saw his wife off for a,
holiday, and on the way fell in with a former
best girl who had joined the tarting profes-
sion. In a house whither they adjourned
she died suddenly. He was arrested, and
released after police inquiry of the explosive
Continental variety. His wife left him and
began legal proceedings for the recovery of
her marriage portion. This ruined his busi-
ness. Meanwhile he developed all three
forms of venereal disease one after the other
and wound up by committing suicide.
“Glen H.” : A ’Frisco paper contains an
account of “a beautiful and petite young
woman” who was seen to plant a kiss on
the face of a nigger with whom she had been
in earnest conversation in the street. The
paper mentions that the lady was “Miss
Daisy Jerome, an Australian actress, who
arrived here a few days ago.” The accom-
panying yarn was to the effect that Daisy
had lost a priceless diamond and sapphire
brooch, and the cullud pusson, having seen
an ad. relating to the missing gaud, had
taken the opportunity of returning it to
Daisy, in full view of her press agent, and
had nobly refused a, monetary reward. Inci-
dentally, it looks as though Daisy holds the
opinion that if she can’t boom herself by
kissing niggers, she should at least be able
to do the trick by mentioning that she is an
“Australian,” which, of course, is very flat-
tering to this part of the Sufferin’ Hemi-
sphere.
“Reel Five”: Where do the old films’ go
when they die? To “the Islands,” my boy.
And “The Purple Sin” and “Where Are
My Wife’s Relations?” are so badly
scratched by the time 75 suburban and
country operators have done their worst
with them that the simple savage has the
idea that it is everlastingly raining in the
countries where the things are concocted.
“Norbell”: Surely “De Oriel” puts too
low a price on the second-hand belltopper
that the melo. mummer got at the popshop
(B. 30/11/T6) ? Three such lids for a
mere dollar! I went to Ikey myself not
long since to purchase a similar absurdity
for “The Worst • Woman’s Wishbone,” and
couldn’t get one at any price. “All snapped
up by the undertakers,” said Uncle. Mutes,
mimes and an occasional troglodytic M.L.C.
are about the only wearers of the prepos-
terous topper nowadays. On the stage I’ve
seen the same shiny tile do duty for seven
different wedding-guests. The dodge was
worked at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, years
ago, when poor Arthur Dacre “doubled” his
cady with the late comedian, Jack £>weeney,
in Grundy’s “A Bunch of Violets.” It was
not a property lid that time, but the radiant
stovepipe that Dacre ahv s wore in the
street.

Dearest Sir, —Reminding to your memory the

will show the entirety of Mr. Chaplin. He will

brim will be replenished.
It was done in French, too.

“X.Y.”: Mention by “Analyst” of the
desirability of thoroughly investigating dyes
produced from local material, reminds the
writer of the home-made colors he saw used
in his childhood. His youth was spent in a
community of which a considerable propor-
tion had been small sheep-farmers in the
highlands of Scotland. There the people had
been accustomed for ages, not only to spin
and weave their wool, but also their flax.
In Australia, while Scottish settlers no longer
did any spinning, much less weaving, they
continued to apply their skill in the pre-
paration and use of dyes for skins. These
were home-made, and were also used with
perfect success upon vegetable matter, such
as corn-huSks and cabbage-tree plaits. That
they were “fast” is evidenced by the fact
that hats upon which they were used wore
out before the color. Black was produced
by a decoction of the leaves of the garden
fig and also from the spatlies of the plan-
tain bunches and the wild apple. One color
was highly esteemed, especially by those
who came from Skye. It was bright golden,
prepared from the young shoots of the pop-
lar tree, and was the only tint besides
black used in the McLeod tartan. Many
years after I had nearly broken my neck
collecting the material for its preparation, I
was amused to read, in a German techno-
chemical formulae book, directions for making
this “new and valuable dye.” The valiant
women who first made it are all dead, and
their knowledge apparently died with them
so far as Australia is concerned.
* * * *
“Snell”: Travellers in a Tait carriage
going into Melbourne the other afternoon
had an unusual entertainment. A second-
cbmer had greeted a man who was alone in
the next compartment, flinging him a surly
good-day. Then followed a few constrained,
commonplace remarks, ending in a brusque
demand for settlement of an account. Hot
words' followed. The debtor was understood
to say he would see the creditor eternally
burning before he would pay. There was a
scuffle and a fall. When the next compart-
ment arrived on the scene the creditor had
the debtor down, and was fumbling about
his face. Apparently he succeeded in his
mission, for he was next seen to hook a full
set of false teeth out of the mouth of -the
prostrate wretch. These he put in his
pocket; after which he moved off and seated
himself elsewhere. He explained that he
was an unpaid dentist who was getting his
own back.
* * * »
“Chloe” : A likely paper-making material
to experiment with is the fibrous stems of the
Scotch thistle—the pest which in parts of
Australia reaches a height of from four to
six feet. Along the banks of many Gipps-
land rivers are acres of good ground covered
with the vegetable Black Watch. The fibre
is equal to that of flax, and should certainly
have a commercial value. The Huns have
already succeeded in making a good rubber
from the milky juice of far inferior speci-
mens, and investment in thistle plantations
might yield a better return than shares in
rubber estates in the tropics. In Australia
you cannot stop the growth of the rubber-
producing thistle. In many parts of the
Island tropics the rubber tree, which grows
so well in prospectuses, cannot be induced to
flourish. Even when it decides to live, no
yield can be obtained for at least four years.
The thistle reaches its maturity within a
year.
“F.N.T.”: I’ve been digging into the
finances of the milk business. On the farm
Strawberry and Daisy are assembled in the
morning, hand-fed, milked and removed to the
paddock. Same at night. The milk is put
through the cooler, canned and sealed, and
then carted five miles to the railway station.
Meantime, of course, the cow-crops are being
grown, chaff is cut, cans scalded, horses
toileted, and so on. The dairyman’s job con-
sists mostly of sending the boy to the depot
for the milk and running round with it to
the customers. The dairyman charges sd.
a quart (it was 6d. until the other week). Of
that the dairy-fanner gets only 2d., out of
which he has to keep his cows and pay for
his farm. The distributor merely has to have
a horse and cart and a couple of cans. The
farmer has to have them, too, in addition to
all the rest of the plant. Then we wonder
why the young men, instead of staying to
make fortunes on the land, come up to the
city and take a cheap job on the trams. Co-
operation, co-operation in buying and selling
—there’s no hope for a prosperous rural
population without it. Why don’t Agricul-
tural Departments take that job in hand?
* * * *
“X.Y.” : Steps are being taken in Maori-
land to form a company for the purpose of
making paper from the local pine timbers.
While N. S. _ Wales has not a sufficient
supply of native pine or other soft white
brush timber to encourage similar enter-
prise, . we have a timber as good, if not
superior, in the blackbutt. When dry, un-
painted blackbutt is exposed to the sun and
rain, a rough natural pulp is produced upon
its surface by the action of the weather.
This pulp is yielded in large quantities ;
wherefore a country producing the timber is
infested with hornets which build paper
nests. This paper is tough and durable, and
much. resembles the old hand-made article.
Certainly, it is of a grey color, but
could be readily and cheaply bleached white.
The best of the blackbutt country lies
between the Manning and Macleay rivers and
extends from the coast for about 20 miles
inland. The timber has the added value of
being the most rapid grower of all the
eucalyptus family. In 1898 the writer saw
mill logs 70ft. in length, with centre girth
of Bft., which had been cut on what had been
absolutely clear wheatfields on the Lake
Innes estate. These trees must, therefore,
have grown from seedlings since the early
’so’s.
* * * *
“Karaji”: The people who are investigat-
ing the properties of Australian timbers for
aeroplane purposes are overlooking l - the best.
Our black brother centuries ago discovered
that the mangrove supplied the lightest and
toughest material for making shields. Those
made of hardwood wei-e only three inches
in breadth, because if made larger they
would have been too unwieldy. But by using
mangrove the warrior was enabled to have a
protector about a foot in width, three feet in
length and three-quarters of an inch in depth
at the thickest part. No spear would pierce
it, no waddv crack it. This valuable quality
is due to the fact that the fibres run in
three different directions, and that the direc-
tion of growth of each layer differs from
that overlying and underlying it. Why this
should be so no botanist has yet explained,
but I wouldn’t bother about that if I was
thinking of manufacturing aeroplanes.

rium. Thus his letter to a creditor:—
Mr. Wooling. Dear Sir, — I receive your notes
these morning. I am sorry that I cannot undertake
it the arangment or the order for your stuffs vvhitch
I buy from you. In regarding your letter sented
to me it make me dispointed. Why! Because you
right enough the trouble is the purchas money
through to my famely Macrath have not paid to the
Board yet. Well, poor fellow couldn’t do anything
at present because we are -in bad time now I tell
you Mr. Wooling. Don’t tried to stuff me up with
some other people debt why you mind your Own.
Let them do what they likes for their own debt.
If they summons me I know the new act now they
can’t put me in jail if I debted up in the war time.
But those before the war started they might do
that on to me. Mr. Wooling I know too much for
those sort of business. Therefore you must be quite
mind your own debt. That is all I am going to
tell you. — Yours trulev, Rawiiiri Te Toartht.
