A Maoriland trader was handed some-
thing new in the way of a guarantee re-
cently by a Japanese merchant. It was a
sword. “If,” said the Jap, “I do business
with you and I fail to supply you with the
proper article, send the sword back to me,
and I will commit hara-kiri.”
* * * *
“Yarra Ben”: The Greeks score again!
The swarthy sweaters can keep their Mel-
bourne soft-drink germ-dispensaries going
till practically any hour they please. But
when Farthing, of the Assembly, moved an
amendment of the Liquor Bill to allow hotel
bars to remain open after 6 o’clock for the
sale of non-intoxicants, it was turned down
by 29 to 19 votes. Thus the door is banged
on the native-born trader and opened to the
oily alien who sits on the fence and winks
the other eye.
* * * *
“Y.Z.”: Lately I have reason to believe
that there is no longer any lack of piratical
enterprise in the Melbourne tradesman. Only
by exercising eternal vigilance can a woman
escape being “done in” day by day. Accounts
are almost inevitably wrong. Discovery
never discourages; Mr. Tradesman comes up
smiling every time with his happy, hopeful
little errors. If you turn him down it is only*
to find the other fellow at the same game,
and perhaps less cordial in accepting correc-
tion at your hands. I believe war condi-
tions have lifted chicanery into an unswerv-
ing, almost general, blushless policy, and
loosed upon the unwary housewife a ravenous
class of Get-Rich-Quicks who have even
mouldier morals than Wallingford.
BROKEN HILL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Broken Hill’s the place for silver,
Goal of many a thirsty quest ;
I who seek for spending silver
North and south and east and west
Never yet have quite attained it,
Though I keep on tramping still;
There are lots of broken bottles
On the road to Broken Hill.
I have knocked about Australia;
It is worth a knock or so ;
And my quest is simple silver —
Spending silver—as I go
To wherever toil awaits me
That an empty purse may fill;
But there’s lots of broken bottles
On the road to Broken Hill.
’Tis a wide and golden country
From whichever end you start;
I, who’ve tramped its many highways,
Know its best and worst by heart;
Bitter disappointments meet you,
And their stress ‘seems like to kill —
There are lots of broken bottles
On the road to Broken Hill.
Oh, it matters not your labor:
You may work at driving piles,
You may tramp through reams of paper,
Or may write in dusty miles ;
’Tis a search for spending silver
Keeps the old world jogging still,
For there’s lots of broken bottles
. On "the road to Broken Hill.
Some have reached the silver city,
Where they rest in pleasant shade;
But in cheques upon the morrow
Is the bulk of mankind paid.
What’s the odds? Although the finish
l)Iay behold us tramping still —
Well, we’ll see the broken bottles
On the road to Broken Hill.
N.S.W.
HAMER.
THE SIMPLE LIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In memory of the late coal strike and in anticipa-
tion of the next.
Progress is shedding her • petticoats pink,
Loosing hen ribbons and freeing her feet;
Naked as Nature she runs with a wink,
Tripping the, folk in the darkening street.
We’re all going bach into Eden again —
Heigh ho! for the leaflet that swings in the rain!
Work has a notice “To Let’’ at the door,
Hunger is frying an egg in the yard,
Drums of djsaste'r may rumble and roar—
What does it iftatter when Folly’s on guard?
We’re all gointj' back into Eden again —
Ilcigh ho! for the apple that falls in the rain!
N.S.W.

SPLENDID REMEDY FOR
CHILDREN’S COUGHS.
Mothers all over Australia are making in
their own homes as good a remedy as money
can buy for Coughs, Colds, Whooping Cough
Croup, Influenza, Bronchitis and Sore
Throats. All they do is buy from their
chemist or store 2s. worth of Hean’s Essence,
and add to it water and sweetening matter
enough to make up to one pint. Such a
quantity of ready-made cough mixtures
would cost at least 12s. The beauty of the
Hean’s Essence mixture is that it contains
no paregoric, laudanum or other opium
drugs, which are apt to do untold harm to
young children. — Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Asquith is supposed to be anything from
£300Q, to £10,000 a year poorer, apart from
his official salary, through losing the Prime
Minister’s job. No secret was made of the
fact that his father-in-law made him an
allowance “to maintain the position”; but
the clubs have never been able to agree as
to the amount.
In Maoriland the other day a wealthy
cocky, was so enraged about not being able
to get a bigger war profit out of his wool
that at a public meeting he expressed the
opinion that the Government should be
blown up with German bombs. Was he
rushed and charged with piracy or arson
or something? Nothing like that happened
—he was a wealthy cocky. Gut a couple
of days later an obscure wharf-laborer —a
man with 15 years’ recent service in the
navy and good discharges—was overheard
to make some foolish remarks to his fellows
about the unrighteousness of war. A belli-
cose beak told him to pay £20 or go to gaol.
We are taking up new industries and
.making ourselves self-contained all right.
The other week-end three Jap boats landed
5500 tons of cargo at Melbourne. One little
lot was 1500 tons of sulphur, and that’s a
thing we must import, because there are pro-
bably not more than a hundred million tons
of it anywhere in the Commonwealth.
HIS LUCK HAD CHANGED.
STEWARD : “Afraid ice'll be tied up when
we reach Sydney, sir; there's a coal
strike on.”
SICK PASSENGER : “Thank Heavens!”
Holman says he can only go upon what his
financial advisers tell him about the London
money market; and of course he can give no
assurance that he will get what he is asking
for. In short, what can we know of England
who only England owe?
Thri cost of examining the leak of cash at
Canberra amounts so far to £4OOO, and will
run into about £6OOO by the time the thing
is over. Which is another cheerful illustra-
tion of lmw to continue making a mess. The
habit all over Australia is to lirst get rid of
money furiously; next to wait until a loud
smell arises : then employ various persons to
find out what has been happening. The
final process is to put all the stationery and
accumulated knowledge in the ice-chest, let
bygones be bygones, and start another
splodge at a new address.
WAITING AT THE CHURCH.
BETHMANN-HOLLWEG : “It seems, your
Majesty, that the beautiful Peace is not
coming to the wedding. Perhaps she
objects to the Church being in ruins.”
The Senate the other day cut down the
vote for Supply to the Hughes Ministry from
three months to two ; and there was a blaze
of protest in consequence. Yet the Senate
was not only absolutely within its rights, but
did the proper thing. What Hughes wanted
was to get into hiding again until April. The
Senate reckoned that a spell until February
would be long enough. There can be no
doubt about this. During the last two years
the proceedings of the Federal Parliament
have been one long shirk, with the House of
Reps, and the Senate shut and the Ministry
shovelling out cash free from control or
criticism. During the last 12 months the
sitting days have numbered only about a
score. The plea that members should have
more holidav to jo “recruiting” was dis-
honest and irrelevant. There is no more
reason why they should shut up shop than
why cobblers and merchants should. These
two and a thousand others are just as much
interested' in recruiting ; arid their assistance
is far more ’likely to be beneficial.
One point in the N.S.W. Government’s
proposed liquor reform deserves applause.
It is that the Local Option vote shall be
taken on a day other than that of the Par-
liamentary election. This is a step towards
keeping the issues clearer. The tremendous
outpouring of talk and literature on the
liquor question for a few weeks before the
poll tends to make even the gravest citizen
forget the essentials of the political con-
troversy. In Maoriland the long wrangle
between Cold Tea and Booze thoroughly
obscured politics; and the big borrowings of
Ward and others went through without pro-
test, while the to be or not to be of the pub
held the whole stage.
Why all this portentous mystery concern-
ing Gregory Wade and his health and his
trip to America, and the vacant job of N. S.
Wales Agent-General, with Holman making
obscure remarks about being hopeful that
Wade will soon get better and serve the
State in another sphere? Why not be honest
about the other sphere and say at once
what the docs in the streets are. barking !
It would be more decent and friendly tor
Holman to arrange for wade to go away
decorated with the fruits of the Grab than
to let him sneak off with a new sphere in a
surreptitious suit-case.
At the time of writing. Dugald Thomson
is the only one of the GO politicians and ex-
politicians who has returned the life railwaj
pass which Kink O’Malley sent out just
before he went out.
Britain whipped out
a card that might well
have been played
earlier when she noti-
field Uncle Samuel
that she could not
guarantee bunker coal
at British ports for
any vessel that was
not being used for the
benefit of the Allies.
The notification is a
polite hint that the
Yankee trader who
traffics with the
enemy through neu-
tral countries will
have to carry his coal
in his pocket, so to
speak, which will
leave less room for
other things. But it
is also a hint that
Britain, in the un-
speakable folly of her
too-aii'able rulers, has
been all along supply-
ing fuel to the trans-
ports which have been
feeding the enemy.
Tasmania is grap-
pling mightily with
syphilis. A while ago
the State Parliament
voted £5OO to fight
the disease, and in
the past year seven
sufferers were treated
at a cost of £134 17s.
4d. Six of them are
reported cured, so
things are looking
nn, and the turn of
the other 29,993 or
thereabouts will come
in good time. Mean-
while the Chief
Health Officer com-
plains that the only
legislation bearing on
the subject is some
inadequate and obso-
lete provision in the
C.D. Act passed away
back in 1879. But
the Lee Ministry is
too busy trying to borrow enough money to
make ends meet to worry about a little mat-
ter like public health.
The recent disruption in the House of
Reps, accentuates the isolation of George
Wise, the Independent member for Gipps-
land. Wise, who had squatted among the
Laborites before the smash, is now an enor-
mous and influential party of one, with the
Ministerial corner all to himself. This self-
banishment accurately illustrates his proper
position in the House. It is a sturdy inti-
mation to the effect that he owes no mechani-
cal allegiance to Hughes, Tudor or Cook.
When you come to think of it, Wise ought
to be regarded as our ideal Parliamentarian.
A man of high private and political integrity,
he is certainly the sort of member we would
be choosing in 75 constituencies instead of
one if the party g'ame were scrapped and a
system of elective Ministries established.
Even English soldiers are beginning to
suspect that there is something wrong about
the sacred principles of Cobden. Writes
Brig.-General Stone in the October Nine-
teenth Century :
The lack of technical equipment remained a very seri-
ous impediment to training for many months after
the outbreak of war, and was very generally attributed
by officers to our Freetrade policy having permitted
Germany to corner certain essential industries, or
branches of industries, and thus completely hold up
the supply necessary for Army purposes. This mad-
dening handicap to training during the first nine
months of the war converted any Freetraders who
could be found in the New Armies into ardent advo-
cates for making this country in the future indus-
trially independent of any foreign country, at any
rate in respect to every article which might conceiv-
ably be required for the equipment and armament of
our Navy and Army."
But the soldier stopped too soon. Britain’s
Freetrade did more than give the Empire a
stone extra to carry. It lifted that stone
off the Germans. It robbed Britain of
work —which meant soldiers and munitions
-—to give it to the enemy.
N. S. Wales Attorney-General Hall, afte
pointing out that the Commonwealth Con
stitution declares that the House of * „
shall last “for three years and no longe ,
shows a way of beating it. His plan p ov _
W. M. Hughes should ask the BrltlS k
eminent to pass a Commonwealth 1 arlia
ment Prolongation Bill through the
British Parliament, and so make the foolisi
political opinions of Australia a neg S
quantity. Presumably Mr. Hall, who w
once a ferocious Democrat, cannot appre
date the fact that in ignoring Australian
political independence he is putting the Con
monwealth into the Crown colony class ana
inviting rebellion. Probably if the request
were made to the British Government it
would refuse to have anything to do witn 1 •
But that doesn’t make Mr. Hall the les
mischievous.
Frank Tudor lately elicited the amazing
information that among a few thousand
Victorians who recently put in a month s
compulsory training no less than 74 were
proved German spies. They were sent to
a compound; but when the proclamation
was withdrawn the whole 74 were released
and are now pervading Victoria doing all
the mischief of which they are capable.
(P.S. —Astounding, isn’t it? Of course it
isn’t true. But it is true that the 74 were
venereal patients who were turned out to
do far more damage than 74 German spies
could in Australia. We are mighty care-
ful to deal with the spy. We are entirely
at the mercy 4,>f syphilis. For, because it
is a “sexual” disease, the law hasn’t been
game so far to compulsorily segregate those
afflicted with it.)
Anstey, M.H.R., is the distressed victim of
a very elaborate practical joke now being
laboriously worked in his electorate, Bruns-
wick (Vic.). Anstey having said there
was no further need of recruiting here, a
local intellect immediately set about collect-
ing a fund to send him to Europe to rectify
General Haig’s views on the question.
Anstey agrees to go if the money is forth-
coming, and the tragic comedians are putting
a lot of diseased energy into the task of
gathering pennies for the ignoble purpose.
So far, about £2O has been collected. The
effort should end there, and the money in
hand might be devoted to sending somebody
to Kew.

BRIGGS: “What did your wife say about your
stopping out so late the other night?”
GRIGGS : “ Don't ask me yet. When she gets
through with the subject I’ll condense it
for you.”
Joe Cook has a secretary whose salary is
paid by- the Commonwealth ; and as Tudor is
now leading a party it is suggested that he
should have a secretary naid by the country,
too. And what about Wise, who forms a
party on his own.? Why shouldn’t he have
a typist as well ?
WITH NIELSEN'S ASSISTANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Mr. Holman, in defending Mr. Nielsen, said that
the latter had come to Sydney for the purpose of
considering the whole position.”
Let us consider the whole position,
Viewing it round from every point.
All the State has gone to perdition;
All our fancies are out of joint.
Labor has split and continues splitting,
Liberals vote with us cheek-by-jowl;
And the hope of Cohen is sadly flitting
From the door where the wolf begins to howl.
AVar is about and above and around us,
Outcasts we with our backs to the wall;
And the hammers of fate are out to pound us
Into something painful and cheap and small.
Fish of the trawlers’ catih seems tainted;
Nothing is as it used- to be;
Office itself is a harlot painted
That fails in the luring of £ s. d.
Let us consider the whole position
AVith Nielsen’s wisdom to help us through—
Wise with American erudition,
He may tell us the thing to do.
He who wrote of the date-palm culture
And- studied the farming parched and dry
May teach us a punch to out the vulture
And make us a shade from the cash-wolf's eye.
He who has been where the graft-scent lingers
And politics bloom; like a gilt-edged rose
And something stays on the smoothest fingers,
He knows how to do it —he knows, he knows !
x.s.w.

NO MORE BROKEN CYLINDERS,
COG WHEELS, etc.
THE DAY OF THE EXPERT. To-day is the day of the expert—the man who knows every detail of his work
—who is thorough, efficient and up to date. The engineer who desires to reach the top of the tree in his profession must
be conversant with every engineering method which makes for speed, complete craftsmanship, and for economy.
THE ENGINEER WHO DOES NOT KNOW of the Oxv-Acetylene process is not abreast of the times; he is
at -big disadvantage, and leaves himself open to the charge of incompetence.
Throughout the world to-day the Oxy-Acetylene process is the latest scientific addition to every engineering
workshop, which, fitted with this process, has an advantage over the opposition impossible Jo estimate. * It is simple,
yet wonderfully effective; its price is trifling, and the plant is portable. Whether the broken parts require to be cut
or welded at the bench or by the roadside is of no consequence.
Tmt ~ work of Iron or Steel welding or cutting can be carried out with Oxy-Acetylene. It is revolutionising
f th . e Engineering trade. Broken parts that once were useless can now be successfully welded and made new
again. This, too, in the matter of minutes. Cost is a matter of pence as compared with the expenditure
of pounds in the old method of buying new parts and making new drawings and castings.
All Engineers should at once write us for free catalogues and illustrations showing the remark-
able variety of work that can be done with Oxy-Acetylene. Following are a few of the broken
and worn machinery parts our process w ill make new again:—-
Cracked or broken Aluminium Crank or Gear Cases, Differential Boxes, Steering Wheels, Pipes,
Cylinders, Pistons, Cranks, Clutches,' Cardan Shafts, Band Brakes, Worn Key ways in Shafts.
Pieces welded to broken Gears and New Teeth Cut, etc.
Broken Machinery of any kind can be welded, including Cast Iron, Wrought Iron, Mild
Steel, Brass, Copper, Gun Metal, etc., which cannot be repaired by any other process.
The latest authoritative work on the subject, “A Practical Manual of
Autogenous Welding,” published by Charles Griffin & Co., London, states:
‘‘Within the last few years welding by oxy-hydrogen and oxy-acetylene blow-
pipes has been applied in thousands of workshops.” Those workshops which are
rapidly adding the process to their equipment include manufacturers, con-
tractors, machine shops, garages, blacksmiths, boilermakers, repair shops,
foundries, steel mills and railroad shops.

Some idea of the extent and continual
growth of the motor-car and motor-tyre industry in
Canada and the U.S.A. can be obtained from a study
of the production figures for the Goodyear Tyre and
Rubber Co.’s plants at Akron, U.S.A., and Bowman-
ville, Canada. Based on the record for one day
made in June last, when 29,976 tyres were turned
out in 24 hours, it means that in one hour Goodyear
turned out over 1200 tyres; each minute twenty
tyres were manufactured; every _ third second a
complete tyre was finished. Imagine them shooting
at that rate out of a large chute into a huge bin,
and then keeping on in a steady stream for
hours. The average daily production is somewhat
under this record day, but even at that, the nuniiei
of tyres made and the material that goes into them
reads into such figures as are absolutely amazing.
.For instance, each hour one and a half tons, approx-
imately, of cotton fabric is manufactured into t>ies
—this is at the rate of fifty pounds per minute, in
each hour nearly seven tons of rubber and compoui c
are made into finished products, or about 250 .poun
per minute day and night throughout the year with-
out cessation. The Goodyear Company through con
bined production of their two plants, is the laig.
■ manufactprer of tyres in the world, a positio
attained mainly by the consistently high quality
their goods,, which are now more than proving
worth on the roads of Australia and .
SPORTING
NOTIONS
The N. S, Wales Legislature has passed
the Tote Bill, and in a few months the machine
should be operating on some of the courses. The
discussion in Parliament ranged around a variety of
questions having not much relationship to the
innards of the Bill—about whether gambling was a.
“sin” and all the rest of it. Also the proprietary
racing clubs came into the controversy, mainly in a
disguise which this page refuses to take seriously.
As the measure now stands it appears that any old
thing calling itself a racing club can get the tote,
although no concern which is not directed by Gov-
ernment proclamation to instal the instrument may
put it in. If a club is directed to put the machine
in it can omit to do so apparently at a cost of '£loo
a day. But the great blot on the Bill as originally
drafted remains in the Act, and that is the running
of the totalisator in double harness with MacShort-
odds. No one attempted to show that any advantage
is likely to be derived by the State, the public or
racing from having both the machine and Ikey in
operation. It is impossible to make out a case for
this double.
There is room, perhaps, for difference of opinion
as to whether the totalisator, as the sole medium of
wagering, would lead to more betting than goes on
at present with the books, The view Tiie Bulletin
has always held is that it would not, and even if the
result was to increase wagering the consequence
would be less calamitous than the method hitherto
followed. But that the addition of the totalisator
to the previous system will increase betting is
not doubted by people who know what the effect of
the totalisator’s use has been in Maoriland. All
through the prolonged argument advocacy of the
totalisator has rested upon the contention that its
introduction would enable racing to be purged of
the bookmaker —that it was an honest and fair-
dealing substitute for a crowd who would be doing
more useful work growing spuds. The plan adopted
in N. S. Wales is based upon the theory that Ik'e
must be maintained at the old address. The system
may lead to a certain class of book being blotted
r" t. It will certainly lead to another class coming
forward.
* * * *
In Bananaland until recently tote divi-
dends were not paid beyond the shilling, which
meant about ,£5OOO per annum extra to the Q.T.C.,
and in the case of proprietary racing John Wren
was the beneficiary. Boiled down it meant that if
there were 1000 on the winner or the second horse,
and the dividend worked out at 19s, lid., the un-
complaining investors received 19s. only, and lid.
to .the number of 1000, equal to £45 16s. Bd., went
into the coffers of the Racing Club or John Wren.
Not bad for one race. Think what it meant for 52
racing days in a year, with six races each day, and
two . horses on which dividends are payable in each
race. Fihelly altered all that.
* * * *
The totalisator is going to be a better
taxing machine in the Speck if the Lee Government
has its way. The State now gets only 1 per cent.,
while the clubs hang on to 9 per cent.; hut a bill
just in proposes to bump the State’s share up to
per cent., leaving the club’s lot where it is. It
also provides that the machine can’t be used by
proprietary clubs, which, if carried, would be a
nasty jolt for the trotting people. As to the broken
mone.yj in future that is to go to some charitable
object approved by the Chief Secretary instead of
being collared by the racing club. The bill is likely
to pass, though there is of course a kick against it.
“Mujik” : When people talk of £5,000,000
going through the totalisator in a year, of course it
doesn’t mean that number of distinct and separate
quids. In the course of its beneficent operations the
tote returns 90 per cent, of its holding on each race
to the successful backers. This oh at any rate a
large portion of it goes back into the machine (for
the man who is winning usually tries to do it some
more) and is distributed among other backers, less
the 10 per cent, each time. Racing clubs have
always speculated on this question of how many dis-
tinct pounds are played up on a day’s racing where
the totalisator is working. Supposing the total on
six races at the end of a day was £24,000. How
much was actually in the gamble ? Say the punters
took £12,000 to the course, and dumped £4OOO on
the first and every other race. How much do they
carry away from the scene of hostilities after back-
ing all the winners? Seems to me that, even if all
the races were won by favorites, you cannot make
more than £9600 of it. And after the next day’s
racing, if that lot were taken to the course, no mat-
ter how much might apparently go through the
tote, the public would only take away £7200, again
assuming that £4OOO went on each of six races. I
don't want to • find fault with anyone, but backing-
horses is inexpressible foolishness, whether you do
it with Ike or the tote. After 20 rounds with a
citizen’s quid there isn’t enough left to buy a corn-
plaster in either case.
Unless there are strong influences at work
..in the opposite direction, there will be no expensive
' tote installations in N. S. Wales until the new
Exchange Electric machine has been put to the
test at Gawler (S.A.) ; and that is expected earl}'
in the New Year. It is the Gabriel patept, whereof
the Sydney and Melbourne racing clubs have been
duly warned. Its advantage over the original “one
stand” totes is that it permits of branch establish-
ments at various parts of the raceground, every bet
made at an outlying station being at once regis-
tered at the main station. AVhen the betting is
turned off at the main the out-stations close auto-
matically, and the grand totals are recorded before
the race is decided. If the E.E.T. proves a success
at Gawler, its installation elsewhere seems bound to
ensue. Stead and other Maorilanders who have seen,
the model at work are bitten with the idea.

Twelve months ago Foil (Bright Steel —
Quatre-foil, both imp.) was prominent in Sydney
racing because he appropriated the Summer Cup with
6st. 91b. on board. Now it looks as' if he may
struggle into the glare again on Boxing Day, with
only ■ nine pounds extra to hump. Foil is a more
than useful gelding, and the fashion in which he
appropriated the Warwick Farm (Sydney) Handicap
on Saturday, with 7st. 121 b. in the saddle, suggests
that very little more improvement will be necessary
to land him among the placegetters in coming big
events. The erratic Karamand King was second to
Foil at the Farm, and he, too, is a possibility in
the near future. First and second in this event
were ridden by brothers —AY. and A. Callinan.
* * * *
If eA r er there looked a racehorse certainty
it was Robkin in Warwick Farm Juvenile Handicap
on Saturday. This colt (lie is by The Welkin)
started favorite in the Breeders’ Plate at Randwick
in the Spring and led well into the straight before
he was captured by more seasoned youngsters and
ended in fourth place. Since then Robkin had not
been stripped for action, but all the same was given
a wonderful chance with only 7st. 121 b. to carry at
Warwick Farm. The writer firmly believed that
Robkin would start at ockls-on, and was consequently
looking for explanations when Ike offered 2 to_l.
Later lie drifted to fours, while Nancy Sykes, which
had won at Moorefield a week earlier, became favorite.
But Robkin won, despite his failure to begin nearly
as well as he did at Randwick. He will, win again.
He is in the December Stakes.

It is not often that 11. R. Denison’s colors
are seen in front at the business end of a race these
dues. But Australienne, a mare by St. Amant—-
Australia, the dam imported by Air. Denison, landed
the Farm Stakes at Warwick Farm for him on Satur-
day. She was lucky to beat Chain Lightning.
Heads separated the placed horses, Redmond, the
favorite, being third. Redmond, by the way, ran
third in the Maiden Handicap earlier in the day.
For that event he was also first choice. He must
therefore owe the people behind him a few dollars.
Australienne is trained by Tuckerman, who was fore-
man for Isaac Earnshaw. AVhen Earnshaw went
out of business Tuckerman stepped into his shoes.
The Denison candidates have always been under the
one roof. It was Earnshaw who prepared Poseidon.

In the struggle to make the races more
popular with owners the V.R.C. committee has cut
down the distances at next year’s Autumn meeting
at Flemington. The E--sendon Stakes is experiment-
ally reduced from a mile and a half to a mile * n 7
a quarter, the Governor’s Plate (late Loch Plate)
from two miles to a mile and a half, and the
King’s Plate from three to two 'miles.
Imported Torbane, who was a disappoint-
ment in both Cups, won a 10-furlong race at Caul-
field last Saturday. He started favorite and won
in great style in good time. Evidently the aged
Britisher can be quite brilliant when he feels in
the humor and conditions are favorable and there
is nothing very special to beat. The quality of the
opposition makes a wonderful lot of difference in
the running of some of these elderly imported ani-
mals. They do little things in the grandest possible
manner, but when the job is too tough they chuck it
up - . .
“E.T.”: The racing glory that once was
Ballarat’s has departed, seemingly for good. The
Miners’ Rest stable, from which Jim Scobie turned
out winners of Cups, Derbies, Maribyrnong Plates
and Grand National Hurdles, now shelters a couple
of “district” nags. At the Newmarket Hotel
stables, where the late Bob Howie trained Suwarrow,
the Vic. Derby winner, and Stockwell, runner-up in
a Melbourne Cup, pigs are being raised nowadays.
And at the place where Tommy AVilson prepared
Melbourne Cup winners Sheet Anchor and Bravo, and
many other notable flat racers and jumpers, there
is these times a dairy farm.

“Wart”: At the Newnham (Launceston)
Racing Club’s meeting (25/11/’l6) Grizette, owned
and bred by Thirkell Bros., won the two-year-old
race by a street, putting up a half-mile record for
Tasmania —48isec. This on a heavy course.
Grizette was ‘bred at Sandridge. She is by the
latelv-defunct Chiron . out of Marie Louise. Marie
Louise was bred in 1890, so was 24 years old when
she foaled Grizette. Her. best foal before was Pro-
gredior, her second, who won the Hobart Cup in
1902. Progredior was by Pilgrim’s Progress.

Women at Toowoomba (Q.) seem to
know something. The turf club there offered a gold

* * * *
Barrister Windeyer, president of Ma
State’s Golf Ass’n, spoke at a gathering of sporting
bodies held at the Sports Club, Sydney, last week
for the purpose of discussing ways and means with
chief-recruiter Mackinnon. AAondeyer remarked that
he had heard liorse-racing spoken of as a national
industry. He believed it was —also that the products
were: horse manure, flies, spielers and bankrupts.
This was fairly hot stuff, but it contains the ele-
ments of truth. No one not incurably blind can
fail to see that the racing business as it is run to-
day is cumbered with a heap of putrifying ' waste
products which are a disgrace and a menace to the
community. Unless this is cleaned up now the
result when Australia becomes sober is inevitable.
The good sportsman will get hurt with the other
one.
There are more ways of making money
out of horses than backing them with Israel. At
the end of the war the man who could put a few
thousand well-bred mares into Europe might find
himself a fair winner. The loss of horse there-
abouts, in spite of the use of the motor-car and the
railway, has been enormous. Millions have gone
down,' and the saving of horse life is almost as
important now in some countries as the protection
of human life. There are many kinds of transport
for which horses alone are useful. The longer the
war goes on the greater the wastage, because with
the killing or foundering of the best horses comes
increasing use of the other sort. Germany started
off with 6,000.000. After using them in war and
for breakfast how many are left?

Matt Murphy got it again on Saturday.
Jimmy Hill, the feather champion, and Matt were
having an argument at Melbourne that evening, and,
after Matt had lost his feet four, times in the fifth,
Hill ended the matter in the sixth by concentrating
the whole national strength into one supreme swing.
The collision with the jaw was emphatic. Matt
looked even less like a world-beater than he did
against Edwards.

*** 1 *
“LV.D.”: Ever see a fair dinkum go
between two old-nran grey ’roos ? They generally
select ■ a fairly open spot in the timber, level arid
not too hard,” and mostly a bit of sparring goes on
before the real encounter on the right spot is
started. Once there, however, no time is wasted.
Facing each other, and rising on their hind toes,
they begin a lively catch-as-catch-can for a hold
with the fore-claws on the neck, shoulders or fore-
arm. Once a fair hold is obtained each animal
becomes possessed with one idea, and that is to
poke or rip a hole in the other fellow’s belly. The
middle toe on the hind foot is sharp and hard from
constant contact with brush and sandy ground, and
the push it gets from a hind leg which is one mass
of sinew and muscle makes it a very dangerous
weapon. I have seen a dog ripped from shoulder to
flank in one stroke, and the resultant wound exposed
heart, liver, lungs and entrails. When a very severe
body blow is struck or both marsupials run out of
wind they make it the end of a round, and loosen-
ing the neck-hold both spring back out of each
other’s reach. If the round finishes on the ground
as it sometimes does after a heavy fall, thev take
out the interval as they lie. Not having any Stadium
fixings I fancy they just lie until sufficiently rested ;
then at it again, and so on till the finish. They
fight veiny fair, and as far as 1 I know rarely take a
mean advantage of each other. The finish is usually
brought about by a severe wound or sheer exhaustion.

103 and five for 24—12 wickets for 127 runs. It

St. Peter’s College, Adelaide, got from
Prince Alfreds the beating at cricket which has
lately been usual. Even Governor Galway, who
holds the scale evenly between the two colleges
was moved to suggest, when presenting the shield
to “Princes,” that “Saints” ought to win once dur-
ing his (probable) five years of office. He has now
finished three, and in all the events —cricket, foot-
ball, tennis and ‘‘sports”—since 1914 began, St.
Peters have had only one narrow win, in the
Sports this year. Rowing they are particularly good
at owing to the influence of late headmaster Girdle-
stone, an Oxford stroke (now living in Sydney) ;
but Prince Alfreds haven’t a boat on the rive*.

one of S. Australia’s best exponents of lacrosse. He

* * * *
One-time Australian cyclists are sweeping
the Yankee tracks. At two recent meetings every
event fell to riders who had migrated from this end
of the globe. Bob Spears (Dubbo) defeated Frank
Kramer (world’s champion sprinter) for the seventh
time during the season, and (with R. McNamara)
won the teams’ race (621 miles) and finished second
in the 25 miles teams’ event. Alf Grenda and A.
J. Goullett won that last lot. * *
Unless all the others were as slow as
British diplomacy in Greece, a youngster who laid
violent hands on the championships of one of the big
schools at Wellington (M.L.) the other day looks
like being something out of the box. His name is
Wilson, and during the afternoon he did this: Won
a heat and the final of 100yds. ; did ditto over
220yds. ; won the 440yds., half-mile walk and long
jump, and heaved a cricket hall 15yds. further
than ’ anyone else. Of course there is a good deal
of resiliency about a kid, hut if a medical man
took Wilson by the ear and told him something about
his innards it wouldn't do any harm. Four violent
sprints, a go over a quarter (the hardest of all
races) and three other performances all on the same
afternoon means calling tenders for a lot of trouble.
“Bedouri” : Re the moke versus man
endurance test. “Barcoo” (B. 23/11/16) is alto-
gether wrong in his distances. Bill Watson, of
Gurrawilla, old V.R.C. starter Watson’s son, would
best any horse easily. Bill certainly walked regu-
larly to the quarterly meetings of the Gregory
North Rabbit Board, held at Wonkiri, distance 65
miles (not 45, as “Barcoo” states), and made it
easily in the day between sun up and dovyn. Watson
once walked from Canterbury to Currawilla, leaving
the former at sunrise. He arrived at Currawilla
about 3 p.m., distance 50 miles, and offered to bet
anyone a tenner he could make Palparara (28 miles
further on) before sundown. He was a champion and
could beat the ordinary moke with ease. “Barcoo”
states Currawilla is 130 miles from Adavale; more
like 250. A legend exists that Watson walked into
Windorah, 120 miles, in a day ard a half to sit on
the Bench where a Chinaman was concerned. He
was “white” and hated Chinamen and Afghans. _At
present he lives a life of repose at St. Ivilda (Vic.)
and bike-riding is his speciality.
“Foolar”: “Alidon” (B. 30/11/’l6) and
others writing of bursting rifle barrels evidently mean
shotgun barrels. 1 have blown dozens of cleaning
rags that were tight enough to break a greenhide
“pull-through” out of my Winchester by inserting a
oartvidge loaded with bullet. I have had the rags
jambed from near the breech to . the muzzle, and
my quick and safe remedy was always a loaded cart-
ridge. Re “Alidon’s” penny-on-muzzle trick. He
can stack coppers as high as Haman’s scaffold on
top of my Winchester muzzle and I will guarantee
to scatter them safely with a bullet without injuring
the barrel. But keep all obstructions out of your
shotgun barrel when shooting if you are not after
a premature journey.

The superiority of wooden auto, tracks
for record-smashing was recently demonstrated by a
Yankee flier named Aitken. Driving a high-powered
ear in the Astor Cup, Aitken whizzed over 250 miles
in 2hr. 23min. 4 3-ssec., or nearly 105 miles
an hour. Alongside this achievement the two pre-
vious world’s bests —9? and 89 miles an hour on
cement and brick-surfared tracks at Brooklands
(Eng.) and Indianapolis respectively—are mere
dawdling.
The police have a queer way of reforming
the ladies who tread the gay paths of the
wicked. In a Sydney court the other day
a newcomer to the old trade was ordered to
find £lO as security that she would be of
good behavior in future. She was able
to produce the tenner and was handing
in the cash when the Ass explained
that she would have to find some-
one else to be her surety. Promptly a fat.
overdressed female, wearing all the marks of
her vile trade, produced herself and did the
necessary, subsequently escorting the recruit
from the court and introducing herself as
she went. How that sort of thing is going
to encourage morality it is hard to say.
LIKE FATHER, UNLIKE SON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
■ Although a bookmaker, I hate to think my chil-
dren should have any knowledge of gambling, and
as the father of three little ones I never allow race-
course matters to be discussed in my household. —-
Mr. O’Dea, president of the Bookmakers’ Association,
asking N.B. IV. Government to give Israel “security
of tenure.”
O Mister President O’Dea,
Mingle, I beg, thy tears with mine ;
Wicked are we, and we must pay,
But let not our poor infants pine;
I’ll keep thy secret from the light—
Let not my babies know I write.
A beautiful and blue-eyed boy,
Who nightly comes to climb my knee,
Blameless and pure, and full of joy—-
And oh ! he is so like to me !
But on his youth ’twould be a curse
If he but knew his pa wrote verse.
So, when he asks me: “What is Oo?”
Confused, I say, “A bishop, dear.”
How could I tell him that I drew
Cash for the stuff like this stuff here?
And so I say: “A bishop, sweet” ;
And scoot in quick shame down the street.
O Mister President O’Dea,
We both are in a shocking mess ;
If my child sees real bishops, say,
I-le'll know I’m not one by my dress ;
And you, by sign of hat and coat,
Suggest not deanship but the Tote.
N.S.W.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

“J.F.H.” (London) : Solomon Lazarus
Levi —now Sir Sydney Lee—has been show-
ing Billjim the historic spots of London at
the request of the Anzac Committee of the
Y.M.C.A. While piloting them around he
was frequently asked to tell them the best
and safest places in which to spend the even-
ing. Reasonable, he says, but a “puzzling
problem.” Solomon or Sydney is the lead-
ing living authority on Shakspeare and the
writer of the standard life of the Bard ; but
the only place where Shak. can be seen is at
the “Old Vic.,” in a slum region on the
south side of the Thames. Nearly all the
London theatres are monopolised by revues,
which the Australians told him they found
“tedious, vapid and barely intelligible
clownage.” They expected the playhouses of
the Empire’s metropolis to offer them sound
and exhilarating music and drama, and he
reckons that an “honorable obligation rests
on Londoners to do it.” Which is large and
vague and magnificent; but no banker would
finance a season on that security.
In the European Shock Exchange Kaiser
William holds the unique position of being
simultaneously beset by the Bulls and the
Bears.
Defence Minister Pearce reckons the
Allies are in a worse position to-day than
they were a year ago. But let us cheer up.
There are gleams like this in the general
gloom :—•
At the second annual meeting of the Pomeranian
and Toy Dogs Club the report stated that the past
year had been most successful.
South Aus. is getting into trouble already
over its proposed list of native names to be
substituted for everything German or
German-sounding. One township gets the
musical title of Wonnaminta. Somebody
pointed out that this means simply emu-
dung, and then the band played. In any
case, tlie name should have been ruled out,
because it is attached to a N. S. Wales
locality already.
“Tiger” : “Ouyen’s” statement that Mallee
pine smoke has been of great benefit to a
sufferer from tuberculosis (B. 2/11/T6) is
open to grave question. The case he men-
tions may have derived benefit from this
alleged remedy, but it is extremely doubtful.
Smoke of any kind is apt to be more harmful
than beneficial in pulmonary troubles, on
account of the irritation it sets up. It is
more than probable that the improvement in
“Ouyen’s” friend’s condition was due either
to the change to the air of the Mallee or to
the exercise—possibly a little of eafck.
Canada, tI.S.A. and parts of Europe have
advertised their pine forests for a number
of years, not for any virtue in pine-smoke
but simply for the air of the pine country.
Results in these forests have, however, been
not as good as those offered by sanatoria in
other districts. It would be a pity if suf-
ferers rushed to the Mallee expecting great
benefit from pine-wood smoke. They would
be risking cruel disappointment.
“Wealsman’s” story of a queer case:—
Recent Bulletin mention of the “only son” phase
of the military exemption question recalls a quaint
variant of the conventional. A youth, called up for
examination at 10 o’clock one morning, learnt from
his tearful parents at 8.30 the astonishing news that
he was not their son at all, and that, as the fact
that he was an adopted child might possibly be
known to the magistrate or to some of the court
offkials, his intended idea of “only son” would,
perhaps, miss fire. When he recovered from the
shock of the revelation, the youth, who had long
wanted to enlist but had refrained at his mother’s
request, urged the newly-discovered relationship as a
reason for the withdrawal of her objection ; but the
old lady wept a. refusal and made him promise
anew that he would try for exemption. In court
he put in the usual plea ; but the magistrate called
him up to the Bench and quietly intimated that he
had been informed that the applicant was merely
an adopted son, and therefore could not legally
claim to be an only son. Exemption regretfully
refused.
The lad was leaving the court, looking very sorry
for the woman who had been the only mother he had
known during his 21 years, when he paused, hesi-
tated a moment, then turned and walked straight
back to-the Bench, with the query: “If 1 am the
only son of my parents—my real parents—what
about exemption?” The magistrate gasped, frowned
and fidgeted with his spectacles, finally stammer-
ing: “Well, well, I—ah—well, I suppose so. Yes,
certainly, if it can be proved. Are von the only
son of your real parents?” The youth said he
hadn’t the faintest notion, but thought it possible
his foster-parents had. “Go home and find out,”
said the P.M. An hour later, just as the court was
rising, the youth came in with his foster-father. The
latter collogued awhile with the magistrate and
argued awhile with the military representative, then
went into the witness-box and swore to “the truth
of the statement he had just made to the Bench.”
Previous judgment reversed. Exemption from mili-
tary service granted “on the grounds,” said the
magistrate, as he wrote it down, “that he is the
only son of his parents.” The applicant left the
court without a stain on his character or a smile
on his usually cheerful face. He looked five years
older, and there was an unwonted glitter in his
eye. as the military representative brushed past,
annoyance in every stride and contempt in his stare.
“If that chap was a German, I’d go to the war to-
morrow,” remarked the youth bitterly. It seems
that the military rep. had asked the old man
whether the yquth’s real' parents now had any other
sons, and had been answered in the affirmative. He
had then triumphantly claimed that the applicant
was not entitled to exemption. But the old man
satisfied the magistrate that, while the lad’s father
and mother now' possessed other children, some of
them sons, the applicant was the only child of the
union of that father and that mother. The parents
had subsequently married other parties, it was at
this stage of the proceedings that the youthful appli-
cant for exemption, who was standing by, waiting
for the verdict, overheard the nonplussed officer drop
a remark. It was not a kind remark, and was not
intended for publication, but while Dad w'as climb-
ing into the witness-box the military man became
aware by a look that Sonny had heard and under-
stood. This knowdedge disconcerted him, and he
missed raising the point that as the applicant was
not bearing his real father’s name, the traditional
reason for exempting only sons —the perpetuation of
the family name—did not apply in this particular
case. And the magistrate did not think of it.
After all, it is perhaps as well for the reputation
of various persons and institutions that the tribunals
have ceased from troubling and the family tangles
are at rest.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

F.C.H.: Too rough to pass judgment on. Anyone

Anew form of theft is becoming common
in ’Roy and ’Wood, Melbourne’s two indus-
trial suburbs where the density of infants
gladdens the heart of Statist Knibbs. Prams
are being stolen, contents and all. The con-
tents are found next day at one of the local
creches, minus anything valuable they may
have had on —usually the gold “Darling’-’ or
“Baby” brooch which is the latest cry in
infantile bijouterie. The thief; of course,
has pawned or sold the pram, and a girl
confederate has dumped the kiddie at the
crSehe. One father, who lived in Easy-
street, Collingwood, but was not therefore
necessarily a millionaire, recently spent an
anxious night of search before lie found his
offspring, which had been collared, with its
pram, outside a shop wherein mother was
matching ribbons. The local police were
lately looking for six go-carts or prams,
each valued at £5 55.. and six parents Had
an axe stropped all ready for the kidnapper.
She went about in taxis
With men of every kind,
The little child was dying
All through a broken heart,
The mother's heart had hardened,
Almost turned to stone,
The baby child was sleeping
In her grave upon the hill.
Surrounded by pure white lilies
Where ne’er a thorn could grow,
Another heart is broken,
But now it is too late,
She sleeps among the lilies,
With her little baby child.
All dressed like a peasant old and thin
Our Billjim helps the war to win.
In the dark of night he creeps into the Hunnish
lines, _ i
Where Huns whisper dreadful words and drink more
dreadful wines.
He finds all their pretty maps,
And thinks out some lovely traps
To catch Hansfritz
And blow him to bits !

THERE is no disappoint-
ment when you use
O-Cedar Polish as directed.
After all, it is the sim-
plest and easiest way.
Just remember this:
Use' O-Cedar Polish with
water. Simply dampen
a cloth with water, wring
it almost dry and add as
much O-Cedar Polish as
the cloth contains water.
Go over the article to be
polished, watch how the
dirt, dust and scum disappear,
notice how the beauty of the grain
appears, see how ‘lively’, bright
and fresh the varnish looks. Then
a few rubs with a dry cloth pro-
duces the desired lustre.
Apply O-Cedar Polish to your
dusting cloth (as you use it daily)
to retain the lustre.
As you see the results O-Cedar Polish
brings about you will realize that O-Cedar
is more than a polish, because it Cleans
as it Polishes.

BANANA LAND
There is no cohesion in political parties
these times. The Queensland Independent
Party, officered and manned by genial Billy
Murphy, doubled its numbers the other day
when .John Adamson joined it. But within
a week the party split over the Chillagoe
business, the deputy-leader voting one way
and the leader the other. It is rumored
that a heresy-hunt is now being vigorously
organised.
John Iluxham’s Police Offences Bill*is a
fearsome-looking thing. It aims at prevent-
ing all betting except on a racecourse, and
• declares even settling in clubs to be illegal.
Card-playing whether for money or matches
is pronounced out of bounds in any public
place, including trains and clubs. The sin
of limiting one’s family is to be visited by
dire penalties; and any idiot may declare
one’s house to be disreputable, the onus of
proof that it isn’t being then on the person
charged. Of course most of the bill will go
out. Cynics say that is w r hy it was brought
in.
“Boro-Belle”: A lady weiglibridge-clerk
has been appointed at the Pleystowe sugar
mill, Maekay. The fair juggler of weights,
while enjoying the distinction of being the
only female cane-weigher in Bananaland, is
not the first to hold such a position, as 12
years ago the mill-owner’s daughter at
Palmyra (now closed) acted in the same
capacity. On one occasion a waggon-load of
cane arrived on the weighbridge while the
clerk was playing a mandolin, and the excise
officer was so charmed with the music that
he would not disturb her. So the cane
waited.
“Gourami”: The late Daniel O’Connor,
of Oxley (near Brisbane), was a born natu-
ral observer of the type of Fabre and
Luther Burbank, but without the latter’s
advertising abilities. lie developed his
hobbies of horticulture and fish culture along
lines of real public usefulness. In his gar-
den at Oxley he grew ami improved all
manner of tropical and subtropical fruits
and vegetables. His scorn fell on the horti-
culturist who is satisfied with the ordinary
gardening successes, and he would maintain
stoutly that two acres of good soil, properly
handled, would support a family. His own
results went to bear out hi ■ contention. The
domestic cultivation of edible fish was per-
haps his favorite hobby, and he spent much
time and money in attempts to acclimatise
useful species. The gourami, the domesti-
cated edible fish of Java, attracted his atten-
tion particularly, and he was never tired
of maintaining that a pond of gourami
should, and one day would, be as ordinary
a feature of the suburban backyard as a
fowlhouse is at present, lie went to Java
and brought back the fish, but attempts to
stock waters in the neighborhood of Bris-
bane failed. For this he blamed eels. He
also collected and took to London and Paris
the first- live specimens of the lungfish
(Ceratodu's forsteri), and refused -a liberal
cheque for his trouble.
“S.G. J.” : The Brotherhood of Man idea
doesn’t get any bumps in our Central
Bananaland town. The local lawn-tennis
club had a meeting the other night. There
were 16 members present, most of them
white Australia; but China was voted to the
chair. You savee?
The last few years of Jack Hamilton’s life
showed up the old ex-athlete, ex-politician,
ex-many things, in a characteristic light. He
had offered his services to the Defence Depart-
ment, and was employed-at Enoggera giving
instruction in the art of handling rifles and
revolvers. Although his sight was naturally
dimmed with years he made astonishingly
good shooting at times, and quite surprised
the folk who looked upon him as a hopeless
back-number. Hamilton’s political career
was exceptionally long, and he was reputed
at one time to be the smartest Parliamentary
whip Bananaland ever knew. By his death
the Johnsonian Club loses one of its
founders.
“450 Cal.”: To the late Jack Hamilton,
sitting on the balcony of a Maryborough
hotel, there came the Major Knockpin of
Steele Rudd’s story, with a drawing of a
new kind of patent rifle target for discussion
and expert advice. Hamilton looked gravely
at his visitor, and announced in his mild,
high-pitched drawl: “Bill, I’ve myself dis-
covered a perfectly reliable automatic self-
registering target.” “Good Lord !” said the
Major; “you’ve made your fortune then?”
“Afraid not,” drawled Hamilton. “You see,
Bill, it isn’t quite practicable for range-

YOUNG HAMEAT (with forced gaiety): “Ha! the dinner-hell! Let us eat, drink and be
merry, for to-morrow our board is due."
OLD BARNSTORMER ( agitated ): “Hush, reckless boy! Jest not with sacred things!”
firing, and it wants a lot of attention. But,”
and the drawl grew longer, “would you like
to see it?” “My colonial, I would,” said
Bill. A stout citizen sauntered by on the
opposite side of the street, and with that
lightning quickness which characterised his
shooting, Hamilton whipped an air pistol
from under the newspaper on his knee, fired
and slipped the pistol back again. The
citizen sprang about two feet into the air,
clapped his hand to the part struck, and
glared furiously round ,for the boy with the
shanghai. The only living beings in sight
were two respectable old men reading
on the verandah. “You see, Bill.” came a
soft drawl in the Major’s ear, “I told you
it wants attention. But, it’s quite automatic
and self-recording, isn't it, Bill?”
“Webley”: Jack Hamilton was the hero'"
of a hundred stories of the North. His
remarkable head and face, which might
have served Watts for a- model ot a new
rendering of a statue of Force, topped a
bodv which for strength and endurance had
few equals in its day. Born in Victoria
oven-70 years ago, lie came to Queensland
in the early digging days, after a chequered
youth, and saw the roaring time of Gympie
and the Palmer. Hamilton made an early
name for himself amongst the rough and
varied collection of humanity on the he ds
for two excellent reasons. One was that
lie was a really good doctor, although lie
had not completed a medical course, and
the other was that lie was about, the tough-
est proposition in Queensland with the
gloves, or without. If anything further were
needed by the inquirer, he was a crack re-
volver shot. His record with a Webley
match pistol topped the world’s _ shooting
lists for years, and the revolver with which
he made it is in the possession of an old
Cooktown resident. W r hen Hamilton was
over 60 years of age he fenced a public bout
with a sergeant of the Defence Force, who
fancied himself a good hand with tlm ioils.
Hamilton fenced with his eyes blindfolded,
and hit his adversary four times to one. The
trick is simple enough, as every fencer
knows, provided the blindfolded man has
extreme speed, an iron wrist, and a suffi-
ciently delicate sense of touch ; but few men
retain these qualities at 60-odd. He swam
the Brisbane River in the ’93 flood, for a
bet, amongst a whirl of grinding timber
which made the feat look like suicide. There
is a story that he fought Larry Foley in
the latter’s best days, for the sheer fun of
the thing, and that he did not get too much
of a beating either. In his day Jack
Hamilton was probably the best-known man
in the North, and there are few bush towns
where he will not be remembered whenever
fighting or revolver shooting comes up for
discussion.
When Bugles Called.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When bugles called they left their land
And turned them tow’rd a distant strand.
Their hearts were set in noble wise,
Their souls were shining in their eyes
What time they caught that message grand.
Oh ! well they heard the clear command
“Come dne—Come all —a patriot band,
This way the path of duty lies,!”
When bugles called.
Not kindest kiss nor beck’ning bland.
Nor fondest touch of gentle hand,
Nor softest tears nor tend’rest sighs
Could turn them from the high emprise
That hid them on where guns are manned
I* When bugles called.
QUEEN £ AND.
F. B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP
Brisbane, December 16, 1916.
My Dear Thargomindah, —
This is for your private ear. The .other
day his Ex. was late for an engagement, anu
he' looked as though he was sure nobocD
would ever forgive him. The reason o
the delay was that his “tank” while careex-
ing along knocked over a horse and can.
Did vice-royalty flee lest his name shouiu
be taken by the Law? No, Thargomindah.
His Ex. hopped out of his chariot and depo
sited himself upon the horizontal horse s
head while the animal’s chains and appui-
tenances were torn otf by the anguished
keeper. Then our Popular and Genial pro-
ceeded on his way with dusty hat and a rent
—but, no matter.
To-day I saw him again—believe me,
now quite tranquil. He was taking the
salute from 2000 soldiers who were illumi-
nating Repatriation Day.
By the way, the boys in camp are in.
for a good time at Christmas if the Girls
Club receives the support it deserves. lhe
scheme is to provide a memorable blow-out
on the 25th. Don’t be backward in putting
your packet in if you’re handy.
Alfred Wall, for 28 years headmaster of
the Milton- State School, and for 50 years
in the service of the Education Department,
has retired. On Wednesday his staff and
pupils said good-bye to him—only officially,
of course; —and loaded him with remem-
brances.
All Hallows’ .Convent School gave its
annual display of needlework on Monday
afternoon, and on Wednesday evening Lady
Goold-Adams handed down the prizes. Then
'she assisted the Governor to enjoy the break-
up concert. Nellie Dowd’s singing and Enid
Parker’s violin playing were the features
of the programme. Arthur Austin . (“Sir-
dar”) hung out a seraphic smile while his
little daughter declaimed some Shakesbacon.
who, as you know, my dear, was a friend
of Oscar Asche.
Douglas Eraser, second son of ex-Senator
Simon, is paying his dad a flying visit in
Melbourne this w y eek to say au re'voir, as he
sails by the Niagara for England via
Canada. On arrival he will stand in to do
his bit along with the regiment into which
little brother Neville went straight from
Oxford in 1914. Douglas has made his
home in Queensland, where he has a big
share in the John Collins and Son station,
Mundoolun. He married the youngest
daughter of the late R. M. Collins —his
cousin, in fact —and she and the son and
heir will go as far as England to see him
“first put this uniform on.”
Owing to the efforts of bird-lovers like
“Beachcomber” Banfield, E. S. Emerson, M.
Forrest, Peter Airey and others who have
been writing about the State’s wonderful
bird-life in the school papers, the Northern
youngster has become a very enthusiastic
Gould Leagueite. Half the kiddies of
Bananaland have sworn off bird-nesting and
catapults as a result of the campaign.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Maryborough Grammar School threw up
its hat last week when G. A. Dunbar was
awarded a Rhodes Scholarship for 1916. He
is the second winner from that school,
11. Harvey being the first, eight years ago.
Dunbar, a big, manly chap, has a fine list
of successes. He left the Queensland Uni-
versity last year, qualified for his commis-
sion at Duntroon, and was last heard of at
Salisbury Plains.
Just-dead Douglas Price, M.A., was, a
few years ago, an Anglican parson, who used
to paralyse his high-toned parishioners by
breadth of view and faculty for assimilating
orthodox Christianity and evolutionary phi-
losophy without the least inconvenience.
When Anglicanism could no longer tolerate
his dilutions of dogma lie founded a kind
of church wherein he lectured on nearly
every subject under the sun. Gentle, ascetic
and cultivated, he was perhaps the best-read
man in Brisbane.

| “Ortheris”: Enter for your mean-man
competition the thief who stole from a
returned transport at Port Melbourne the
trick terrier Anzac, a real dog-of-war who
had seen service in Gallipoli, Egypt, France
and Belgium, and was the stay and com-
fort of Private G. D. Beer, now at Caulfield
Base Hospital. Though Anzac was allowed
the freedom-of London, where he gained the
special notice of Queen Alexandra, the
Customs authorities refused him a landing
when he came back to Australia, aud as his
master could not remain on the transport to
look after him, the thief had his opportunity.
The dog left Australia as a pup three weeks
old with the First Artillery Brigade, and
was fed on the bottle. He quickly became
an old_ soldier, and would lie in bed and
have his pulse and temperature taken like a
human patient. Ragtime dancing, standing
on liis head, and coming to “ ’shun !” with a
pipe in his mouth were among his minor
accomplishments.
A really original and picturesque will has
been upset by the Supreme Court of South
Aus. An eccentric spinster left £5OOO to
“develop kauri and other valuable forests
of hundreds of varieties of Bambusa, and fill
them with local and foreign song and useful
bird-life, in the inland districts of Australia.”
The other legatees objected that it couldn’t
be done; and Capt. S. A. White. Adelaide’s
chief authority on bird-life, supplied a strong
affidavit that the trees wouldn’t grow, the
birds couldn’t live in them if they did, there
is no society to carry out the work, and
anyway the £5OOO is not enough, meaning
only about £2OO a year. The Chief Justice
turned the idea down as “fantastic and im-
practicable.” There was still a question
whether the money should be devoted to the
nearest “charity” that is practical—in this
case, afforestation. But the Court decided
that that was not the intention; so the
family gets what the lawyers don’t.
No wonder the enemy calls us “silly
Pritishers.” Here is an excerpt from a
letter from a Northern sugar farm. It was
written by a girl to her soldier-brother who
is now in camp at five bob a day:—
We had a gang of six Germans and gave them £1
a day, but they were all the time grumbling. When
they had cut about a quarter of the cane they pulled
out. They said it was too far from the barracks,
which are only a quarter of a mile off. However, we
secured a gang of Spaniards, and they insisted on
occupying our house, so we all had to go into town
to live till the cane is cut.
Meantime State Governments are still bor-
rowing and spending millions “in order to
give our own people employment.”
Government experiments in feeding cattle
on prickly-pear at Wallumbilla seem to
solve some of the drought problem. On
chopped pear, with the addition of concen-
trated food costing 2d. per head a day, stock
thrived for 150 days without water. Fur-
ther, the animals put on flesh, and settled
for all time the fallacy that pear turns the
fat yellow.
“Rangan”: Early next year ' Banana-
land’s new cement co. will start to provide
some of the 40,000 tons of cement the State
annually imports along with its toys,
clotlies-pegs, etc. The co. began badly, as
some of its £200.000 or so had to go
towards “transferring the contract to Eng-
land” when the war broke out —where from
it is needless to say. Once on its legs, how-
ever, the co. should do well. Its plant is
only a few miles from the limestone quarry,
right up against the Darra (Ipswich) shale
beds, and 10 miles from the coalfields; and
though Australia doesn’t know enough about
cement-making plants to be able to swear
that this one will be a success, the foreign
experts swear that it is all right. Some of
them, in fact, encourage us to expect the
time when we shall get cement at a dollar
a barrel, same as Uncle Sam.
A new fish lately presented to the Brisbane
Museum has been christened Something -or*
other Theodorei, in compliment to the
Treasurer. The marine curio is described as
an “elegant species with beautiful colora-
tions”—just like a spendthrift Labor Budget
before" the Auditor-General's comments dull
its brilliancy.
Gunn, who represents a .border electorate
in the Bananaland Assembly, is the latest
Parliamentary convert to Unification. The
anti-Federal attitude of the Ryan Govern-
ment in stopping live-stock traffic south-
wards was the deciding factor.
Brisbane Grammar School has certainly
done its little bit in the Big Scrap. To date
it has sent 680 old boys to the Front. The
original 9th Battalion had eight of them as
officers and there were scores in the ranks;
and the 15th w*as commanded by •an old
Brisbane Grammar boy, with another as
second in command and about eight more
with commissions. The first two Light
Horse regiments from Queensland also had
old B.G.S. boys in the most important com-
mands. The roll includes such names as
Brig.-General Foott (son of Mrs. Hannay
Foott), Brig.-General Sellheim, Lt.-Col.
Hubert Harris, and Lt.-Col. G. H. Bourne,
hero of the Romani battle. So far no V.C.
has come the way of the school, but there
are several D.S.O.’s and a number of Mili-
tary Crosses and Military Medals. •
“J.D.C.” : A peculiar thing about telegraph
operating is that, after years of key work,
many men become what we call “broken
down.” The Americans call it getting “glass
arni.” By that or any other name it is just as
rotten. The afflicted telegraphist finds diffi-
culty in making some letters on the key, and
as time goes on the number of difficult let-
ters increases. It is pitiful to watch the
contortions of a broken-down man trying to
send. It is more pitiful to have to receive
from him at the other end. Medical autho-
rities don’t seem to be able to exactly define
the complaint, but undoubtedly nerve strain
is a factor. Some years ago an American
invented a machine which mechanically
makes the dots, the dashes being sandwiched
in by the telegraphist. Variations of this '
idea are embodied in several semi-mechanical
senders now in use throughout Australia,

BRIEF HUPMOBILE SPECIFICATIONS.
Hupmobile Models: 5-seater, 7-seater, 2-seater, sedan, and 5-seater and 2-seater with detachable winter tops. Motor: Four-cylinder, 95 m/m bore, 140 m/m stroke (32in. x s£in.).
Transmission- Three forward speeds and reverse; multiple disc clutch. Bear Axle floating type, spiral bevel gear. Cam shaft and crank shaft bearings bronze shell, babbitt
lined. Fong wheel base (119 in. on 2- and 5-seater, 134 in. on 7-seater). Tyres 880 x 120 m/m or 34in. x 4in. on 2- and 5-seater, 920 x 120 m/m or 35in. x 4iin. on 7-seater. Electric
starting and lighting; ventilating, rain vision screen; one-man hood; quick-acting side curtains; door curtain carriers; deep upholstery; speedometer; robe rail, foot rail and
carpet in tonneau; non-skid tyres on rear; five demountable rims; tyre carrier, pump, jack and full set of tools. Magneto ignition, wire wheels, special colors, khaki hood and
seat covers at small additional cost over list price of car.

The Inky Way.
C. Melton, of the Brisbane Courier, has
had 57 years’ service on that paper. The
old gentleman—though in nothing but in
mere years is he old —often walks from Bris-
bane to Sandgate and back (25 miles) for a
bit of exercise. His memories of early Bris-
bane roam away to the time when Woolloon-
gabba was wattle-scented, when Moreton Bay
sent representatives to the N. S. Wales Par-
liament, and when one could wade across the
Brisbane River at points where ocean liners
now float.
* * * *
Prom S. M. Herald’s report of the Angli-
can Synod (6/12/’l6) :
The toast was honored, and the entire assemblage
sang “Praise God, from Whom all blessings flow.”
Mr. T. A. Dibbs responded.
We hate to seem pernickety: but, really,
isn’t Mr. Dibbs taking too much upon him-
self?
. A * * *
'Every year Australia To-day improves
as a national advt. The issue for 1917 is
the twelfth and the most alluring. The contri-
butions range from the figures of the various
statisticians to the tales of Sundry. Even the
G.-G. has been given a chance to do some-
thing for the country that keeps his billy
boiling. “Forestry in Australia” —forestry
being one of his specialties—is the text of
his sermon. The cover is Norman Lindsay’s
and the rest of the publication is too numer-
ous to mention. The spreading Commercial
Travellers are the publishers.
Now in the artillery, camp at The Warren,
Marrickville (N.S.W.), R. R. Kidston, “war
barrister” — -i.e., one of those who have
taken an early exam, so that they
may get to the Front. He was well
known to Sydney undergrads as “Horace
de Vere” and “Maurice Typert”; under
those pen-names he contributed to Hermes
and the “books” of the last four Uni.
pageants. Chris Brennan signalled him
as a coming poet; and Adrian Consett
Stephen found him a loyal second in his
(Stephen’s) dramatic ventures. After
graduation Kidston held a captain’s com-
mission on the Censor’s staff, but finally
decided that that was no place for a fit man
when there were returned soldiers available.
* * * *
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”:—
A building of much interest to the Inky Way is
being demolished. It’s a few yards north of The
Bulletin office. The building was of the
Macquarie era and on the site of a virgin grant to
George Johnston, an officer of marines who later on
became Colonel of the N.S.W. Corps and deposer
of Captain Bligh. Here were buildings known as
Redman’s Court, Mr. Redman being an old-time
gaoler; and therein was the office of what is now
the S. M. Herald, first published in 1831 “in
Redman’s Court, by Stephens, Stokes and McGarvie.”
* * * *
Few front-rank penmen have wandered as
much as Fred W. Ward. It was William
Curnow, also an ex-Wesleyan parson, who,
when editor of the S. M. Herald, inveigled
him on to the press. After helping Curnow
on the Herald he brought out the Echo
(evening) and then the Mail for the Fair-
faxes. After that he edited the Sydney
Telegraph (1885-90), and then moved on
to London, where he wrote leaders for the
Times and wasn’t happy. Soon back in
Australia he edited the Brisbane Courier
and was associate on the Argus. Henry
Gullett had got the right to nominate his
successor when he retired from the Sydney
Telegraph, and he didn’t think twice when
the time came. The anchor looked as though
it .was down for good when the kindly old
man with the long whiskers left the Tele-
graph to go into retirement. But, unlike
his old friend Gullett, he can’t retire; so he
goes back to Brisbane to help his other old
friend Brentnall with the Telegraph —the
first evening paper he has handled since the
days of the dead-and-almost-forgotten
Echo.
* * * *
Like F. W. Ward, who goes to Brisbane
to edit the Telegraph, Brentnall, the prin-
cipal owner, is a retired Methodist minister.
Fifty-odd years ago he was one of four
young parsons who landed in Sydney. . After
20 years he changed his collar, joined the
staff of the Telegraph, and became a share-
holder and chairman of directors. While
stationed at Wagga he was the parson who
married Tom Castro, the Tichborne claimant.
* * * *
Rather than lose sixpence the Argus the
other day published the following:—
Vacancy for two gentleman boarders, Germans
preferred, all home comforts, near city. ,
Argus.
If the advt. had expressed a preference for
unionists or mere Australians a few of the
Argus’s eyes would probably have seen some-
thing offensive in it.
* * * *
“H. 0.”: This particular snub dditor was
suffering from a morbid desire to tell the
age of everybody. He thought that the
public had a yearning to know that the
drunk and disorderly, who was fined 15 bob,
was 41, and the bloke who pinched a whip
from a cab driver, 39. This craving foT ages
made the evening-paper minion who was
doing the police courts feel tired. He gave
the sub. a hint of his weariness on being
dragged from the court in the middle of a
case to answer the inquiry, per ’phone:
“What was the age of the man who was
committed for stealing a pair of boots?”
“Really., I don’t know,” replied the faithful
tripehound apologetically. “I didn’t get a
chance to examine his teeth !”
PEN AND SWORD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two years have passed since war began,
And every day the prints 1 scan.
With joy I see
That Germany
Is in Hell’s hottest bin.
I know the wires are gospel facts
For even in religious tracts,
Yea, in the Age,
Our vict’ries rage,
And yet the blighters won’t give in.
Great Heavens, perhaps—’sh, ’sh, speak 10w....
They’re bound to get the Argus, though.
N. S. W.
