Lee—Fore—Brace.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TIERE’S a call will surely haunt me
till I reach the final goal,
A call that brings my boyhood back
to me —
A rotten sort of curse that clings like
burrs thick on my soul,
A curse enhanced by thirsty years of sea; _
It tells me of my first trip, and a big
Norwegian mate,
With lungs the Bashan bull could not dis-
grace,
Keeps yelling out one order like a herald
sent by Fate:
“Come, shift yehselves, yeh blankers! Lee —
fore —brace!”
Lee — foweri-—brace!
Each man goes to his place
Cursing every mother’s son that owns the
blank Norwegian race.
Then: “Heave and bust the backstays—
heave and.... that’ll do, make fast !”'
And you’ll get the same damned order thrice
before the watch is past !
When our eyes are blind with Cape Horn
spray and “greybeards” sweep the decks,
And all the gear is coated thick with ice, _
Cockney Bill will wax facetious —we’re *in
water to our necks —
Saying: “Why go dahn ter Mawgit—ain’t
this nice?”
But we’ve little time for joking, for the old
man’s cracking on, x
And the sheer-poles dip right under on our
lee,
And we know the fo’c’s’le’s flooded —not a
dry stitch left to don —■
Whilst we’re thinking what we’re going to
have for tea.
Lee — fower. —brace !
Oh ! lie’s everything that’s base
Is that mate. .. .“Look out! Hold fast!” a
curler misses by an ace !
“That damned old howlin’ square-head’s
’azin’ us fer flamin’ spite,”
Growls Yankee Jack—' “l’ll fix ’im if we get
ter port all right!”
When we’re making spunyarn sennit —“just
to keep the watch employed”—
And we’re thinking that we all could keep
one down,
And Cockney Bill is telling of a spree that
he enjoyed
With lurid details culled from Canning
Town.
And German Frank is telling us “dot Sit-
ney vos der blace
Vere lofely leedle frauleins, yoost so high,
Vos vaitin’, for der askin’, dot vos so, ja” !
Lee — fore —brace !
We drop our balls of spunyarn and stand by.
Lee —fore—brace !
No more talk of skirts and lace.
“Move yehself, yeh big fat Deutscher, you’re
a filthy old disgrace!”
And the old man at the fife-rail he applauds
the second mate:
“Well, the mate has got you trained well,
Mr. Brown, at any rate!”
When we get up wet and hungry in the
morning from our bunks,
Though all we feel inclined to do is cuss,
We go foraging for scouses, sailors-joys and
dandy-funks
While the cook—a coal-black despot—glares
at us;
Then he opens wide his duff-trap and he
yells at everyone
Till his lips obscure the coastline of his
face:
“If yo’ cain’t come round no soonah, Ah
ain’t gwine ter give yo’ none” !
But his voice is drowned with: “For’ard!
Lee — fore —brace !”
Lee —fore-—brace !
See us playing chevy-chase
With our sea-boots squelching water and
our “maps” one huge grimace!
For we’re scowling hot and dirty ’cause
we’ve lost our bit o’ food ;
But it’s no use getting shirty When we
ain’t on earth for good !
When we’ve dropped the pick in Sydney
after four months on the sea
And we feel at last that we’re upon the
earth,
We’ve got to rig the stages overside and
mournfully
Chip away at iron-rust for all we’re worth.
Hear the owner’s missus chirrup: “Oh, I
think a ship is grand!
A sailing ship—it seems so full of grace !”
I’m thinking as I’m sitting, chipping, ham-
mer in my hand,
Of those words—and of their echo: “Lee —
fore—brace !
Lee — fore — brace!
P’raps the good God has a place
Someivhere up among His angels where a
“shell” can go the pace!
Where there won’t be any “pound and
pint” or rotten Board of Trade,
And we’ll sign on in a gospel ship that won’t
stint marmalade!
N.s.w.
ALBATROSS.
The Dust of March.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
nyr O one, upon any occasion, ever smiled
l\ half so earnestly as Tippy Windle;
and here, to-day, he was smiling his
damnedest.
The grave Georgiana turned her exquisite
nose towards him and stared curiously. She
noted that he was younger (yet older) than
she was, that the brown curls were inlaid
with silvery fibrils, that the cheeks had lines
which stood as much for vivacity as for
numbers of years, that the eyes burnt
strongly under the lively wiry brows.
A curtain, with elusive depths of green
in it, gave him the Icarian appearance of a
posed figure standing against the dignified
background of an eighteenth century paint-
ing.
There were a number of good water-colors
on every side; a horde of odd-jacketed books
shoved pell-mellishly into shelves along the
wall, and all begging with one voice for
immediate reading; a bronze miniature fist
shook its menace from a pedestalled wave.
And (supernal enchantment) there was a
carpet that was beautiful.
Georgiana, as still, in proportion, as she
had chosen to be grave, felt, and saw neg-
lected, the warm flutter of her June wood-
fire in all the corners of the room.
There was a spectacular geranium heavily
asleep on the inside windowsill, and the
air was so full of its sensual radiance that
Georgiana focussed it as plainly through the
back of her head as though she were facing
it.
"‘You have always loved me less in
March,” he said, helping her to his mind,
metaphorically, with a saltspoon.
“Why do you talk of March,” she replied,
“when this is already June?”
“Yes.... but there is always the dust of
March! I have adored you for five years,
and in every one of those five, from the
month of March until God knows what time
in July, you have been a difficult creature.”
Georgiana carried her toe over the fender
and assumed the attitude of a hopelessly
puzzled, slightly bored, slightly amused,
wholly beautiful young woman.
Just at that moment one of the swing-
windows flew open with a bang, and she and
Tippy rushed together to shut it.
“Was there ever such a day?”
He leant forward to kiss her, but she
withheld him by an invisible arm-thrust,
and, picking up her book safely, retreated
to the uttermost corner of a sofa. Tippy
occupied the thither end of the same piece
of furniture, from whence he began telling
her he had “had enough of it.”
“You don’t love me the way you used to
do. And wouldn’t it be better for you to
say so, than to have me continually forcing
you to speak untruths which are equally
bad for both of us?
“Look here,” he clamored. “I’m for a
straight-out issue. Do you love me, or do
you not? By God, I know what it is that
you’re after! You would like to evade the
dishonor of being the first to signal ‘enough.’
I can hear your heart already clapping
hands when I tell you that it is finished .
Over for all time!”
Georgiana’s lips appeared numb for a
moment; then they curved themselves up to
represent the smile of a woman who is dead.
She made a movement of her hands, from
her waist outwards, as though conveying a
necklace into the air, and dropping it. Her
teeth sparkled through the dewy dimness of
her mouth; and then, cunningly bending
her beautiful neck, she rested her chin on
her fingers and remained so, for a few
minutes, looking rapturously happy.
At length she straightened up a little and
said, almost with affection: “Tippy, dear,”
and then again, “Tippy, dearest, tell me who
she is?”
Tippy kicked at her cocksparrow toe
perched upon the fender, and the silence re-
mained unbroken while she delicately acted
an assumption of pain.
At last he said : “You’ve heard me talk of
her often.”
“The typist who owns the four Clausens?”
“She has the blackest hair I have ever
seen.”
“I’m glad she hasn’t got red hair. Do
you remember Mrs. Doctor Welton? Red-
haired women are seldom beauties; they
almost invariably have sallow complexions.”
“I saw the Clausens; they are quite won-
derful. Two of them are done on book-size
slate. Nobody else can paint Autumn as
Clausen does. His Summers, too, are posi-
tively thrilling. I could imagine his picture
called ‘The Silent Hour’ withering all the
flowers on your mantelpiece.”
“And has she got ‘The Silent Hour’?”
“Oh, no. Only some pastels he gave her
just before she said good-bye to him in
London.”
Georgiana remained meditatively caress-
ing her lips, with an inchlet of red stuff
taken by sleight-of-hand from a bag in her
lap.
“Now I lcnoic you have been to her
house,” she asserted. “And you never told
me !”
“Only through lack of opportunity,” he
answered. “I went home with her on Mon-
day night. We caught the last tram to-
gether. She had a pile of her husband’s
books, mostly on technical subjects; she
said he had left them in his office when he
went to New Guinea six weeks ago, and they
had lain there ever since. Of course, I tried
to take them from her, and they flopped all
over the floor of the carriage, and the butter
I was carrying went with them; then, after
I had done the picking up, I found she had
paid my fare, and wouldn’t let me return
the money.”
“Come, come! You'd better make a clean
breast of the whole thing,” said Georgiana,
and releasing an ear-ring into the palm of
her very lively little hand sparkled it
between her fingers over the flame in the
fireplace.
“Did you kiss her?”
“Not in the tram. .. .not even in the house
afterwards.”
“Then you ‘did go into the house with
her?.. . .At what an hour!”
“Midnight!. ... But it was all owing to an
accident. She lives in a quite darkish part
of the neighborhood; great street-trees on
either side of the road with only one lamp
too far away to throw any light for protec-
tion. When we got to her gate the garden
was all black inside the pickets of the fence,
and I thought it only right that I should go
up the drive with her, not merely to carry
the books, which were a heavy load, but
also to see her safely to the door.”
“I see it all, I see it all,” said Georgiana,
and dropped the ear-ring into the beautiful
carpet. “Is there a man in this world
capable of an honest and utterly single-
hearted affection?”
“I stepped through the gateway into a
sunken bricked-in entrance with three steps
to rise to the garden, and as I did so I stuck
my foot, almost up to the knee, into a full
bucket of water. You may be sure I hopped
out again more quickly than I had gone into
it, and, afterwards, leant against a pine-tree
while I wrung the dampness out of the edges
of my trousers. Of course, Gadey pretended
to be angry.. . .and sorry, both, at the same
time; but she laughed., for all that, as loudly
as I did, and the end-up of it was I had to
let myself be persuaded to go into the house
to dry in front of the fire.
“The door opened to Gadey’s key, dis-
creetly, and without any more noise than the
(Continued on next page.)

behind, no part of the system is free from danger; the back
aches, the limbs drag, the eyelids seem like lead; there are
dropsical swellings, rheumatic pains, dizzy spells, urinary
troubles.

drawing aside of a curtain; then, making
across a shadowy passage, we found our-
selves in what I at mce instinctively felt to
be quite a companionable darkness. This
lasted for just three perfectly-delicious
seconds, and not any longer.
“A soft glow of light filled the room in a
moment, and it was difficult to tell how or
whence it had come into existence. But
there were the four Cl-ausens, an Amand
Jean and a strapping Sargent that looked
almost as though it had been laid in with
whip strokes, a transparent lump of jade,
sago-green, and in the shape of a Chinese
hippogriff, a picture of Norman Lindsay
(drawn by himself) offering his prayers to
the Devil, a crooked sampler framed right
up to the margin, a bowlful of pipes.. . .and
a real live cat in an armchair.
“ ‘Delphine,’ said Gadey to the cat, ‘you
ought to be ashamed of yourself! Why
aren’t you with Dora?’
“ ‘Dora,’ she explained, ‘is my sister. She
has been ill the whole of her life. And
generally, when I come home in the even-
ings, I find Dora sound asleep in her bed,
with Delphine clasped tight to . her breast.’
Then she took the cat by the soft, elastic
back of the neck and kissed its paws for
punishment.
“ ‘There’s an English translation of
Rabelais might shock you,’ she continued,
nodding her head at a thick red volume on
a shelf. ‘You will find six original and
entirely wicked drawings in it by August
Helmholtz. My husband says they are
valuable.’
“By this time I had laid my load of tech-
nical literature on a small side table, and
was looking ruefullv at my own separate
parcel of squashed butter; thence my gaze
travelled to the wet ends of my unfortunate
trousers.
“‘A quarter past twelve,’ rattled on
Gadey. ‘And here am I asking you to take
an interest in pictures and books when all
your thoughts must be turned towards fresh
socks and —toast.’ (She had mentioned
toast for supper.)
“ ‘Because 1 have got fresh socks,’ she
continued. ‘I finished some a week ago, and
as the heels all went wrong I was too
ashamed to send them to the soldiers. Stay
here and I’ll bring a pair to you.’
“So I remained where I was, and, build-
ing a fire out of some splintered pine-sticks
that lay handy to my reach, stretched back
into the legs of a chair and all but fell off
to sleep.
“The next thing I can remember is Gadey
feeding the cat.
“ ‘Delphine ! Delphine !’ said Gadey ; and
Delphine gave soft, ghostly barks in imita-
tion of a dog. For a reward she received
scraps of tawny red toast saturated with
butter.
“And then, all at once, from some far-
away part of the house, I heard a clock
strike one, and I turned over on my side to
rise up and put an end to my visit.
“Gadey and the cat were both looking at
me. The shadow of Delpliine’s nose and
whiskers danced across the firelit cheeks
of her fascinating mistress.
“Delphine and Gadey were very close to
me. . . .1 inclined three aerial inches nearer.”
“And you kissed Gadey!” interrupted
Georgiana. She was full of well-bred ex-
citement ; her eyes shone gallantly across
the sofa-space between herself and Tippy
Windle until he had no life left that didn’t
hurricane with love for her.
“I kissed. . . .Delphine !”
“Tippy,” said Georgiana. “The dust of
March has blown away. Hold me in your
arms, and don’t let me go until I ask you.”
Tippy had her tight to his breast.
“Mine for ever,” he exclaimed kissing the
top of her chin; and then (to himself) in a
voice nobody on earth could follow, ‘Until
March, 1917!’ ”
After a little while Georgiana permitted
herself to look up into his eyes.
“You don’t love Gadey? Tell me,
Tippy, you don’t love her.”
“I don’t love Gadey.”
“You never ever will?”
“I never ever will. I never ever can.”
“What do you mean by never ever can?”
“She doesn’t exist. Gadey was my....
my. . . .invention.”
“Your invention! And Delphine, too?”
“Delphine, too.”
And then Tippy essayed to kiss Georgiana
for the millionth time; but she hooked her-
self back against the tall, trembling curtain-
fall.
Nevertheless he actuallv accomplished his
purpose, and, in telline- Gadey of it next
day. he said : “For reciprocation, it was like
kissing the beautifullest dead sheep in the
world.”
Gadey flew into a terrible rage.
“I’ll have nothing more to do with you,”
she declared. “Go back to your Georgiana.”
“But I can’t go to Georgiana,” he replied
in a terror. “She doesn’t exist. She’s my
own most sacred invention.”
“You said she had hair the color of barley-
sugar,” snapped Gadey. “And what is the
color of THIS?”
She carefully uncurled a hair off his coat
on the end of her finger.
“Barley-sugar, by thunder!”
And so Tippy lost them both. Gadey.
because she had a very real rival; and
Georgiana because he had told her that
Gadey didn’t exist.
HUGH McCRAE.
“Ham and Eggs.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
»
“T"V OES tlieatricalism,” I asked, “become
j W second nature with some actors?”
We were sitting in the smoking-
room, when a well-known actor walked in
and greeted Brown in an absurdly stagey
manner.
Brown laughed.
“Well, what’s the cause,” I went on, “of
this histrionic —er —phenomenon, this —er —-
psychol”
“Did you ever hear the story of Ham and
Eggs?” lie asked.
When Jones bought the Pictorium he
didn’t expect the combination of amusement
tax, heavy duty on imported films and cut-
throat competition to put him on nodding
acquaintance with the bankruptcy court in
such a short time. Even converting the
theatre into a continuous picture-show didn’t
make the takings look interesting. A
novelty in the programme was needed. Then,
realising the sentimentality of Australian
audiences, he bethought him of Hamilton
and Eggleston, who were familiarly known
in the vaudeville world as Ham and Eggs.
They had appeared with success some years
previously, but being squeezed out by the in-
vasion of acrobats and ragtimers were com-
pelled to take the usual if-it-comes-in-you'll-
get-it engagements, touring the Backblocks.
“Find out where they are,” he directed his
manager, “an’ give ’em a contract; They
haven’t been near the city for years, an !
while the public’s got this patriotic wave
on we won’t run a risk by engaging local
talent
“What’s their turn like?” asked the
manager.
“Rotten from what I remember,” replied
Jones. “Song-an’-dance, made-up as bush-
men, an’ they imitate kookaburras, magpies
an’ sodawater-bottles.”
Ham and Eggs were at Coolctown, with a
troupe full of hope and no money, when the
telegram caught them, and they boarded the
first boat for Sydney. Jones widely adver-
tised the return of Australia’s old favorites,
fresh from foreign successes, and announced
their opening on the afternoon of the boat’s
arrival.
The steamer was late, and Ham and Eggs
stepped ashore to be greeted by an agitated
and perspiring Jones. Then followed a
hurried claiming of trunks, a reckless driv-
ing to the Pictorium, and a frenzied rush to
dress for the opening performance.
“Wish we could’ve seen a bit of old Syd-
ney,” said Ham, rubbing some vermilion on
his nose before a cracked mirror in the
badly-lighted dressing-room.
“Me, too,” replied Eggs. “We c’d put it
over some of them blokes that reckon we’re
has-beens.”
The stage-manager’s yell came down the
stairs. “Hey, Hamaneggs! That’s you.
Come on.”
Dressed as vaudeville bushmen, in red
shirts, white moleskin trousers, and pale
blue sashes in lieu of belts, they ambled on
to the stage set up as a bush scene, singing
their opening lines :
We love to wander ’neath the gum-trees,
Lis’nin’ to the magpie’s cry.
Then a few steps were danced.
With the smell o-of eucalyptus,
An’ the dear old mopoke’s sigh.
Another dance followed, and so on through
the song.
They were enjoying a long-promised smoke
in the dressing-room, and wondering, in the
light of the reception given them, why Syd-
ney hadn’t beckoned sooner, when the stage-
manager’s yell broke in on them again.
“Hamaneggs ! Come on ! Waitin’!”
“But we’ve just done our turn,” said Ham.
“Well, get it into your heads,” the stage-
manager grow-led, “this is a continuous show
—you an’ pictures from 11 a.m. till 11 p.m.
Don’t argue, the stage waits.”
Once more they sang their love of the lofty
gum and danced their appreciation of the
mopoke and the magpie.
About 7 o’clock Ham suddenly discovered
lie was hungry: but the intervals between
their performances, while the pictures were
being screened, wouldn’t allow them chang-
ing into street clothes and going out for
dinner, so they decided to send a boy em-
ployed on the stage, working “effects,” to a
restaurant. The boy gladly dropped a tin
dish containing some shot, which was
swirled around when Niagara Falls appeared
on the screen, and departed on the quest for
food.
In the midst of the meal came the insistent
call from the stage, and so through the even-
ing two weary performers mechanically sang
and danced of joys that were not. Then re-
membering they hadn’t arranged for accom-
modation for the night they sought the stage-
manager.
“Try the Harbor View Hotel,” he advised.
“Round in the back street, an’ handy.”
Refusing Jones’s invitation to supper, they
threw pyjamas and toothbrushes into a suit-
case, and dragged themselves to the hotel,
where they secured a room.
Jones and the manager, sitting back in
their chairs after supper, joined in the
general excitement caused by the clanging
of the fire-engines dashing down the street.
Tljey questioned the waiter.
“They tell me the Harbor View’s on fire,
sir!”
Simultaneously they thought of Ham and
Eggs, and rushing out of the cafe were swept
along by the crowd that were making for the
scene.
Turning the corner they could see the
fire had already eaten into the roof, and
huge flames, vomiting through the windows
of the sixtli and seventh floors, were licking
the walls.
Jones, pushing and jostling through the
swaying crowd of upturned faces, forced his
way to the door of the hotel. Entering the
vestibule, he was jambed against the wall
by the stream of terrified guests pouring
down the stairways and making for the
street. Fighting his way to the office, where
two men were feverishly gathering the books,
Jones shrieked an inquiry for Ham and
Eggs,
“Sixt’ floor. Room ninety, came the
answering yell.
Up the stairs Jones rushed, around count-
less landings, where the long, snaky lines of
hose wriggled under the pressure of water,
up more stairs, till at last he reached the
sixth floor. Half-choked by the clouds of
steam and smoke that rolled down the corri-
dor, he staggered from door to door until he
came opposite Room 90. Rasping for breath,
he tried the door. It was locked !
Yelling in a frenzy of desperation he
kicked and hammered on the door. “Hey,
Hamaneggs ! Hamaneggs ! You there?”
The door opened. Standing together, in
striped pyjamas, with tousled hair, and the-
effects of sleep still weigning down their lids,
Ham and Eggs looked vacantly at Jones.
Then in unison they ambled through the
doorway, singing their opening lines :
We love to wander ’neath the gum-trees,
Lis’nin’ to the magpie’s cry.

What is Talcum Powder?
TALGUM is an inorganic
substance —neither damp-
ness, heat or perspiration
affect it. Rice, starch, and
other vegetable powders are
organic —they are affected
by temperature and mois-
ture —perspiration sours
them —when sour they be-
come a source of irritation
and a breeding place for
infectious germs.
